Juvenile Idiopathic Arthritis on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 2-year-old female is brought to the pediatrician's office by her mother with the complaint of knee pain, stiffness, and a limp. History reveals that symptom onset was approximately two months ago. Her mother also notes that she has been having daily fevers for the past two weeks. Physical examination reveals an ill appearing child who walks with a limp and guards her right leg with attempts to move her right knee through its range of motion. The knee is warm on palpation with an apparent effusion, however it is not erythematous. There is a salmon-colored macular rash on her trunk that her mother states will come and go with the fever.  The most likely diagnosis is

1. Kawasaki disease
2. rheumatic fever
3. septic arthritis
4. systemic lupus erythematosus
5. systemic-onset juvenile idiopathic arthritis

The correct answer is: E
Systemic-onset juvenile idiopathic arthritis (SoJIA) is a subtype of what is also known as juvenile rheumatoid arthritis or adult still's disease, when it occurs in an adult.  It classically presents as arthritis with morning stiffness, high spiking fevers, and an evanescent salmon-colored macular rash usually located on the chest and abdomen, and occasionally on the extremities.  The rash typically coincides with the fevers.  In this particular subtype, patients often will typically appear systemically ill.  Other observed findings can include splenomegaly, pericardial effusion, anemia, and/or leukocytosis.  Diagnosis is based on history and physical examination.  Arthritis must be present for 6 weeks to make the diagnosis.  Most cases will resolve with NSAIDs or aspirin.  If there is no response, steroids may be used.

Answer A: Kawasaki disease typically presents as an acute febrile illness lasting 5 or more days with a polymorphic rash, injected eyes, and mucosal erythema with a strawberry tongue. Swelling and erythema of the hands and feet occur early followed by desquamation in the second week of the illness.


Answer B: Rheumatic fever is caused by an autoimmune process following infection with group A streptococci that typically presents with carditis, polyarthritis, chorea, erythema marginatum, and subcutaneous nodules. It is a clinical diagnosis based on the Jones criteria.


Answer C: Septic arthritis typically presents with single joint involvement, high fevers, severe pain, and/or erythematous joints. Diagnosis is based on history, physical examination, and on findings of arthrocentesis which will reveal bacteria, elevated white blood cells, and positive cultures.


Answer D: Systemic lupus erythematosus is an autoimmune disease resulting in diffuse inflammation that typically presents with polyarthritis, malar rashes, renal involvement, serositis, CNS involvement, and/or photosensitivity.




Bottom Line: Systemic-onset juvenile idiopathic arthritis presents as arthritis with daily, high spiking fevers, and an evanescent salmon-colored macular rash on the trunk.

For more information, see:

Juvenile Idiopathic Arthritis on Medscape

Link: "DeQuervain's tenosynovitis" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 25-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of right-sided wrist pain of two months duration. History reveals the pain is worsened by picking up her 6-month-old child and texting on her phone. She occasionally has difficulty gripping items with her right hand. Physical examination reveals swelling over her right thumb, and pain with passive motion. The pain radiates from her thumb to her forearm. 
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The physical exam maneuver which will confirm the most likely diagnosis is

1. Allen’s test
2. Finkelstein’s test
3. Phalen’s test
4. Tinel’s test
5. Watson’s test

The correct answer is: B
Finkelstein's test is performed with the patient’s thumb inside the fist while ulnar deviating the hand. If pain is elicited over the distal radius the test is positive and suggests DeQuervain’s tenosynovitis. This condition is an inflammation of the tendons of the first finger. These include the abductor pollicis longus and extensor pollicis brevis. DeQuervain's tenosynovitis is especially common in mothers of children 6-12 months old because of repeated use of the pollicis muscles when lifting the child. Treatment initially consists of splinting, activity modification, oral steroids or corticosteroid injections. Refractory cases many be treated with surgical release of the first compartment.

Answer A: Allen’s test is occlusion of the radial and ulnar arteries with pumping of the fist, then releasing one artery. A positive test is a delay or absence of blood returning to the palm suggestive of compromise in flow of the artery released. This test is to ensure patency of the radial and ulnar arteries before performing an arterial puncture to obtain an arterial blood gas.


Answer C: Phalen’s test is maximal flexion of both wrists for several minutes causing the reproduction of symptoms (numbness or tingling) suggesting carpal tunnel syndrome.


Answer D: Tinel’s test is performed by tapping the volar wrist; the test is positive when pain and numbness are elicited. This test also suggests the diagnosis of carpal tunnel syndrome.


Answer E: Watson’s test is performed by pushing the scaphoid anteroposterior with the wrist in radial or ulnar deviation. If the scaphoid subluxes or reduces, this suggests scapholunate ligament injury.




Bottom Line: A positive Finkelstein's test elicits pain in the distal radius when the patient ulnar deviates the hand with the thumb in a closed fist. It is suggestive of DeQuervain’s tenosynovitis.

For more information, see:
"DeQuervain's tenosynovitis" on Medscape

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 25-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of right-sided wrist pain of two months duration. History reveals the pain is worsened by picking up her 6-month-old child and texting on her phone. She occasionally has difficulty gripping items with her right hand. Physical examination reveals swelling over her right thumb, and pain with passive motion. The pain radiates from her thumb to her forearm. 
Question 2 of 2 in this set
This condition most likely involves

1. abductor pollicis brevis
2. abductor pollicis longus
3. extensor carpi radialis brevis
4. extensor carpi ulnaris
5. extensor pollicis longus

The correct answer is: B
The abductor pollicis longus and extensor pollicis brevis tendons are affected in DeQuervain’s tenosynovitis, which is tested for using the Finkelstein maneuver.

Answer A: There is no tendon named after the abductor pollicis brevis.


Answer C: The extensor carpi radialis brevis tendon is part of the second compartment and is not involved in DeQuervain's tenosynovitis.


Answer D: The extensor carpi ulnaris tendon is in the sixth compartment of the wrist and is responsible for snapping over the ulnar styloid, not DeQuervain's tenosynovitis.


Answer E: The extensor pollicis longus tendon makes up the third compartment of the wrist and courses around Lister’s tubercle.




Bottom Line: The abductor pollicis longus and extensor pollicis brevis are the two tendons inflamed in DeQuervain’s tenosynovitis.

COMBANK Insight : Basic anatomy questions like this one are very easily confused so make sure to review such topics as DeQuervain’s tenosynovitis, carpal tunnel syndrome, and Colles' fracture.

For more information, see:
"DeQuervain's tenosynovitis" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 16-year-old male presents with lateral knee pain. Structural examination reveals an anterior fibular head somatic dysfunction. Which of the following physical exam findings are consistent with this diagnosis?

1. Distal fibula is anterior and resists posterior springing
2. Proximal fibular head resists anterior springing
3. Talus externally rotated causing the foot to evert and dorsiflex
4. Talus externally rotated causing the foot to invert and plantarflex
5. Talus internally rotated causing the foot to invert and plantarflex

The correct answer is: C
The physical examination findings of an anterior fibular head somatic dysfunction include: a proximal fibular head that resists posterior spring, the distal fibula may be posterior, and the talus is externally rotated causing the foot to evert and dorsiflex. The fibular head glides anteriorly with pronation (dorsiflexion, eversion, and abduction) of the ankle, and posteriorly with supination (plantarflexion, inversion, and adduction) of the ankle. When the proximal portion of the fibula (fibular head) is "stuck" either in anterior or posterior the distal fibula will be "stuck" in the opposite motion (e.g. an anterior fibular head will have a posterior distal fibula). When the distal fibula is posterior the talus will prefer external rotation. This external rotation causes the foot to prefer eversion and dorsiflexion. Conversely, when the distal fibula is anterior the talus will prefer internal rotation causing the foot to prefer inversion and plantarflexion.

Answer A: The distal fibula is anterior and resists posterior springing in a posterior fibular head somatic dysfunction.


Answer B: The proximal fibular head resists anterior springing in a posterior fibular head somatic dysfunction.


Answer D: An externally rotated talus would cause the foot to evert and dorsiflex. An internally rotated talus would cause the foot to invert and plantarflex.


Answer E: An internally rotated talus, causing the foot to invert and plantarflex, is present with a posterior fibular head dysfunction.




Bottom Line: The physical examination findings of an anterior fibular head somatic dysfunction include: a proximal fibular head that resists posterior spring, the distal fibula may be posterior, and the talus is externally rotated causing the foot to evert and dorsiflex.

The physical examination findings of a posterior fibular head somatic dysfunction include: a proximal fibular head that resists anterior spring, the distal fibula may be anterior and resist posterior spring, and the talus will be internally rotated causing the foot to invert and plantarflex.

Last Updated: 9/21/2016

Link: "Babesiosis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Fever
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 10-year-old male presents to the emergency department with flu-like symptoms of three days’ duration. History reveals fevers, chills, nausea, vomiting and fatigue began after the patient returned from a camping trip near Cape Cod 2 weeks ago. Vitals reveal a blood pressure of 118/67 mmHg, a heart rate of 58/min, a respiratory rate of 20/min, and a temperature of 39.1°C (102.4ºF). Physical examination reveals a mildly jaundiced and diaphoretic young male appearing very fatigued. Laboratory analysis reveals

platelets  115/mL
total bilirubin  4.8 mg/dL 
haptoglobin   22 mg/dL
(normal = 46-165 mg/dL) 
LDH 446 U/L

A peripheral smear is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most likely etiology is

1. Babesia microti
2. Borrelia burgdorferi
3. Plasmodium falciparum
4. Rickettsia rickettsii
5. Salmonella typhi

The correct answer is: A
Babesiosis is a zoonotic disease with the primary vector of the parasite being ticks of the genus Ixodes. In the United States, human infection with Babesia is primarily due to the rodent strain B. microti, found mostly in northeastern and Midwestern states. The clinical signs and symptoms of babesiosis are related to the parasitism of red blood cells, which enter the host’s bloodstream during the tick bite, infect red blood cells, and then trophozoites are produced. Patient’s with babesiosis have symptoms similar to those of malaria and is related to the degree or parasitism. The disease manifestation is broad, ranging from a silent infection to malaria-like diseases that result in severe hemolysis. The most common symptoms are fatigue and weakness (91%), fever (91%), shaking chills (77%) and diaphoresis (69%). Increase in lactate dehydrogenase (LDH) and a properly stained peripheral blood smear showing parasite infection of the red blood cells yield the most useful results. If the patient is healthy, treatment is generally not required. However, treatment is recommended for all diagnosed cases to prevent sequelae. Elderly, immunocompromised, or asplenic patients should be put on a treatment regimen of intravenous clindamycin and oral quinine or intravenous atovaquone and azithromycin.

Answer B: A patient with babesiosis may be co-infected by Borrelia burgdorferi as the primary vector, Ixodes, is the same. This patient did not present with the usual erythema migrans, which is present in 90% of patients. Likewise, bradycardia, leukopenia, thrombocytopenia, hemolytic anemia and elevated LDH are not common in this disease. Suspected cases should be treated without lab results with ten days of doxycycline.


Answer C: Infection with Plasmodium falciparum leads to malaria and may present with very similar symptoms and lab results. This protozoa is transmitted by an infective female Anopheles mosquito vector, but has been eradicated from the United States. It is generally found in sub-Saharan Africa, which this patient was not exposed to this area. Paroxysms of fever, shaking chills, and sweats are also a common feature of this infection. The four drug classes currently used to treat malaria include quinoline compounds, antifolates, artemisinin derivatives, and antimicrobials.


Answer D: Infection with Rickettsia rickettsii leads to Rocky Mountain spotted fever and is transmitted by the tick, Dermacentor variabilis. Most cases are found in the southeast and south central regions. The most common symptoms are fever greater than 102°F within three days after tick bite (94%), headache (86%), myalgias (85%), and CNS symptoms (25%). Up to 90% of patients develop a maculopapular rash with 43% demonstrating a rash on the palms and soles. The drug of choice for treatment is doxycycline.


Answer E: Infection with Salmonella typhi leads to typhoid fever, which is typically found in developing nations with poor sanitary conditions. Symptoms begin with fever after 7-14 days that follows a pattern characterized by a rising temperature each day that drops by the subsequent morning and plateaus after the first week. Accompanying this is gastrointestinal manifestations, dry cough, dull frontal headache, delirium, and malaise. During the second week of illness, the symptoms progress with the abdomen becoming distended. In the third week the fever continues and the patient becomes more toxic and with significant weight loss. Definitive treatment of typhoid fever is based on susceptibility, and generally includes fluoroquinolones.




Bottom Line: Infection by Babesia microti should be suspected in cases where a person has been exposed to ticks especially in northeastern U.S. These patients will have flu-like symptoms and diagnosis can be confirmed with labs including increased LDH and peripheral blood smear demonstrating trophozoites in red blood cells.

For more information, see:
"Babesiosis" on Medscape.

"Chronic Lymphocytic Leukemia Clinical Presentation" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 68-year-old male with a history of chronic lymphocytic leukemia presents for follow-up with his oncologist. On review of symptoms the patient reports worsening fatigue and a recent fever that has persisted for one weeks' duration. He denies sick contacts, weight loss, or night sweats. Physical examination reveals enlarged, fixed axillary and abdominal lymph nodes. Lymph node biopsy reveals a monotonous infiltrate of small lymphocytes on one side with a pattern of diffuse large cells on the right. The most likely explanation for these findings is

1. acute myelogenous leukemia
2. benign leukemoid reaction
3. infectious mononucleosis
4. progressive chronic lymphocytic leukemia
5. Richter's transformation

The correct answer is: E
Richter’s transformation (RT) is a complication of chronic lymphocytic leukemia (CLL) or less commonly, Hairy cell leukemia, into a diffuse large B cell lymphoma. Diffuse large B-cell lymphoma is the most common type of Non-Hodgkin’s lymphoma.  RT should be suspected in patients with CLL who develop worsening B symptoms (fever, night sweats, and unintended weight loss) or rapidly progressive lymphadenopathy. Biopsy is required to make the diagnosis and histologic pattern exhibits diffuse large B cells. In this patient, the biopsy shows the pre-existing CLL, represented by the small lymphocytes, next to diffuse large B cells, which characterizes the transformation of CLL into a large B cell lymphoma.

Answer A: Acute myelogenous leukemia (AML) is a clonal proliferation of myeloid precursor cells with a reduced ability to differentiate into the mature cellular form. This accumulation of blasts or immature forms in the bone marrow and blood causes a variable reduction in the production of normal blood cells, platelets and mature granulocytes. AML typically affects older age adults with a median age of 65 years. Symptoms include general malaise and repeated infections and/or hemorrhagic findings from abnormal white and red blood cell counts as well as platelets. Enlarged lymph nodes are not a common finding. A bone marrow biopsy is necessary for a diagnosis. Immature cells such as myeloblasts are seen. Elongated clumps of granular material in the cytoplasm of leukemic blasts called Auer rods are diagnostic. Auer rods and myeloblasts were not evident on this patient’s biopsy.


Answer B: A leukemoid reaction is an elevated white blood cell count (>50,000). Leukemoid reactions are generally benign and typically in response to infection, drugs or from an underlying medical condition such as Diabetic Ketoacidosis. The peripheral blood smear shows a mix of mature neutrophil precursors in contrast to the immature forms seen in AML. This patient has a predominance of lymphocytes and large B cells making a leukemoid reaction less likely.


Answer C: Infectious Mononucleosis (IM) is a clinical manifestation of the Ebstein Barr Virus (EBV). EBV is a widely disseminated herpes virus that is spread through oral secretions. Typical features include low-grade temperature, arthralgias, malaise, pharyngitis and cervical adenopathy. Given this patient’s immunocompromised state and lack of pharyngeal complaints, IM is a less likely diagnosis. The lymph node biopsy secures the diagnosis.


Answer D: Chronic Lymphocytic Leukemia (CLL) is a type of leukemia that affects B cell lymphocytes. CLL is the most common leukemia that occurs after the age of 50. Patients with CLL are often asymptomatic and are diagnosed on routine blood work that reveals lymphocytosis (WBC 50,000-200,000). Diagnosis is confirmed by peripheral blood smear showing a predominance of mature, small lymphocytes. CLL can follow an indolent course and a patient may not need treatment. Patients with CLL have abnormal cellular and humoral-mediated immune responses and are at risk for infections. This patient may have developed an opportunistic infection but the lymph node biopsy and rapidly progressing lymph nodes make this a less likely diagnosis.




Bottom Line: Richter’s transformation should be suspected in a patient with CLL with worsening lymphadenopathy and B symptoms.

For more information, see:
Agabegi S, Agabegi E. Step-Up To Medicine. 3rd ed. Philadelphia, PA. Lippincott: Williams & Wilkins; 2013: 353-5.

"Chronic Lymphocytic Leukemia Clinical Presentation" on Medscape.

Link: "Contraception" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Services Rendered
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 22-year-old female presents to the office with questions concerning contraceptive options. History reveals she has just started a new relationship and they are planning to be sexually active in the near future. She has not had intercourse in the past and has never used contraceptives. She has no medical problems and takes no medications. She has a family history of a "blood disorder" and was told by her mother she should not take hormones. Her greatest concern is becoming pregnant and she would like the most effective contraceptive against pregnancy. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 120/82 mmHg, a heart rate of 62/min, and respiratory rate of 18/min. Physical exam reveals a healthy appearing female with a regular rate and rhythm and clear breath sounds bilaterally. In addition to counseling the patient on the risk of STIs, the most appropriate recommendation is

1. coitus interruptus
2. progestin implant
3. male condom
4. copper IUD
5. oral contraceptive pill

The correct answer is: D
Copper IUDs are one of the most effective contraceptives on the market. They can stay in place for up to ten years and have a failure rate of less than 0.8%. The Copper IUD works by acting as a natural spermicide within the uterus, and is an excellent option for patients who cannot take hormones but desire long term contraception.  

Answer A: Coitus interruptus is not a medically recommended contraceptive method as it has poor efficacy and involves the cessation of intercourse prior to ejaculation. It does not protect against sexually transmitted infections.


Answer B: The progestin implant is the most effective form of reversible contraception with failure rates around 0.05%. However, the patient in this case has requested a non hormonal form of contraception.


Answer C: The male condom has a failure rate of around 82% with typical use. It is effective at preventing some STIs.


Answer E: The oral contraceptive pill has a failure rate of around 9%. It does not protect against STIs.




Bottom Line: The copper IUD is a highly effective, non hormonal, reversible contraceptive. 

COMBANK Insight : Understand when to recommend different forms of contraception. 

For more information, see:
"Contraception" on Medscape.

Link: "Atrial fibrillation" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 45-year-old woman presents to the emergency department with the complaint of dizziness. History reveals the dizziness has been happening off and on for the last 3 days and she also reports feeling fluttering in her chest. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 124/72 mmHg and a heart rate of 145/min. An ECG is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most appropriate management is

1. aspirin, morphine, nitroglycerine
2. procainamide
3. cardioversion
4. diltiazem
5. digoxin

The correct answer is: D
The ECG shown is atrial fibrillation with rapid ventricular response and treatment is aimed at rate control. This is caused by rapid, disorderly firing from a second atrial focus, resulting in lack of coordinated atrial contractions with independent sporadic ventricular contractions. The medications of choice are calcium channel blockers or beta-blockers as well as anticoagulation (poor atrial contraction causes blood stasis, which leads to mural thrombi formation and a risk of embolization). If the patient had presented to the emergency room within 48 hours of onset then cardioversion could be an option. Since it has been at least 3 days there is a risk of thrombus formation in this patient who has not been anti-coagulated and cardioversion should be avoided.

Answer A: Aspirin, morphine, and nitroglycerine are used for unstable angina or myocardial infarction (MI). Unstable angina typically presents with worsening angina that occurs at rest, which is frequently caused by plaque rupture in the coronary arteries. ECG will show ST depression. An MI results in tissue death from ischemia caused by occlusion of the coronary vessels or vasospasm. ECG will show ST elevation in cases of an STEMI.


Answer B: Procainamide is used for atrial fibrillation with Wolff-Parkinson-White (WPW) syndrome, where AV nodal blocking agents like calcium channel blockers should be avoided. WPW is caused by a separate accessory conduction pathway between the atria and ventricles and returns a conduction impulse to the AV node. ECG will show a delta wave and shortened PR. Amiodarone is another treatment option.


Answer C: Cardioversion is used for unstable patients or patients within 48 hours of new-onset atrial fibrillation. Hemodynamic instability means systolic blood pressure < 90, chest pain, shortness of breath, or confusion. Patients experiencing ventricular tachycardia or fibrillation may require electrical cardioversion. ECG will show a series of 3+ premature ventricular contractions with a heart rate of 160-240 (VT) and lack of ordered ventricular contraction, which leads to no cardiac output (v-fib), respectively.


Answer E: Digoxin can be used adjunctively to treat atrial fibrillation, but is less effective for rate control than calcium channel blockers or beta-blockers. Digoxin slows the ventricular rate during atrial fibrillation primarily by vagotonic inhibition of AV nodal conduction. In patients with HF due to systolic dysfunction and AF, digoxin has two potential benefits, reduction in the ventricular rate and improvement in contractility. However, since digoxin is less effective in rate control than beta-blockers and calcium channel blockers and has no ability to terminate AF, it should not be used as a first-line treatment. Furthermore, as mentioned previously, patients who have atrial fibrillation must be anticoagulated.




Bottom Line: Atrial fibrillation with rapid ventricular response can be initially managed with diltiazem (calcium channel blockers or beta-blockers).

COMBANK Insight : Management of atrial fibrillation, acute and chronic, is a common topic on the COMLEX. Management of less common associations may be seen, such as rapid ventricular response (diltiazem) and Wolff-Parkinson-White (procainamide).

For more information, see:
"Atrial fibrillation" on Medscape.

Link: Medscape "Pleural Effusion"


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 71-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaint of progressive shortness of breath and peripheral edema for one weeks' duration. He is admitted to the hospital and an echocardiogram shows a low ejection fraction. A computerized tomographic scan is obtained and reveals bilateral pleural effusions. The pleural effusion can be best described as

1. being caused by inflammation
2. fluid with a high protein level and high specific gravity
3. fluid with a low protein level and low specific gravity
4. having a pleural: serum LDH ratio >0.6
5. having a pleural: serum protein ratio >0.5

The correct answer is: C
Pleural effusions are classified as either exudative or transudative. Transudative effusions usually are due to a change in hydrostatic or oncotic pressure, as in conditions such as congestive heart failure, liver cirrhosis, and nephrotic syndrome. The patient in the above scenario has pleural effusions secondary to congestive heart failure, which makes transudative fluid the correct statement.

Answers A & D & E: This is characteristic of an exudative effusion.


Answer B: This describes an exudative effusion. Exudative effusions are generally due to inflammation, such as an infectious or neoplastic process, and are characterized by a pleural to serum LDH ratio of >0.6, a pleural to serum protein ratio >0.5 and a total protein >3 g/dL.




Bottom Line: A pleural effusion qualifies as an exudate with the one or more of the following criteria: pleural to serum LDH ratio of >0.6, pleural: serum protein ratio >0.5, total protein >3 g/dL, or pleural fluid LDH >2/3 the normal upper limit of serum LDH.

COMBANK Insight : Light's criteria are used to evaluate whether fluid from a pleural effusion is a transudate or exudate. Transudative fluid has a low protein content whereas exudative fluid has a high protein content. Light's criteria for an exudative pleural effusion are a total protein of effusion/ total protein of serum > 0.5 OR LDH of the effusion/ LDH of serum > 0.6 OR LDH of the effusion >2/3 the upper limit of normal of LDH of serum. This criterion has 98% sensitivity and 83% specificity. It misidentifies 25% of transudates as exudates.

For more information, see:
Medscape "Pleural Effusion"

Link: "Lyme disease" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 29-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of bilateral knee pain. History reveals the patient is a park ranger at Niagara Falls and noticed a small target-like rash in his armpit a few weeks ago. He states the knee pain has been worsening over the last few weeks and he has also been experiencing more fatigue than usual. Vital signs reveal show a temperature of 37.1ºC (98.8ºF), a blood pressure of 116/74 mmHg, a heart rate of 68/min, and a respiratory rate of 14/min. Physical examination reveals a mild effusion and erythema in both knees. Range of motion is within normal limits and McMurray and Apley tests are negative. There is no ligamentous laxity.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The best initial test is

1. blood culture
2. drug screen
3. enzyme linked immunosorbent assay
4. urinary antigen testing
5. western blot

The correct answer is: C
Enzyme linked immunosorbent assay (ELISA) is the initial screening test for any patient with a recent history of travel or residency in an area endemic for Lyme disease, has a risk of exposure to ticks, and has symptoms consistent with early disseminated or late Lyme disease. This patient meets all of these criteria for initiating this screening serologic testing. Lyme disease, caused by Borrelia burgdorferi, is transmitted by the black-legged tick and is typically found in northeastern United States. Early localized disease typically includes a bullseye-like rash called erythema migrans and other nonspecific viral-like syndrome symptoms such as fatigue, joint pain and swelling, and fevers. The knees are common sites of joint pain in Lyme disease. Early disseminated disease manifests with possible neurologic, cardiac, ocular, or other cutaneous findings. Late disease includes arthritis, worsening neurologic findings resembling encephalopathy, and further cutaneous features.

Answer A: Blood culture media for B. burgdorferi is not readily available in most clinical laboratories, so it would not be the initial test of choice. Blood cultures in one study were found to yield positive results in only 46 percent of cases with PCR yielding positive results in 71 percent of these same cases. This demonstrates that blood cultures are not the optimal test for screening this disease.


Answer B: This clinical scenario would not be suspect for drug abuse, so a drug screen would not be necessary.


Answer D: Urinary antigen testing demonstrates no value in the diagnosis of Lyme disease.


Answer E: Western blot is the confirmatory test for determining whether a person has Lyme disease. It provides more information than ELISA regarding the type of antigens that are reacting. This test is utilized when the ELISA test is either positive or if the test is equivocal.




Bottom Line: Enzyme linked immunosorbent assay (ELISA) is the initial screening test for any patient with a recent history of travel or residency in an area endemic for Lyme disease, has a risk of exposure to ticks, and has symptoms consistent with early disseminated or late Lyme disease.

For more information, see:
"Lyme disease" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 29-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of bilateral knee pain. History reveals the patient is a park ranger at Niagara Falls and noticed a small target-like rash in his armpit a few weeks ago. He states the knee pain has been worsening over the last few weeks and he has also been experiencing more fatigue than usual. Vital signs reveal show a temperature of 37.1ºC (98.8ºF), a blood pressure of 116/74 mmHg, a heart rate of 68/min, and a respiratory rate of 14/min. Physical examination reveals a mild effusion and erythema in both knees. Range of motion is within normal limits and McMurray and Apley tests are negative. There is no ligamentous laxity.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The initial test is found to be positive. The test to confirm the diagnosis is?

1. blood culture
2. drug screen
3. enzyme linked immunosorbent assay
4. no further testing is necessary
5. western blot

The correct answer is: E
Western blot is the confirmatory test for determining whether a person has Lyme disease. It provides more information than enzyme linked immunosorbent assay (ELISA) regarding the type of antigens that are reacting, and has a high specificity. This test is utilized when the ELISA test is either positive (due to chance of false positive results) or if the test is equivocal. If the ELISA test is negative, then a person is considered negative and would not require further testing.

Answer A: Blood culture media for Borrelia burgdorferi is not readily available in most clinical laboratories, so it would not be the initial test of choice. Blood cultures in one study were found to yield positive results in only 46 percent of cases with PCR yielding positive results in 71 percent of these same cases. This demonstrates that blood cultures are not the optimal test for screening this disease.


Answer B: This clinical scenario would not be suspect for drug abuse, so a drug screen would not be necessary.


Answer C: Enzyme linked immunosorbent assay (ELISA) is the initial screening test for any patient with a recent history of travel or residency in an area endemic for Lyme disease, has a risk of exposure to ticks, and has symptoms consistent with early disseminated or late Lyme disease. It should be confirmed with western blot.


Answer D: The ELISA test has very high sensitivity, but its specificity can vary. This test depends on presence of IgM in the serum, so timing plays a crucial role. Since the specificity is not as high, utilizing the two-tier protocol with Western blot is necessary for those that test either positive or equivocal with ELISA.




Bottom Line: Western blot is the confirmatory test for Lyme disease, and is utilized in patients testing positive or equivocal with ELISA.

For more information, see:
"Lyme disease" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination

Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 21-year-old G3P0 female patient presents to the emergency room at 8 weeks' gestation with vaginal bleeding. History reveals she does not believe she has passed any products of conception. Physical examination reveals there is blood in the vaginal vault and a dilated cervical os. Ultrasound confirms an intrauterine pregnancy and fetal heart motion is observed. The most likely diagnosis is

1. complete abortion
2. incomplete abortion
3. inevitable abortion
4. missed abortion
5. threatened abortion

The correct answer is: C
An inevitable abortion occurs when the cervix has dilated during the first trimester of pregnancy without passage of the products of conception.

Answer A: Complete abortion is the expulsion of all products of conception.


Answer B: Incomplete abortion is when some, but not all, products of conception are passed.


Answer D: Missed abortion is fetal demise without cervical dilation or passage of products of conception.


Answer E: Threatened abortion refers to any vaginal bleeding before 20 weeks of gestation.




Bottom Line: An inevitable abortion occurs when the cervix has dilated during the first trimester of pregnancy without passage of the products of conception.

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 59-year-old male presents to the emergency department complaining of fever, chills and dysuria for the past 24 hours. Past medical history is significant for hypothyroidism, hypertension and obesity. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 38.4°C (101.2°F), blood pressure of 146/79mm/Hg, heart rate 92/min, respiratory rate of 18/min, and an oxygen saturation of 96% on room air. Physical examination reveals normal external genitalia, non-tender testes, and an exquisitely tender prostate on rectal exam. The most appropriate management is

1. amoxicillin-clavulanate
2. ampicillin
3. ceftriaxone plus doxycycline
4. nitrofurantoin
5. trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole

The correct answer is: E
This patient is presenting with acute bacterial prostatitis. In approximately 80% of cases, the causative organism is Escherichia coli. The remainders of the cases are caused by Klebsiella, Enterobacter, Proteus and Pseudomonas species. The acutely inflamed prostate can cause fevers, rigors, dysuria, bacteriuria and a tense, tender prostate on exam. Voiding urgency and bladder outlet obstruction or retention is also common. Acute bacterial prostatitis is usually spontaneous but has been known to occur due to indwelling Foley catheters. First-line agents in the treatment of acute prostatitis are trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole or a fluoroquinolone such as ciprofloxacin. Alternative antibiotics include ampicillin with gentamicin. Chronic prostatitis is less frequent and is more difficult to treat than the acute infection of the prostate. Chronic prostatitis is usually associated with low back pain or perineal pain. Rarely, some cases of prostatitis can lead to a prostatic abscess.

Answer A: Amoxicillin-clavulanate is used to treat infections of the lower respiratory tract, otitis media, sinusitis, skin, urinary tract and bite wounds. It does cover Klebsiella and Enterobacter but these organisms are less likely causes of acute bacterial prostatitis. The most likely causative organism is this patient is Escherichia coli for which amoxicillin-clavulanate is not an appropriate treatment option.


Answer B: Ampicillin would be an appropriate answer if the patient had a known infection with Enterobacter species. Post-massage urine or prostatic expressate can be cultured in patients with prostatitis to identify the causative organism. Since 80% of acute bacterial prostatitis is caused by Escherichia coli, ampicillin is not the appropriate answer choice. Ampicillin combined with gentamicin would be an appropriate treatment for empiric coverage of acute bacterial prostatitis.


Answer C: Ceftriaxone and doxycycline would be appropriate medications for the treatment of epididymitis in a sexually active male less than 35 years old. These medications cover Neisseria gonorrhea and Chlamydia trachomatis. These organisms are not likely causes of acute bacterial prostatitis and therefore would not be the appropriate medication choice in this patient.


Answer D: Nitrofurantoin is a treatment option for urinary tract infections caused by organisms such as Escherichia coli, Enterobacter and Klebsiella; however, it does not penetrate prostatic tissue and would not be an appropriate choice for this patient.




Bottom Line: Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole is the first-line antibiotic for treatment of acute bacterial prostatitis.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 52-year-old male presents to the office after being diagnosed with chronic obstructive pulmonary disease. The patient asks which treatments are available to him that will decrease his morbidity and mortality. Morbidity and mortality have been shown to be decreased in patients suffering from chronic obstructive pulmonary disease who are treated with

1. antibiotics
2. home oxygen
3. inhaled anticholinergics
4. inhaled long-acting beta-2 agonists
5. inhaled short-acting beta-2 agonists

The correct answer is: B
Home oxygen has been shown to reduce morbidity and mortality in patients suffering from COPD. The only things to have such an effect are home O2, smoking cessation, and lung volume reduction surgery.

Answers A & C & D & E: All options provide the patient with symptomatic relief and improved quality of life although have not been proven to have an effects on morbidity and mortality of the patient. Bottom Line: Home oxygen therapy, smoking cessation, and lung volume reduction surgery have been shown to reduce morbidity and mortality in patients with chronic obstructive pulmonary disease.




COMBANK Insight : Understanding the difference between symptomatic treatment and improvement in morbidity and mortality is important. Make sure you read the question carefully to see which of the two is being asked.

Link: "Hypokalemia in DKA" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 14-year-old female with insulin-dependent diabetes presents to the emergency department complaining of two days’ duration of right-sided abdominal pain, nausea, and vomiting. The patient states that she has 100% compliance with her insulin therapy, is not sexually active, and has no urinary complaints. Physical examination reveals a diaphoretic female with hyperpnea and tenderness to her right lower quadrant. Vitals reveal a blood pressure of 110/70 mmHg, a heart rate of 120/min, a respiratory rate of 34/min, a room air oxygen saturation of 99%, and a temperature of 39.0°C (102.2°F). She has a urinalysis positive for ketones. Diagnostic studies reveal the following:

Sodium:   129 mEq/L
Potassium:  3.9 mEq/L
Chloride:  92 mmol/L             
Bicarbonate:                   7 mEq/dL
Glucose: 488 mg/dL 

 You initiate intravenous fluid resuscitation with 0.9% normal saline, start the patient on an insulin drip and plan admit her to the ICU for further care. This patient is most likely to develop

1. hyperkalemia
2. hyperchloremia
3. hypernatremia
4. hypokalemia
5. hyponatremia

The correct answer is: D
This patient presents with diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA). This is an endocrinology emergency that occurs mainly in insulin-dependent diabetics. DKA presents with a positive anion gap metabolic acidosis. This patients anion gap is thirty as calculated by the following formula; Na - (Cl+HCO3). Patients with DKA are treated with fluids, insulin, and electrolyte repletion as necessary. As you give this patient insulin, potassium is driven into the intracellular space via the Na/K pump. Since total body potassium stores are typically depleted in DKA, this patient can have a significant and life threatening drop in her serum potassium level. Checking frequent potassium levels is required when treating DKA. Most hospitals have specialized order sets designed to avoid the life threatening complications of severe hypokalemia.

Answer A: Patients experiencing diabetic ketoacidosis are frequently depleted of total body potassium stores. The patient likely has a significant acidosis as indicated by serum bicarbonate of 7 mEq/dL. Due to the Na/K pump, the relative amount of potassium is directly influenced by the amount of hydrogen ions in the serum. As the acidosis is corrected by insulin, potassium is driven into the intracellular space, thus lowering the serum potassium concentration.


Answer B: This patient has hypochloremia now due to her anion-gap metabolic acidosis. As you correct with 0.9% sodium chloride, the chloride will return to normal concentration. Rarely is hyperchloremia of significant clinical influence.


Answer C: The patient currently has pseudohyponatremia due to excessive glucose in the serum. This is corrected by calculating the corrected sodium by adding 1.8 x [glucose] >100 to the measured sodium. As you correct this patient’s glucose with crystalloid infusion, the sodium should return to normal concentration.


Answer E: The patient has a pseudohyponatremia from excessive glucose in the serum. As the serum glucose decreases, the serum sodium will increase.




Bottom Line: Hypokalemia is the most important electrolyte abnormality in a patient with diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA). Frequent monitoring of serum potassium levels is mandatory after starting insulin therapy.

COMBANK Insight : Understanding potassium shifts is important in several areas of medicine. An easy way to remember that hypokalemia will occur when treating DKA with insulin is by remembering the treatment for hyperkalemia. Insulin or inhaled beta-agonists are used commonly to treat hyperkalemia. They work by driving extracellular potassium intracellularly, thus lowering serum potassium concentrations.

For more information, see:
Ayala C, Spellberg B. Boards and Wards. 4th Ed. Philadelphia, PA; Lippincott Williams & Wilkins; 2010: 78-79.
"Hypokalemia in DKA" on Medscape.
Sanjay A, et al. "Prevalence of hypokalemia in ED patients with diabetic ketoacidosis." Vol 30;3:481-484. March 2012. http://www.ajemjournal.com/article/S0735-6757(11)00011-8/abstract

Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 70-year-old female presents to your office with complaint of a new tan-colored skin lesion. She first noticed the lesion several months ago when she was putting on her bra. She also noticed multiple similar tan lesions located below her right breast. One of her right breast lesions is as shown in the exhibit. The most appropriate management is

1. ketoconazole 2% topical cream
2. punch biopsy
3. reassurance
4. shave biopsy
5. wide local excision

The correct answer is: C
The lesion shown in the exhibit is a classic seborrheic keratosis. It has the characteristic “stuck-on appearance” with a well-circumscribed border. Upon palpation, the surface feels rough and will crumble if picked. SKs are more common with age and can occur on any hair-bearing surface. They usually occur on sun-exposed areas and underneath the breasts, where they can become red and irritated. Patients often believe these lesions are melanomas because of their tan to dark color and occasional irregular borders. SKs are usually of no clinical significance unless many erupt suddenly, which could be part of a paraneoplastic syndrome. SKs themselves have no malignant potential and do not need removal, although they can be removed for cosmetic reasons, thus reassurance is the most appropriate answer.

Answer A: Ketoconazole topical cream is an anti-fungal used for tinea infections and seborrheic dermatitis, not seborrheic keratosis.


Answer B: A punch biopsy allows for visualization of the full skin thickness. Punch biopsies can be performed on a wide variety of skin conditions, including suspected squamous cell and basal cell carcinomas. A punch biopsy is not necessary in this situation.


Answer D: Shave biopsy is used for skin lesions suspected to affect only the epidermis and dermis. If this lesion were in a cosmetically unfavorable location, such as the face, then a shave biopsy could be performed to remove the lesion. Since the patient is not bothered by their appearance, reassurance is the only necessary treatment.


Answer E: A wide local excision is usually the treatment of choice for squamous cell and basal cell carcinomas and malignant melanoma. If the melanoma is >1 mm depth, then a sentinel lymph node study must also be performed.




Bottom Line: Seborrheic Keratosis is a benign hyperplasia of basaloid epidermal cells with horn pseudocysts. SKs occur as dark brown to tan plaques or papules and have a characteristic stuck on appearance. Unless cosmetically unfavorable, reassurance is the best treatment choice.

For more information, see:
Habif, TP. Clinical Dermatology: A color guide to diagnosis and therapy. 5th ed. Mosby Elsevier. 2010. pp: 776-783.

Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 7-year-old male presents to the office with a rash. The patient's mother reports the rash began on the head and spread to the chest. Further history reveals one week earlier the patient stayed home from school after developing a cough, runny nose, and high fever. Medical history reveals no known drug allergies, and an unknown immunization status. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 103.2ºF. Physical examination reveals a maculopapular rash on the head and trunk of the patient.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. Chickenpox
2. Fifth disease
3. German measles
4. Measles
5. Roseola

The correct answer is: D
The most likely diagnosis is measles. A common mnemonic to remember the prodrome symptoms of measles is the “3-C’s.” Cough, coryza, and conjunctivitis as well as Koplik spots (which may or may not be noted) are characteristic of measles in the early stage. Coryza is used to describe the symptoms of a cold such as nasal congestion. Koplik spots are red oral lesions with a white center. All of the above symptoms are prodromic and will disappear before the measles rash itself. Two to five days later the child will have a high fever with a maculopapular rash that begins on the face and spreads downward. The rash will last up to five days and resolve from the head downward. Measles is now a preventable disease due to the MMR live attenuated vaccine.

Answer A: Chickenpox also can present with high fever but the characteristic pruritic rash first appears on the trunk and then spreads peripherally as a mixture of red papules that develop into clear vesicles. These vesicles then break and form scabs which tend to localize in a “cropped” pattern.


Answer B: Fifth disease presents with the erythematous cheeks referred to as the "slapped cheeks" pattern. It subsequently forms an erythematous, pruritic, maculopapular rash that begins on the upper extremities and then spreads to the trunk in a reticular pattern.


Answer C: Rubella (german measles) is caused by an RNA togavirus and presents as an erythematous maculopapular rash with generalized lymphadenopathy and low-grade fever.


Answer E: Roseola is caused by human herpes virus six (HHV-6). It occurs in young children and presents as a high fever with no symptoms that resolves when the rash forms. It appears as a maculopapular rash on the trunk, which spreads peripherally.




Bottom Line: Measles typically presents with symptoms of malaise, high fever, along with the three Cs (Cough, Coryza, and Conjunctivitis).

For more information, see:
"Measles" on Medscape.

Link: "Measles" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 7-year-old male presents to the office with a rash. The patient's mother reports the rash began on the head and spread to the chest. Further history reveals one week earlier the patient stayed home from school after developing a cough, runny nose, and high fever. Medical history reveals no known drug allergies, and an unknown immunization status. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 103.2ºF. Physical examination reveals a maculopapular rash on the head and trunk of the patient.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
A potential secondary complication of this diagnosis is

1. fetal hydrops
2. rocky mountain spotted fever
3. shingles
4. subacute sclerosing panencephalitis
5. thrombocytopenia

The correct answer is: D
Subacute sclerosing panencephalitis (SSPE) is a very rare but progressively fatal degenerative disease of the CNS.  Its pathogenesis is not well understood but is likely from a persistent viral infection in the CNS. Patients with SSPE tend to be younger than 20 years of age, and SSPE develops about 7-10 years after having measles. There are multiple stages to the disease: Stage I lasts weeks to years and the patient develops insidious neurologic symptoms (eg: personality changes, lethargy), Stage II lasts three months to one year and is characterized by myoclonus, progressive dementia, and motor or sensory disease. In stage II patients maintain massive myoclonic jerks very frequently (as often as every 5-10 seconds). Stages III and IV are end-stages and are characterized by further neurologic deterioration, flaccidity or rigidity (usually decorticate), and autonomic dysfunction. Myoclonus is actually absent in these end-stages and death usually occurs in stage IV. Seizures can occur any time and some cases may go into temporary remission in between stages. Of note, this is a different entity than post-infectious encephalomyelitis which is thought to be of autoimmune origin and occurs much sooner after measles infection (usually within two weeks).

Answers A & B & C & E: These conditions are not associated with measles.




Bottom Line: Subacute sclerosing panencephalitis (SSPE) is a rare but fatal neurologic complication of measles that occurs years after the infection.

COMBANK Insight : Medications used in the treatment or prevention of measles include vitamin A, antivirals (eg, ribavirin), measles virus vaccine, and human immunoglobulin.

For more information, see:
"Measles" on Medscape.

Link: "Hemophilia A" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 13-year-old male is brought to the emergency department after being involved in a motor vehicle accident. The boy’s mother states her son has a history of hemophilia A. Which of the following laboratory test are most consistent with this history?

1. decreased platelet count, increased PT, increased PTT
2. decreased platelet count, increased PT, normal PTT
3. decreased platelet count, normal PT, normal PTT
4. normal platelet count, normal PT, increased PTT
5. normal platelet count, increased PT, normal PTT

The correct answer is: D
Hemophilia A is an X-linked recessive disorder that is caused by a deficiency of factor VIII of the coagulation cascade. More specifically, factor VIII is a part of the intrinsic pathway of the coagulation cascade, the activity of which is measured by the PTT. Therefore, a deficiency in factor VIII would cause an increased PTT.

Answers A & B & C: Since this disease involves clotting factor VIII, it would not have an effect on platelets. Therefore, these answer choices can be eliminated.


Answer E: Factor VIII is not a part of the extrinsic pathway, which would have effects on PT, thus making this answer choice incorrect as well.




Bottom Line: The following coagulation profile is consistent with the diagnosis of hemophilia A: normal platelet count, normal PT, increased PTT.

COMBANK Insight : Male patients with severe hemophilia may present at the time of circumcision. Easy bruising may occur at the start of ambulation or primary dentition. The patient may have a history of hemarthroses and prolonged bleeding with surgical procedures, trauma, dental extraction, and he or she may have spontaneous bleeding in soft tissues.

For more information, see:
"Hemophilia A" on Medscape.

20 of 200

Link: "Wilson Disease" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 31-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of malaise and abdominal discomfort. Her husband reports over the last few months her mood has been erratic and she has been stumbling frequently. The patient denies any drug or alcohol use. Physical examination reveals a flat affect and lack of expression. She has several centrally blanching angiomas on her chest and upper back and a distended abdomen notable for shifting dullness. Laboratory examination reveals:

ceruloplasmin level  15 mg/dL
(normal 20-50 mg/dL) 
AST  247 U/L 
ALT  197 U/L 
total bilirubin  15 mg/dL 
alkaline phosphatase  7 U/L

The most likely diagnosis is

1. depression with substance abuse
2. hemochromatosis
3. hepatolenticular degeneration
4. multiple sclerosis
5. zinc deficiency

The correct answer is: C
Wilson disease, also known as hepatolenticular degeneration, is a disease of impaired biliary copper excretion. In Wilson disease the defect lies in copper transporting P-type ATPase due to autosomal recessive mutations in the ATP7B gene. Excess copper resulting from promotes free radical formation that results in oxidation of lipids and proteins. Initially copper accumulates in the liver, damaging hepatocytes and leading to cirrhosis. As serum copper levels increase, it deposits in other organs. The most common presentation of Wilson disease is cirrhosis and should be considered in any unexplained liver disease, especially in individuals under the age of 40 years. Other manifestations of Wilson disease include neuropsychiatric symptoms (difficulty speaking, excessive salivation, ataxia, masklike facies, clumsiness with the hands, and personality changes), osteoarthropathy with premature osteoarthritis, hemolytic anemia (Coombs-negative), and less commonly, renal impairment. Physical exam will reveal stigmata of cirrhosis (spider angiomas, ascites, palmar erythema) and ophthalmic exam may reveal Kayser-Fleischer rings. Kayser-Fleischer rings are greenish gold to brown in color and are formed by the deposition of copper in the Descemet membrane in the limbus of the cornea – usually only seen by slit lamp examination. The diagnosis of Wilson disease is made by serum ceruloplasmin levels less than 20 mg/dL; urinary copper excretion rate greater than 100 mg/day, and liver biopsy that demonstrates tissue copper levels of greater than 250 mcg/g of dry weight. Low alkaline phosphatase levels are also characteristic. Treatment includes chelation therapy with penicillamine, low copper diet, and liver transplantation (which is curative).

Answer A: While depression with substance abuse is certainly possible with the presentation, the laboratory exam is more consistent with Wilson disease.


Answer B: Hemochromatosis is an autosomal recessive genetic disorder leading to abnormal accumulation of iron in parenchymal organs, leading to organ toxicity. It is associated with high serum levels ferritin, transferrin, iron, and high transferrin saturation. Hemochromatosis is the most common inherited liver disease in white persons and the most common autosomal recessive genetic disorder.


Answer D: Multiple sclerosis (MS) is an immune-mediated inflammatory disease that attacks myelinated axons in the central nervous system. The presentation of MS is highly variable but can include cognitive changes, ataxia, paresis, fatigue, and visual symptoms. Episodes of MS present months or years apart and in different anatomic locations. Characteristic brain imaging studies include periventricular white matter changes demonstrated by MRI.


Answer E: Zinc deficiency is a nutritional deficiency associated with malabsorption, diarrhea, chronic liver disease, chronic renal disease, sickle-cell disease, diabetes, malignancy, and can also occur after bariatric surgery, and heavy metal exposure. Acrodermatitis enteropathica is an autosomal recessive disorder of zinc absorption seen in infants. Signs of zinc deficiency include diarrhea, anorexia, acne, and impairments in sight, taste, smell, and memory. While zinc deficiency can result in low alkaline phosphatase levels it is not associated with alterations in ceruloplasmin levels.




Bottom Line: Wilson disease, also known as hepatolenticular degeneration, is characterized by cirrhosis and neuropsychiatric changes and is diagnosed by low serum ceruloplasmin levels, elevated copper excretion, and identification of Kayser-Fleischer rings.

For more information, see:
"Wilson Disease" on Medscape.


Last Updated: 9/22/2016

Link: "Seborrheic Dermatitis" on Medscape.



Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 75-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of a rash. Past medical history includes Alzheimer disease. Physical examination reveals macular areas of erythema and scaling behind the ears, on the scalp, eyebrows, and nasal labial folds. A picture is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most likely diagnosis is

1. atopic dermatitis
2. pityriasis rosea
3. psoriasis
4. seborrheic dermatitis
5. tinea versicolor

The correct answer is: D
The patient has the typical areas of involvement of seborrheic dermatitis, which presents as scaling and flaking in areas of sebaceous glands. This common dermatitis appears to be worse in many neurological diseases, including Alzheimer's disease, Parkinson's disease, and is also very common and severe in patients with HIV/AIDS. In general, symptoms are worse in the winter. Treatment included Selenium sulfide shampoo to the affected areas and topical steroids for severe disease.

Answer A: Atopic dermatitis (atopic eczema) is a disease characterized by chronic skin inflammation which is atopic, hereditary, and non-contagious. The skin of a patient with atopic dermatitis reacts abnormally and easily to irritants, food, and environmental allergens and becomes red, flaky and extremely pruritic, opening the skin up to potential infection. The skin on the flexural surfaces of the joints (for example inner sides of elbows and knees) are most commonly affected.


Answer B: Pityriasis rosea typically presents as numerous patches of pink or red that are oval in shape and are most commonly seen on the torso. A single, large red "herald" patch may occur 1 to 20 days before smaller, more numerous patches are witnessed.


Answer C: Psoriasis also affects the skin and joints. The scaly patches caused by psoriasis, called psoriatic plaques, are areas of inflammation and excessive skin production.


Answer E: Tinea versicolor (pityriasis versicolor) is associated when the skin becomes infected with Malassezia furfur. Generally oval or irregularly shaped spots of 0.5 to 2.5 cm in diameter, often merging together to form a larger patch, these spots are most commonly seen on the back, armpit, chest, lower legs, and neck. Occasionally it can be seen on the face. Under a microscope, scrapings are characterized by a "spaghetti and meat ball appearance" that occur secondary to filament production.




Bottom Line: Seborrheic dermatitis commonly presents as regions of macular scaling behind the ears and on the scalp, eyebrows, nasal labial folds, and central chest and is common in neurological disorders and in patients with HIV/AIDS.

COMBANK Insight : Make sure you recognize different types of skin disorders both by description and by image.

For more information, see:
"Seborrheic Dermatitis" on Medscape.

Link: "Megaloblastic Anemia" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 35-year-old female presents to the office with complaints of fatigue, weakness, and impaired balance with walking. She has a history of Hashimoto's thyroiditis and several bouts of gastritis. Physical examination reveals impaired vibration sensation. Peripheral blood smear is obtained as shown in the exhibit. Which of the following is the most accurate statement about her diagnosis?

1. it is caused by a bacterial infection
2. she has an increased risk of gastric lymphoma
3. she has anti-parietal intrinsic factor antibodies
4. she has folic acid deficiency
5. there is excess secretion of gastric acid

The correct answer is: C
This patient is presenting with pernicious anemia. The characteristic findings include megaloblastic anemia with hypersegmented neutrophils (as the blood smear shows), chronic gastritis and vitamin B12 deficiency. This is an autoimmune disorder and is usually co-morbid with other autoimmune diseases (in this case Hashimoto thyroiditis). Autoantibodies against the parietal cells of the stomach and intrinsic factor are usually found.

Answer A: Bacterial infection with H. pylori is a common cause of gastritis but it is not associated with autoimmune disease and hypersegmented neutrophils.


Answer B: She has an increased risk of gastric carcinoma, not lymphoma.


Answer D: A deficiency of folic acid also causes megaloblastic anemia, but without neurological symptoms.


Answer E: Pernicious anemia is associated low gastric acid secretion, not increased, because it targets parietal cells.




Bottom Line: Pernicious anemia is associated with chronic gastritis and vitamin B12 deficiency. Antibodies against parietal cells and intrinsic factor are found.

COMBANK Insight : Know the causes of megaloblastic anemia. Vitamin B12 deficiency causes neurological symptoms while folic acid deficiency does not.

Link: "Etiology of lower gastrointestinal bleeding in adults" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Bleeding
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 68-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of increased fatigue. History reveals dark stools over the last 2-3 weeks. Physical examination reveals pale conjunctiva, capillary refill > 3 seconds, and tissue texture changes at T10. A stool guaiac test is positive (+). 
Question 1 of 2 in this set
Where is the gastrointestinal bleed most likely bleeding from?

1. Cecum
2. Esophageal varices
3. Gastric ulcer
4. Sigmoid colon
5. Splenic flexure of the large intestine

The correct answer is: A
The cecum is part of the midgut, which receives innervation from T10-11. The foregut is from the oral cavity to the proximal 1/2 of the 2nd portion of the duodenum (the descending portion) at the position of the major papilla (otherwise known as the ampulla of Vater and entry of the common bile duct). The midgut extends from this point of the duodenum to the distal 1/3 of the transverse colon. Of note, some anatomic references consider the splenic flexure to be the end of the midgut. The hindgut continues from this point to the rectum. Studies have shown that the color and consistency of stool is not sensitive or specific enough to confidently identify the location of a GI bleed. The most definitive step in the diagnosis of this patient would be some form of endoscopy (i.e. EGD, Pill-cam, and/or colonoscopy).

Answer B: The esophagus is part of the foregut, which receives innervation from T5-9.


Answer C: The stomach is part of the foregut, which receives innervation from T5-9.


Answer D: The sigmoid colon is part of the hindgut, which receives innervation from T12-L2.


Answer E: The splenic flexure is part of the hindgut, which receives innervation from T12-L2.




Bottom Line: Gastrointestinal bleeding is a very common cause of anemia in the elderly population. The cecum is the most common site of diverticular bleeding. Of note, the left side of the colon is the most common site of diverticuli, but not of diverticular bleeds.

COMBANK Insight : Knowing the innervation levels and Chapman's reflexes are invaluable for doing well on COMLEX. Often, you will be required to choose between a few answer choices which are based solely on osteopathic findings.

For more information, see:
"Etiology of lower gastrointestinal bleeding in adults" on UpToDate.

Link: "Upper gastrointestinal bleeding" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Bleeding
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 68-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of increased fatigue. History reveals dark stools over the last 2-3 weeks. Physical examination reveals pale conjunctiva, capillary refill > 3 seconds, and tissue texture changes at T10. A stool guaiac test is positive (+). 
Question 2 of 2 in this set
What is the most common etiology of an upper gastrointestinal bleed?

1. Arteriovenous malformation
2. Esophageal varices
3. Gastrointestinal malignancy
4. Mallory-Weiss tear
5. Peptic ulcer disease

The correct answer is: E
Peptic ulcer disease (PUD) accounts for the majority of upper gastrointestinal bleeding (UGIB). It is estimated that PUD accounts for 30-40% of UGIB. Upper GI bleeding occurs from a source proximal to the ligament of Treitz. Symptoms can include hematemesis, hematochezia, melena, and fatigue due to blood loss, light-headedness, and syncope. Peptic ulcer disease is highly correlated with Helicobacter pylori infection, which creates disruption of the gastric mucosa and ulcerations. If the ulcerations occur at the location of blood vessels bleeding can occur.

Answer A: Arteriovenous malformations (AVMs), are the third most common cause of UGIB. AVM is considered the most common etiology of lower gastrointestinal bleeding in patients over 60-years-old, like the one in the question; however, recent studies contend that AVM might not be as common as previously thought.


Answer B: Esophageal varices are the second most common cause of UGIB.


Answer C: Gastrointestinal malignancy is the fifth most common cause of UGIB.


Answer D: Mallory-Weiss tears are the fourth most common cause of UGIB.




Bottom Line: The most common causes of UGIB: Peptic ulcer disease > Esophageal varices > Arteriovenous malformations > Mallory-Weiss tears > Malignancy.

COMBANK Insight : Do not forget that GI bleeds are very common and require extensive workup to determine the source and appropriate follow-up.

For more information, see:
"Upper gastrointestinal bleeding" on Medscape

Link: "Nipple discharge" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Discharge
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Surgery
A 41-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of bloody nipple discharge in her left breast. She has no significant past medical history and no family history of breast disease. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 121/71 mmHg, a heart rate of 71/min, a temperature of 37.0°C (98.6°F), and a respiratory rate of 12/min. Physical examination reveals bloody discharge that can be manually expressed from the left breast. There are no lesions on the nipples or palpable masses in either breast. Mammography does not detect any masses or nodules. Breast ultrasound detects a small cyst within the mammary duct. The most likely diagnosis is

1. fibroadenoma
2. intraductal papilloma
3. lobular carcinoma in situ
4. paget's disease of the breast
5. radial scar

The correct answer is: B
Intraductal papillomas result from papillary cells that grow into the lumen of a cyst in the mammary ducts. These are usually solitary lesions that may result in bloody or clear nipple discharge. They may be detected as a lump on breast examination, a cyst on ultrasound, or through ductography. These lesions may harbor ductal carcinoma in situ (DCIS); therefore, once detected, excision is of the papilloma is indicated to rule-out areas of atypia. If there are no atypical cells in the papilloma, there is no increased risk of malignancy associated with these lesions.

Answer A: Fibroadenoma is a benign solid tumor of the breast that does not typically present with nipple discharge.


Answer C: Lobular carcinoma in situ (LCIS) is a noninvasive lesion of the lobules and terminal ducts of the breast, which may progress to advanced disease. It should be noted that LCIS is associated with bilateral breast disease, while DCIS is associated with unilateral breast disease.


Answer D: Paget’s disease of the breast may present with bloody discharge, but there is usually a scaly or ulcerated lesion on the nipple.


Answer E: Radial scar is a sclerosing lesion of the breast that may appear as a malignancy on mammography and does not typically present with nipple discharge.




Bottom Line: Intraductal papillomas are typically benign breast lesions that present with bloody nipple discharge but should be excised given small risk of harboring DCIS.

For more information, see:
"Nipple discharge" on UpToDate.
"Overview of benign breast disease" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Psychiatry
Discipline: Psychiatry
A 23-year-old female presents to the emergency department with cuts she made with a razor blade to her left forearm and right hip. She states her boyfriend broke up with her recently and she has been feeling very lonely and empty. She reports frequent cutting with a razor blade on herself when feeling this way in the past. She lives with a roommate but reports constant fighting with her. The most likely diagnosis is

1. bipolar disorder type II
2. borderline personality disorder
3. dysthymic disorder
4. major depressive disorder
5. schizoid personality disorder

The correct answer is: B
This description is that of a patient with borderline personality disorder. These patients have a chronic pattern of unstable mood and relationships, with friends, family and in romantic relationships. They are often impulsive and attempt suicide, and have feelings of emptiness.

Answer A: The diagnosis of bipolar disorder type II requires fluctuating moods between hypomania and depression.


Answer C: Dysthymic disorder presents with depressive symptoms that have been ongoing for two years without meeting criteria for major depressive disorder.


Answer D: The criteria for making the diagnosis of major depressive disorder requires a minimum of two weeks of a minimum of five of the following symptoms: altered sleep or appetite, decreased interests, feelings of guilt, decreased energy, decreased concentration, suicidal ideation or psychomotor agitation.


Answer E: Schizoid personality disorder presents as a patient who is isolated, detached, and a "loner," with restricted emotional expressions, who is content and prefers being alone.




Bottom Line: Borderline personality disorder is diagnosed as pattern of unstable moods and relationships with frequent self-mutilation and suicide attempts with feelings of emptiness.

Link: "Chorionic villus sampling: Risks, complications, and techniques" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 38-year-old G1P0 female at 8 weeks' gestation is scheduled to undergo fetal karyotyping via chorionic villous sampling. Despite the recommended gestational age of 9 to 11 weeks for the procedure, the patient is adamant to have the testing done today. The most appropriate advice regarding risk to the fetus for performing the procedure at this time is

1. increased risk for limb abnormalities
2. increased risk for premature rupture of membranes
3. increased risk for preterm labor
4. no increased risk
5. significantly increased risk for fetal death

The correct answer is: A
Chorionic villous sampling (CVS) can be utilized sooner than amniocentesis to assess fetal karyotype and is typically performed between 9 and 11 weeks gestation. CVS involves transcervical or transabdominal aspiration of placental chorionic villi tissue. The advantage of using it is that it has a diagnostic accuracy comparable to that of amniocentesis while the disadvantage of it is that it carries a risk of limb reduction or, less commonly, fetal loss and it cannot detect open neural tube defects. Amniocentesis is performed at 15-20 weeks, and it consists of transabdominal aspiration of amniotic fluid using ultrasound guided needle evaluation of fetal cells for genetic studies.

Answer B: Although PROM is a potential complication of the procedure, performing CVS prior to 9 weeks gestation specifically places the fetus at in increased risk for limb abnormalities. PROM presents with a sudden gush of fluid from the vagina. It can be diagnosed using the fern or nitrazine tests.


Answer C: Although preterm labor is a potential complication of the procedure, performing CVS prior to 9 weeks gestation specifically places the fetus at in increased risk for limb abnormalities. This is thought to be due to vascular interruption that occurs during the procedure. Fetal fibronectin can be used to predict the probability of preterm labor.


Answer D: The patient is at an increased risk by having the procedure done and increased risk for having it done early.


Answer E: Although fetal death is a drastic potential complication of the procedure, performing CVS prior to 9 weeks gestation specifically places the fetus at in increased risk for limb abnormalities.




Bottom Line: Patients who undergo CVS prior to 9 weeks gestation are at most increased risk for the risk of fetal limb abnormalities.

COMBANK Insight : Amniocentesis, chorionic villous sampling, PUBS, and fluorescent in-situ hybridization should be reviewed prior to test day. It is not uncommon for these topics to appear in a matching section or in other stand-alone formats. Each test is different and you should understand when to use them and under which scenario. Questions dealing with complications of these procedures, such as in the above case, are also commonly seen.

For more information, see:
"Chorionic villus sampling: Risks, complications, and techniques" on UpToDate.
