Link: "Prevention of recurrent variceal hemorrhage in patients with cirrhosis" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Bleeding
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 67-year-old male presents to the emergency department with a two-hour history of bloody emesis. History reveals the episodes began spontaneously this morning. After the episodes, the patient felt dizzy and stumbled but denies falling or losing consciousness. He denies any previous history of hematemesis. He has a history of hepatitis C virus related cirrhosis and is currently on the liver transplantation list. Vitals reveal a blood pressure of 76/40 mmHg, a heart rate of 115/min, a temperature of 37.1°C, and a respiratory rate of 15/min. Physical examination reveals the patient's skin is yellow in color and his sclerae are icteric. There is shifting abdominal dullness, and increased deep tendon reflexes bilaterally. The patient is admitted to the intensive care unit, volume repleted, given 2 units of fresh frozen plasma, and started on intravenous ceftriaxone and octreotide. An emergent esophagogastroduodenoscopy (EGD) is performed and reveals actively bleeding esophageal varices. Which of the following pharmacologic agents is indicated in this patient for prevention of recurrence?

1. alpha 1 blockade
2. alpha 2 blockade
3. beta 1 blockade
4. both alpha and beta blockade
5. nonselective beta blockade

The correct answer is: E
This patient has actively bleeding gastroesophageal varices, which is one of the most significant complications of portal hypertension. Cirrhosis is the most common cause of portal hypertension, as seen in the patient who has cirrhosis secondary to his chronic hepatitis C virus infection. Portal hypertension is the result of splanchnic vasodilation leading to increased splanchnic blood flow in combination with an increased resistance to blood flow secondary to the intrahepatic fibrosis and endothelial dysfunction present in a cirrhotic liver. The increase pressure gradient distends the veins proximal to the site of obstruction, which leads to the gastroesophageal varices.

The lifetime risk in a cirrhotic for a variceal bleed is approximately 30% with an associated mortality risk of 30% to 50%, making prophylaxis essential. Current guidelines recommend that all patients with a diagnosis of cirrhosis undergo endoscopic screening for gastroesophageal varices. Prophylaxis should be initiated if large varices are noted and has been shown to decrease the risk of an esophageal bleed by 30% to 40%. Nonselective beta blockers, such as propranolol or nadolol, are the drugs of choice for prophylaxis of variceal bleeding. Nonselective beta-blockers block adrenergic dilatory tone in mesenteric arterioles which results in unopposed alpha-adrenergic mediated vasoconstriction and therefore a decrease in portal inflow. In patients, such as this one, who has already had a variceal bleed, esophageal band ligation is recommended in addition to a nonselective beta blockade.

Answer A: Alpha 1 blockers include medications such as alfuzosin, prazosin, doxazosin, and tamsulosin. This class of drugs is used to treat hypertension and benign prostatic hyperplasia.


Answer B: Alpha 2 blockers such as mirtazapine are used for depression. Known side effects of the drug are sedation, increased appetite, and increased serum cholesterol.


Answer C: Beta 1 blockers such as atenolol are mainly used for the treatment of hypertension and hypertrophic cardiomyopathy. This medication decreases heart rate and preload. In contrast to nonselective beta-blockers, the medication does not cross the blood-brain barrier and does not produce neurologic side effects.


Answer D: Both alpha and beta blockade is accomplished with drugs such as labetalol. They are used for treatment of pregnancy-induced hypertension as well as chronic hypertension secondary to pheochromocytoma.




Bottom Line: Nonselective beta-blockers, such as propranolol and nadolol, are the preferred pharmacologic agents for prophylaxis against variceal hemorrhage. In patients who have already had a variceal bleed; prophylaxis included endoscopic esophageal band ligation in addition to a nonselective beta-blocker.

For more information, see:
"Prevention of recurrent variceal hemorrhage in patients with cirrhosis" on UpToDate.

Link: "Weight gain and loss in pregnancy" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 32-year-old G1P0 female presents to the office for her first prenatal visit. History reveals she exercises daily and maintains a healthy diet. It is determined that her BMI is 22.3. She is concerned about the appropriate weight gain throughout her pregnancy. The most appropriate advice for this patient is

1. she should gain 11-20 pounds during her pregnancy
2. she should gain 15-25 pounds during her pregnancy
3. she should gain 25-35 pounds during her pregnancy
4. she should gain more than 35 pounds during her pregnancy
5. she should lose weight during her pregnancy

The correct answer is: C
Current guidelines for changes in weight during the course of pregnancy are based on pre-pregnancy body mass index. An increase of 100-300 kcal/day is recommended during the course of pregnancy while an increase of 500 kcal/day is recommended during breast-feeding in the postpartum period. Excessive weight gain is greater than 1.5 kg/month while inadequate weight gain is less than 1 kg/month. Guidelines for weight gain according to BMI are divided into four categories: underweight (BMI <19.8), acceptable (BMI 19.8-26.0), overweight (BMI 26.1-29.0) and severely overweight (BMI > 29.0). The recommended weight gain for underweight patients is greater than 35 pounds, 25-35 pounds for patients in the acceptable category, 15-25 pounds for overweight patients and 11-20 pounds for severely overweight patients.

Answer A: Severely overweight patients should gain 11-20 pounds during pregnancy.


Answer B: Overweight patients should gain 15-25 pounds during pregnancy.


Answer D: Underweight patients should gain more than 35 pounds during pregnancy.


Answer E: Patients should not be advised to lose weight during their pregnancy.




Bottom Line: Guidelines for weight gain according to BMI are divided into four categories: underweight (BMI <19.8), acceptable (BMI 19.8-26.0), overweight (BMI 26.1-29.0) and severely overweight (BMI > 29.0). The recommended weight gain for underweight patients is greater than 35 pounds, 25-35 pounds for patients in the acceptable category, 15-25 pounds for overweight patients and 11-20 pounds for severely overweight patients.

For more information, see:
"Weight gain and loss in pregnancy" on UpToDate.

Link: "Infective Endocarditis"


Patient Presentation: Fever
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 78-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of fever, fatigue and weight loss. Past medical history reveals hypertension, diabetes and aortic stenosis. Past surgical history is positive for a cholecystectomy and aortic valve replacement. Vitals reveal he is hypertensive, tachycardic, and febrile. Physical examination reveals a blowing diastolic murmur at the right second intercostal space, subungual petechiae and small tender nodules on the third and fourth fingers on the left hand. Blood cultures are positive for gram positive cocci that are catalase positive and coagulase negative. The most likely organism causing this patient’s condition is

1. Staphylococcus aureus
2. Staphylococcus epidermidis
3. Streptococcus bovis
4. Streptococcus viridans
5. Candida albicans

The correct answer is: B
The most likely diagnosis is subacute infective endocarditis. Subacute endocarditis occurs in patients that have underlying valve abnormalities. This diagnosis can be made by the following Duke criteria. Major criteria include positive blood cultures growing common organisms, positive echocardiogram or onset of new heart murmur. Minor criteria include the presence of a predisposing valve abnormality, fever, embolic disease, immunologic phenomena such as roth spots, osler nodes, and janeway lesions, or one positive blood culture with a rare organism. In order to make the diagnosis two major criteria, or one major and three minor, or five minor criteria need to be apparent. The most common etiology for subacute endocarditis includes Streptococcus viridans and Staphlococcus epidermidis. Of these Staphlococcus epidermidis is a gram positive, catalase positive, and coagulase negative organism that can cause prosthetic valve endocarditis.

Answer A: Staphlococcus aureus is the most common cause of acute bacterial endocarditis. This is a gram positive, catalase positive, and coagulase positive organism.


Answer C: Streptococcus bovis is commonly associated with GI malignancies such as colon cancer. This is a gram positive and catalase negative organism.


Answer D: Streptococcus viridans is most commonly found in patients with left-sided subacute bacterial endocarditis. These organisms are gram positive, and catalase negative.


Answer E: Candida albicans accounts for most cases of infective endocarditis in patients with long-term indwelling catheters, malignancy, AIDs, or organ transplantation.




Bottom Line: Staphlococcus epidermidis is catalase positive and coagulase negative and can cause prosthetic valve (subacute) endocarditis.

For more information, see:
"Infective Endocarditis"

Link: "Personality disorders" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Psychiatry
Discipline: Psychiatry
A 23-year-old female tells you that she lives alone and does not have many friends. Based on your discussions, it is apparent the patient isolates herself and participates in activities that require her to be alone. She does not express feelings of inadequacy or fear of ridicule. Based on this information, you suspect the patient is suffering from what personality disorder?

1. Avoidant
2. Borderline
3. Dependant
4. Schizoid
5. Schizotypal

The correct answer is: D
The patient is demonstrating behavior most consistent with schizoid personality disorder, which presents with lack of interest in social relationships, the tendency towards a solitary lifestyle, secretiveness, and emotional coldness toward family and friends. This type of personality disorder is uncommon in clinical settings. A person with this disorder is markedly detached from others and has little desire for close relationships. This person's life is marked by little pleasure in activities. People with this disorder appear indifferent to the praise or criticism of others and often seem cold or aloof.

Answer A: Avoidant personality disorder also shares some of these features; however, patients also demonstrate feelings of inadequacy and are in constant fear of being ridiculed by peers or other members of society. Unlike people with schizoid personality disorder, those with avoidant personality disorder desperately want to have friends.


Answer B: Patients with borderline personalities demonstrate extreme "black and white" thinking, known as “splitting” and have instability in mood, interpersonal relationships, and sense of self. Borderline pathology in children refers to a syndrome characterized by a combination of externalizing symptoms (disruptive behavioral problems), internalizing symptoms (mood and anxiety symptoms), and cognitive symptoms.


Answer C: There is no sign of dependence on other people.


Answer E: Schizotypal personality disorder is associated with “weird” behavior characterized by unconventional beliefs such as being convinced of having extra sensory abilities. The above patient is not demonstrating any signs of “odd” or “strange” behavior. People with this disorder exhibit marked eccentricities of thought, perception, and behavior.




Bottom Line: Patients suffering from schizoid personality disorder are “loners” contented with being alone. They differ from avoidant and schizotypal personality-types in that they do not feel inadequate or insecure and have no signs of “weird” thoughts or behavior.

COMBANK Insight : Personality disorders commonly show up on COMLEX exams. If you are able to recognize the main feature of each disorder, you will be able to get the majority of questions correct.

For more information, see:
"Personality disorders" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 68-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of abdominal pain for 5 days' duration. History reveals her last bowel movement was 3 days ago and she is experiencing nausea and vomiting. She denies fever or chills. Past surgical history is significant for 2 cesarean sections and a laparoscopic cholecystectomy. Physical examination reveals normal vital signs with a diffusely tender abdomen, decreased bowel sounds and a non-reducible, mid-line abdominal mass above the umbilicus. Laboratory analysis is unremarkable. The most likely diagnosis is

1. incarcerated hernia
2. incisional hernia
3. paraesophageal hernia
4. strangulated hernia
5. umbilical hernia

The correct answer is: A
The patient is presenting with symptoms of a small bowel obstruction secondary to an incarcerated ventral hernia. Ventral hernias are found in the midline of the abdomen and occur when a defect in the transversus abdominal fascia occurs allowing a hole through which bowel herniates. If the loop of bowel becomes trapped within the defect and cannot be reduced, it is termed an "incarcerated" hernia. If the blood supply to an incarcerated portion of bowel becomes compromised, it is termed a "strangulated" hernia which requires emergent operative reduction and repair of the defect with possible resection of the involved bowel.

Answer B: An incisional hernia occurs when a portion of bowel herniates through a previous surgical scar. While this patient's hernia could have resulted from scars left by prior surgeries, the hernia is clearly not reducible and thus the best answer choice is incarcerated hernia.


Answer C: A paraesophageal hernia is a type of hiatal hernia which occurs when the fundus of the stomach herniates through the diaphragm. This type of hernia is not palpated in the abdominal midline.


Answer D: A strangulated hernia occurs when the blood supply to the portion of trapped bowel becomes compromised causing necrosis of the bowel. Patients generally present very ill with fever and chills as well as laboratory abnormalities such as lactic acidosis and electrolyte derangements due to the necrotic bowel. This requires emergent surgical intervention with possible bowel resection if necrosis is clearly present.



Answer E: An umbilical hernia is a form of midline hernia where bowel herniates through the umbilicus, whereas this patient presents with a hernia superior to the umbilicus. Umbilical hernias can be present normally in children up to 2 years of age after which most spontaneously reduce.




Bottom Line: Incarcerated hernias consist of a loop of non-reducible bowel which has an intact blood supply and patients often have normal vitals and labs, unlike those with a strangulated hernia.

33 of 200

Link: Lichen Planus on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 53-year-old woman presents with a pruritic rash on her wrists and both legs.  History reveals the rash started on the wrists about 6 months ago and progressed to the legs over the past few months. She denies any new medications, lotions, or perfumes and has not had a recent illness. She has tried to use various over the counter creams with minimal improvement in the pruritus, but the rash continues to progress. She has a history of type 2 diabetes mellitus, diabetic retinopathy, chronic foot ulcer, hypertension, hyperlipidemia, and obesity. Physical examination reveals multiple violaceous, flat-topped papules scattered on wrists and distal legs. The oral buccal mucosa also revealed white lines in a lace like pattern of which the patient was unaware.  The most likely diagnosis is

1. basal cell carcinoma
2. Kaposi sarcoma
3. lichen planus
4. mycosis fungoides
5. psoriasis

The correct answer is: C
Lichen planus is an idiopathic inflammatory disease of skin and mucous membranes that most often affects adults between 30-60 years of age. The classic presentation is a pruritic, flat-topped papular eruption characterized by its violaceous color, polygonal shape, and overlying white fine lacelike lines called Wickham's striae. Lichen planus is most commonly found on the wrists, lower legs, flexor forearms, presacral, genitalia, and oral buccal mucosa. Oral lichen planus is often asymptomatic, as seen in this case, and presents as fine, white, lacelike lines again referred to as Wickham's striae.

Answer A: Basal cell carcinoma (BCC) is the most common skin cancer, yet it accounts for less than 0.1% of patient deaths due to cancer. Patients presenting with BCC often report a slowly enlarging lesion that does not heal and that bleeds intermittently. As tumors most commonly occur on the face, patients often give a history of an acne bump that occasionally bleeds. Consider BCC in any patient with a history of a sore on sun-exposed skin that does not heal, especially if it has central ulceration with rolled edges.


Answer B: AIDS-related Kaposi sarcoma carries a variable clinical course ranging from minimal mucocutaneous disease to widespread organ involvement. The lesions may involve the skin, oral mucosa, lymph nodes, and visceral organs. Most patients present with cutaneous findings that are classically red to purple macules, papules, and nodules.


Answer D: Mycosis fungoides (MF) is the most common type of cutaneous T-cell lymphoma (50%), which has led some authors to use this term synonymously with cutaneous T-cell lymphoma. MF is a malignant lymphoma characterized by the expansion of a clone of CD4+ (or helper) memory T cells that normally patrol and home to the skin. Various stages of MF include patch, plaque, or tumoral. The patch phase is characterized by flat, erythematous patches that may have a fine scale and may be pruritic. The plaques tend to be raised with fine scale. Annular or serpiginous patterns with central clearing are common. The tumoral stage may present with non-tender, erythematous to brown nodules or tumors.


Answer E: Psoriasis is a chronic, non-contagious, inflammatory disorder. Many patients with psoriasis have a familial predisposition for the illness, which most commonly manifests itself on the skin of the elbows, knees, scalp, lumbosacral areas, and intergluteal cleft. Classically, psoriasis appears as well demarcated erythematous plaques with thick silvery scale. The joints can also be affected by psoriasis in up to 35% of patients with the disease.




Bottom Line: Lichen planus classically presents as flat-topped, purpuric, polygonal, pruritic papules on the wrists and flexor extremities.

For more information, see:
Lichen Planus on Medscape

Link: "Kienbock Disease" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 39-year-old male who is employed as a carpenter presents to the outpatient office for evaluation of left wrist pain and reduced grip strength. History reveals that the patient is left hand dominant with pain over the dorsum of the wrist. There is no report of paresthesias into the fingers and the patient states, "I don't remember my hand ever falling asleep." The patient cannot recall any recent trauma to the affected wrist although he does subject his hand to repetitive motions on an almost daily basis. Evaluation reveals tenderness on palpation over the dorsal wrist. There is no pain with examination of the metacarpals or phalanges. Range of motion testing is significant for reduced extension and flexion at the wrist joint. Finkelstein test, Allen test and Watson test are found to be negative. Plain radiographs obtained in the office demonstrate degenerative changes around the lunate. The most likely cause of this patient's presentation is

1. a Bennett fracture
2. a Jones' fracture
3. de Quervain's tenosynovitis
4. Kienbock disease
5. Panner's disease

The correct answer is: D
Avascular necrosis of the lunate is referred to as Kienbock disease. The pathophysiology of this disorder is poorly understood and treatment is often guided by radiographic classification of disease severity. Pain over the dorsum of the wrist that inhibits the range of motion, wrist flexion/extension, with radiographic evidence of lunate degeneration or arthritis is most consistent with Kienbock disease. Severity of degeneration, as demonstrated on radiographic imaging helps to determine the appropriate treatment approach.

Answer A: A fracture involving the base of the first metacarpal extending into the intra-articular space. The patient in this case is not demonstrating pain or symptoms consistent with this diagnosis.


Answer B: A stress fracture involving the base or proximal third of the fifth metatarsal.


Answer C: Entrapment tendinitis of the tendons contained within the first dorsal compartment at the wrist. Pain is elicited with the Finkelstein test, which was negative for this patient. The absence of thumb or dorsal pain along the extensor surface of the radial aspect of the wrist makes this diagnosis less probable.


Answer E: Also known as osteochondritis of the capitellum, Panner's disease is frequently seen in older children and younger adolescents (7-12 years of age) and is commonly related to overuse of the arm that can occur with throwing or weight lifting.




Bottom Line: Kienbock disease is avascular necrosis of the lunate.

For more information, see:
"Kienbock Disease" on Medscape

Link: "Febrile Seizures" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 3-year-old male presents to the emergency department by ambulance with his mother who reports the patient had a seizure lasting one minute. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 40 ºC (104 ºF), a blood pressure of 110/65 mmHg, a heart rate of 150/min, and a respiratory rate of 30/min. Physical examination reveals an alert and oriented, well-developed male in no distress. There is clear nasal discharge and watery, clear eye discharge bilaterally. The tympanic membranes are visualized and are normal in appearance. Neurologic examination is unremarkable and Brudzinski and Kernig signs are negative. The most appropriate initial management is

1. obtain a brain CT without contrast
2. obtain blood cultures
3. perform lumbar puncture
4. provide supportive care including antipyretics
5. start empiric broad spectrum antibiotics

The correct answer is: D
Simple febrile seizures are common in children ages 6 months to 5 years. They occur with rapid rise of temperature during a febrile illness. Simple febrile seizures are defined as lasting less than fifteen minutes, generalized (generalized clonic or generalized tonic-clonic), and occurring only once in a 24-hour time period. This patient is the appropriate age for a simple febrile seizure. A postictal state is common but should resolve as it did in this scenario. Fever should be reduced with antipyretics and a child > 18 months of age can be safely discharged from the emergency department with no further workup and supportive care only. Parental reassurance is necessary, as seizures are a frightening event. Children with simple febrile seizures have a 30% chance of having another febrile seizure. A simple febrile seizure increases the chance of the patient developing epilepsy later in life from 1% (general population) to 2%.

Answer A: This patient had a simple febrile seizure and further laboratory or radiographic workup is not recommended in children over 18-months-old. Neuroimaging should be reserved for children with complex febrile seizures, defined as a seizure occurring in the setting of a febrile illness that lasts over fifteen minutes, is focal, or occurs more than once in a 24-hour period. In addition to brain CT, patients with complex febrile seizures require further workup including laboratory testing and lumbar puncture.


Answer B: Blood cultures are unlikely to be of any benefit in a patient with a simple febrile seizure and are not recommended. If there is concern for fever source or other indications for a sepsis workup, further testing is warranted. In this case however, the patient is likely febrile from acute viral syndrome and is the appropriate age for a simple febrile seizure.


Answer C: In simple febrile seizures, lumbar puncture is strongly recommended for patients < 12-months-old and should be considered in those 12 to 18-months-old. In this age group, children may lack classic physical exam findings for CNS infection. Otherwise, CNS infection should only be investigated with lumbar puncture in a child if there are signs or symptoms such as altered mental status, meningismus, bulging anterior fontanelle, or abnormal finding on neurologic examination. This patient is 3-years old and has no meningeal signs and a normal neurologic examination, therefore a lumbar puncture is not recommended.


Answer E: Empiric broad-spectrum antibiotics are indicated when there is a suspicion of bacteremia and sepsis and if there is a source of infection. This patient has signs of a viral infection. In addition, he is mentating normally and has no signs of neurologic infection. Tachycardia is common in fever, and this patient has a normal blood pressure for his age. The tachycardia should resolve with defervescence and further workup is warranted if it does not. Therefore, initiation of broad-spectrum antibiotics would not be beneficial in the initial course of this patient’s presentation.




Bottom Line: Simple febrile seizures are common in children ages 6 months to 5 years, initial management includes antipyretics and supportive care.

For more information, see:
"Febrile Seizures" on Medscape.

Link: "Diuretic-induced hyperuricemia and gout" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 58-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of toe pain. History reveals redness, swelling, and pain in the right great toe that began a few days ago, she denies any trauma. Past medical history reveals hypertension for which she was recently started on hydrochlorothiazide. Physical examination reveals an edematous, erythematous right great toe that is tender to palpation and has limited range of motion due to pain. Thiazide diuretics have been shown to cause the following electrolyte effects

1. Hypocalcemia, hyponatremia, hypokalemia
2. Hypocalcemia, hyponatremia, hyperkalemia
3. Hypercalcemia, hyponatremia, hyperkalemia
4. Hypercalcemia, hyponatremia, hypokalemia
5. Hypercalcemia, hypernatremia, hypokalemia

The correct answer is: D
This patient is suffering from an acute gout attack likely precipitated from initiation of hydrochlorothiazide. Thiazide diuretics have been shown to cause several metabolic effects including hyperglycemia, hypercalcemia, hyponatremia, and hypokalemia. Their use can also can result in metabolic syndrome, which is defined as hypertension, dyslipidemia, and obesity. Thiazides are known to precipitate gout attacks due to increased reabsorption of urate and decreased excretion of urate resulting in hyperuricemia. Loop diuretics are also known to cause hyperuricemia and can precipitate gout flares.

Answer A: Thiazides cause hypercalcemia, not hypocalcemia by increasing the reabsorption of calcium in the proximal tubule. Loop diuretics can lead to hypocalcemia.


Answer B: Thiazides cause hypercalcemia, not hypocalcemia by increasing the reabsorption of calcium in the proximal tubule. Loop diuretics can lead to hypocalcemia. They also cause hypokalemia, not hyperkalemia. Thiazides cause increased cellular uptake of sodium in the collecting ducts in response to more sodium delivery, resulting in Na/K exchange and subsequent potassium loss.


Answer C: Thiazides cause hypokalemia, not hyperkalemia. Thiazides cause increased cellular uptake of sodium, resulting in Na/K exchange and subsequent potassium loss.


Answer E: Thiazides cause hyponatremia, not hypernatremia. Thiazides decrease blood pressure by blocking the reabsorption of sodium and chloride ions resulting in decreased sodium levels.




Bottom Line: Thiazide diuretics precipitate gouty attacks by causing hyperuricemia. They can cause hypercalcemia, hyponatremia and hypokalemia, as well as additional unwanted side effects.

COMBANK Insight : Be familiar with the major side effects of the cardiovascular drugs as you will most likely see a question asking what the side effects are or what drug is associated with the following side effects.

For more information, see:
"Diuretic-induced hyperuricemia and gout" on UpToDate.

Link: "Pediatric Contact Dermatitis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A healthy 10-year-old male presents to the summer camp clinic with a pruritic rash. Physical examination reveals clear fluid-filled vesicles on his arm in a linear pattern. The most likely diagnosis is

1. Sporotrichosis
2. Rhus dermatitis
3. Herpes zoster
4. Tinea corporis
5. Bartonellosis

The correct answer is: B
Rhus dermatitis is a form of allergic contact dermatitis caused by plants (poison ivy, poison sumac, poison oak).  Urushiol is the allergenic substance in poison ivy.  Lesions follow the pattern of contact and typically develop as clear, fluid-filled papules and vesicles.  They are intensely pruritic and erythematous due to release of histamine.   Contact dermatitis is a type IV hypersensitivity reaction. Treatment involves avoidance of triggers, keeping the skin moist with lotions, topical steroids, and antihistamines for symptomatic relief.

Answer A: Sporotrichosis is an infection caused by the fungus Sporothrix schenckii. Inoculation of the fungus into the skin or subcutaneous tissue is common in people with exposure to soil containing the fungus. The lymphocutaneous form is the most common type that begins days to weeks after initial inoculation. First, a papule develops at the site, which may ulcerate and have overlying erythema. Drainage from the lesion can occur, but is not grossly purulent and usually odorless. Lesions similar in presentation can develop along lymphatic channels proximal to the original lesion. Culture is the gold standard for diagnosis with oral Itraconazole the treatment of choice for uncomplicated infections.


Answer C: Herpes zoster presents in a dermatomal pattern, which is not consistent with the rash described in the above scenario. Most people have a prodromal pain in the same area anywhere from days to weeks before the rash erupts. Pain is typically described as burning or stabbing pain, and can be quite severe. The rash begins as erythematous papules that progressively develop into grouped vesicles or bullae. Within a couple of days, these lesions can become more pustular or hemorrhagic in nature. Close monitoring should be applied for immunocompromised patients and those with lesions involving the eye. Anti-viral therapy started within the first 48-72 hours of onset of rash has demonstrated benefit in lessening the severity and duration of pain, promoting more rapid healing of skin lesions, preventing new lesion formation, decreasing viral shedding to reduce the risk of transmission, and preventing post-herpetic neuralgia.


Answer D: Tinea corporis, also known as ringworm, is a fungal (dermatophyte) infection that most commonly affects the torso. KOH preparation will demonstrate segmented hyphae and arthrospores, which are the typical findings of dermatophyte infections. Characteristic features include pruritic, circular, erythematous, patches that are scaling or plaque like. First line treatment is with a topical anti-fungal. While this patient is presenting with signs of a fungal infection, the quantity and location of lesions is unusual. Likewise, the KOH preparation is negative for hyphae, which excludes this diagnosis.


Answer E: Bartonellosis is an infection common associated with the microorganism Bartonella henselae, but can refer to other infections by the species in the Bartonella genus. The common microbe in the U.S. is typically transmitted through inoculation by an infected cat, thus the common name of Cat Scratch Fever. Typical manifestation is regional swollen lymph nodes with overlying erythema, which is occasionally suppurative. Disseminated disease is not common, but can be life threatening. Azithromycin is the common choice for treatment.




Bottom Line: Rhus dermatitis is allergic contact dermatitis typically due to the allergenic substance urushiol from poison ivy and related plants. It produces intensely pruritic and clear, fluid-filled vesicles in the pattern of exposure.

For more information, see:
"Pediatric Contact Dermatitis" on Medscape.
"Poison ivy (Toxicodendron) dermatitis" on UpToDate.

Link: Polymyalgia Rheumatica on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 58-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of shoulder pain. History reveals stiffness that starts in her neck and moves into her shoulders that is worse in the morning. She also reports weakness when arising from a chair. Physical examination reveals a fatigued appearing woman in no acute distress. There is diffuse tenderness to palpation in the neck and bilateral shoulder girdles. Upper extremity range of motion is limited due to pain. Diagnostic studies reveal a normal creatine kinase, negative rheumatoid factor, and an erythrocyte sedimentation rate of 58 mm/hr.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. cervical radiculopathy
2. fibromyalgia
3. impingement syndrome
4. polymyalgia rheumatica
5. polymyositis

The correct answer is: D
Polymyalgia rheumatica is a chronic inflammatory condition-affecting individuals usually over the age of 50. It classically presents with pain and stiffness in the neck, shoulder girdle, and/or hips. Because of the pain in the shoulders and/or hips, patients will often complain of difficulty combing their hair and/or rising from a chair. Systemic symptoms such as malaise, fatigue, depression, and fever may occur in as many as 50% of patients. Common laboratory findings include a normocytic, normochromic anemia, a normal creatine kinase, an elevated erythrocyte sedimentation rate (often >40 mm/hr), and an elevated serum interleukin-6 which parallels the inflammatory activity. Autoantibody markers including antinuclear antibodies, rheumatoid factor, and anti-cyclic citrullinated peptide are often normal.

Answer A: Cervical radiculopathy would be more likely if the patient was complaining of radiating numbness, tingling, or electric-like pain from her neck to her shoulder or down into her arm. This patient also complains of pain and stiffness bilaterally which, although does not exclude radiculopathy, makes it less likely.


Answer B: Fibromyalgia is described as chronic widespread tenderness and pain and is most common in younger to middle-aged women. The typical syndrome consists of persistent widespread pain and tenderness of greater than three months' duration, stiffness, fatigue and sleep disturbances, and cognitive difficulties. Physical examination is typically normal with the exception of tenderness. It is thought to be a multifactorial disturbance in central pain processing. It is a diagnosis of exclusion. Laboratory testing is usually normal.


Answer C: Impingement syndrome is caused by a reduction of the subacromial space and impingement on the structures that pass through which include the supraspinatus, long head of the bicep tendon, and the subacromial bursa. Physical exam reveals reproduction of pain with the Neer and Hawkins impingement tests during which the tendons are compressed between the coracoacromial arch and the humeral head. The most common cause for impingement syndrome is anatomic variation in the coracoacromial arch. Fatigue from overuse and weakness may also contribute. Laboratory evaluation would be normal.


Answer E: Polymyositis is another chronic inflammatory disease, which can be easily confused with polymyalgia rheumatica. This disease, however, causes symmetric proximal muscle weakness, and the muscles are usually painless. Weakness usually develops over three to six months. Diagnostic studies reveal leukocytosis, elevated erythrocyte sedimentation rate, elevated C-reactive protein, positive rheumatoid factor (>50% of patients), and an elevated creatine kinase. The elevated creatine kinase and rheumatoid factor can help to distinguish this from polymyalgia rheumatica.




Bottom Line: Polymyalgia rheumatica presents in middle to older aged people with proximal muscle stiffness and pain.

For more information, see:

Fischer C, August E, Gonzalez-Mayo A, et al. Master the Boards USMLE Step 2 CK. New York, NY: Kaplan Publishing; 2011: 191

Polymyalgia Rheumatica on Medscape

Link: Polymyalgia Rheumatica on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 58-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of shoulder pain. History reveals stiffness that starts in her neck and moves into her shoulders that is worse in the morning. She also reports weakness when arising from a chair. Physical examination reveals a fatigued appearing woman in no acute distress. There is diffuse tenderness to palpation in the neck and bilateral shoulder girdles. Upper extremity range of motion is limited due to pain. Diagnostic studies reveal a normal creatine kinase, negative rheumatoid factor, and an erythrocyte sedimentation rate of 58 mm/hr.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most appropriate treatment at this time is

1. corticosteroid injections
2. hydrocodone
3. laminectomy
4. oral steroids
5. physical therapy

The correct answer is: D
Corticosteroids are the treatment of choice for polymyalgia rheumatica. It typically responds very well to even low dose steroids. The steroids do not, however, alter the natural course of polymyalgia rheumatica and relapses are common. Patients are typically started on prednisone 15 mg/day with a very slow, prolonged taper over the following months. If, however, there is no improvement within the first week, steroids may be increased and an alternate diagnosis should be suspected. Calcium and vitamin D should also be started when initiating corticosteroid treatment. Continued activity is recommended, as activity restriction is unnecessary. For patients who do not respond quickly with corticosteroid treatment, physical therapy may be initiated.

Answer A: Systemic steroids are needed to treat polymyalgia rheumatica. Corticosteroid injections are useful in the treatment of various other musculoskeletal conditions where only a local effect is needed such as impingement syndrome and osteoarthritis.


Answer B: Hydrocodone is a narcotic analgesic with significant abuse potential. It is not recommended here, as the symptoms tend to respond very well and very rapidly to systemic steroids.


Answer C: This patient has no evidence of radicular symptoms or neurologic involvement and thus has no indication for surgical intervention. Also, there is no mention of neurodiagnostic imaging in the question stem as would be required before a neurosurgical procedure would be planned.


Answer E: Although physical therapy may seem like an appealing choice, and can often be a beneficial adjunctive therapy for any neuromusculoskeletal disorder, systemic steroids is the treatment of choice and should not be delayed for initiation of physical therapy.




Bottom Line: Oral steroids are the first line treatment for polymyalgia rheumatica.

For more information, see:

Fischer C, August E, Gonzalez-Mayo A, et al. Master the Boards USMLE Step 2 CK. New York, NY: Kaplan Publishing; 2011: 191

Link: "Wegner Granulomatosis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 64-year-old male presents with the complaint of sinus congestion and headache for three days' duration. He also admits to a ten-pound weight loss and increased fatigue for three months' duration. History reveals he has been treated for sinusitis four times in the last year, and is being treated for hypertension and hyperlipidemia. His medications include simvastatin and lisinopril. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 135/88 mmHg, a heart rate of 94/min, and a temperature of 38.4°C (101.2°F). Physical examination is significant for a saddle nose deformity and palpable purpura of the lower extremities. Laboratory analysis reveals a positive c-ANCA. Which of the following is most likely to be seen?

1. eosinophilia
2. gummas in the liver
3. low serum C3
4. positive anti-glomerular basement membrane antibodies
5. segmental crescentic necrotizing glomerulonephritis

The correct answer is: E
The patient in the above scenario is most likely suffering from Wegener's granulomatosis, an autoimmune disorder leading to granulomas in the upper and lower airways as well as crescentic glomerulonephritis. It presents with constitutional symptoms such as fever, malaise and weight loss as well as upper airway symptoms including recurrent sinusitis and saddle nose deformities. It can also lead to lower airway symptoms such as hemoptysis. It is characterized by a positive c-ANCA.

Answer A: Eosinophilia is seen in Churg-Strauss, which is characterized by a triad of asthma, eosinophilia and necrotizing vasculitis. Like Wegener's granulomatosis, it also may cause sinusitis, but does not cause the saddle nose deformity characteristic of Wegener's.


Answer B: Gummas in the liver are seen in tertiary syphilis but may be found anywhere throughout the body. Tertiary syphilis presents with focal neurological deficits, tabes dorsalis, dementia, bone pain and cardiovascular events.


Answer C: Low C3 is seen in post-streptococcal glomerulonephritis. It presents as tea-colored urine and signs of kidney dysfunction following an upper respiratory infection with Group A Streptococcus. It does not cause a saddle nose deformity or positive c-ANCA.


Answer D: Positive anti-glomerular basement membrane antibodies are seen in Goodpasture syndrome. Goodpasture syndrome usually presents with lower airway symptoms, such as hemoptysis, and does not cause a saddle nose deformity.




Bottom Line: Wegner's granulomatosis presents with upper airway symptoms and necrotizing glomerulonephritis.

For more information, see:
"Wegner Granulomatosis" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 67-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of pain with breathing. Past medical history reveals metastatic breast cancer. A radiograph of the chest demonstrates several pathologic rib fractures that appear to be healed. Physical examination reveals the 2nd rib moves easily into exhalation bilaterally, but the right side does not move into its inhalation position. To aid in the patient’s breathing, osteopathic manipulative treatment should be aimed at treatment of the

1. Anterior scalene
2. Middle scalene
3. Pectoralis minor
4. Posterior scalene
5. Sternocleidomastoid

The correct answer is: D
This patient has an exhalation dysfunction of the right 2nd rib. Since somatic dysfunctions are named for their ease, a rib that moves easily into exhalation but not into inhalation will be diagnosed as an exhalation dysfunction. The posterior scalene aids in respiration and elevates the 2nd rib during forced inhalation. Directing muscle energy techniques to the posterior scalene will bring the 2nd rib into its inhalation restrictive barrier.

Answers A & B: The anterior and middle scalenes attach to the 1st rib. Treating these muscles with muscle energy would address an exhalation dysfunction of the 1st rib.


Answer C: The pectoralis minor muscles attach to ribs 3-5 and are effective in treating exhalation dysfunctions of those ribs.


Answer E: The sternocleidomastoid (SCM) is an accessory muscle of respiration. However, it attaches to the clavicle and manubrium and would not be effective in treating a rib dysfunction.




Bottom Line: The posterior scalene attaches to the 2nd rib and is utilized in muscle energy techniques to treat exhalation dysfunctions involving the second rib.

COMBANK Insight : It is extremely likely that you will face at least one or two questions dealing with rib dysfunctions. Know how to diagnose and treat these and also be familiar with the muscles involved, such as in the above case. The following rib/muscle association are important to commit to memory: Anterior Scalene: Rib 1, Middle Scalene: Rib 1, Posterior Scalene: Rib 2, Pectoralis Minor: Ribs 3-5, Serratus Anterior: Ribs 6-9, Latissimus Dorsi: Ribs 10-11, and Quadratus Lumborum: Rib 12.

Link: "Initial Evaluation and Management of the Burn Patient" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Surgery
A 30-year-old male presents to the emergency department with multiple burns. History reveals the patient is a firefighter and was trapped in a burning building while responding to a call. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 37°C (98.6°F), a blood pressure of 155/90 mmHg, a heart rate of 100/min, a respiratory rate of 26/min, an oxygen saturation of 88% on high-flow oxygen, and a weight of 100 kg. Physical examination reveals a confused male in respiratory distress with an estimated 50% total body surface area of partial and full thickness burns. There is soot throughout his mouth, singed nasal passages, and mild oropharyngeal edema. The correct amount of resuscitative fluids with 0.9% sodium chloride to administer to this patient in the first 8 hours is

1. 1,000 mL
2. 1,120 mL
3. 2,000 mL
4. 10,000 mL
5. 20,000 mL

The correct answer is: D
Intravenous fluids are incredibly important in resuscitation of burn patients. The appropriate fluids are 0.9% NaCl or Lactated Ringers. Fluid resuscitation should follow the Parkland formula, which is: 4 mL x %TBSA x weight (kg) = mL fluid over 24 hours. Note that the TBSA (total body surface area of burn) only includes 2nd degree (partial thickness) and 3rd degree (full thickness) burns. Once the total volume is calculated, half of this volume should be given in the first 8 hours, and the second half over the following 16 hours. In this calculation: 4 mL x 50 (%TBSA) x 100 kg= 20,000 mL. Divide by 2, and 10,000 mL (10 L) should be given in the first 8 hours. The Parkland formula determines how much fluid should be given in addition to maintenance fluids.

Answers A & C: See answer explanation for formula. 1 or 2 L is much too little fluid. This answer should not make intuitive sense, as this is a large burn, a large patient, and a small fluid volume.


Answer B: Using the maintenance fluid calculation for children, one can come up with 140 mL/hr x 8 hrs = 1120 mL. This is the 4:2:1 rule: 4 mL/kg for the first 10 kg, plus 2 mL/kg for the next 10 kg, plus 1 mL/kg for each remaining kg of a child’s weight. This formula estimates the appropriate maintenance fluids for children, not burn patients.


Answer E: This is the correct amount of total fluid to be given over the first 24 hours. Remember that ½ should be given over the first 8 hours and the second half over the remaining 16 hours.




Bottom Line: Fluid resuscitation in adult burns should follow the Parkland formula, which is: 4 mL x %TBSA x weight (kg) = mL fluid over 24 hours, half of which is given in the first 8 hours.

COMBANK Insight : On level 2-CE, the most critical knowledge when it comes to burns are 1) Airway management issues 2) Fluids per Parkland formula 3) Calculating % TBSA. If you know these three concepts, you will fly through any burn question.

For more information, see:
"Initial Evaluation and Management of the Burn Patient" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 65-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of burning epigastric pain at night. History reveals the patient has a history of gastroesophageal reflux disease and is noncompliant with lifestyle modifications. Physical examination reveals tenderness to palpation in the epigastric region without rebound or guarding. An endoscopy is performed as shown in the exhibit. The following finding will most likely be found on biopsy.

1. Adenocarcinoma
2. Columnar metaplasia
3. Inflammation
4. Squamous cell dysplasia
5. Squamous cell metaplasia

The correct answer is: B
This patient has long-standing gastroesophageal reflux disease (GERD) that has led to Barrett's esophagus. Barrett's esophagus is defined as columnar metaplasia of the squamous cells in the esophagus. The changes can be clearly seen on endoscopy, however a biopsy is necessary to confirm the diagnosis. Patients with Barrett's esophagus are at a higher risk for adenocarcinoma of the esophagus and should be treated with proton pump inhibitors as well as lifestyle modifications. Lifestyle modifications include avoiding foods that decrease the pressure of the lower esophageal sphincter such as caffeine, chocolate, and alcohol, as well as elevating the head of the bed at night.

Answer A: Barrett's esophagus is the precursor to adenocarcinoma, but is itself benign. This patient has no symptoms indicating adenocarcinoma of the esophagus, which would include difficulty swallowing, weight loss, and night sweats.


Answer C: Inflammation is seen in esophagitis, not Barrett's. Esophagitis may be due to swallowing a chemical irritant.


Answer D: Squamous cell dysplasia is the precursor to squamous cell carcinoma, which occurs in the upper esophagus usually due to smoking. Lower esophageal cancer due to longstanding GERD forms as adenocarcinoma.


Answer E: Metaplasia is the transformation of one type of differentiated cell into another type of differentiated cell. Since squamous cells are the normal type of cell that lines the esophagus, squamous metaplasia is impossible in the esophagus.




Bottom Line: Barrett's esophagus is columnar metaplasia of squamous cells. It is the benign precursor of adenocarcinoma.

UpToDate

Link:
"Polyarteritis nodosa" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 45-year-old Caucasian male presents to the office with the complaint of periumbilical pain for one week. History reveals headaches, weight loss, fevers, weakness, and malaise during this time. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 37.6ºC (99.7ºF), a blood pressure of 145/90 mmHg, and a heart rate of 86/min. Physical examination reveals an ill-appearing male who appears older than his stated age. The abdomen is diffusely tender to palpation without rebound or guarding. Angiogram of the abdomen reveals multiple aneurysms in the mesenteric and renal arteries. 
Question 1 of 3 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. renal artery stenosis
2. Crohn's disease
3. Churg-Strauss syndrome
4. polyarteritis nodosa
5. acute pancreatitis

The correct answer is: D
Polyarteritis nodosa (PAN) is a vasculitis of small and medium-sized arteries that most commonly involves the skin, joints, peripheral nerves, kidneys, and the gastrointestinal (GI) tract. Although there are many manifestations of PAN, it should be considered in all patients presenting with symptoms including fever, malaise, weight loss, and anorexia. Peripheral or central nervous symptoms, dermatologic findings (vascular purpura, subcutaneous nodules, and livedo reticularis), myalgias, and orchitis are also possible presenting signs of PAN. Approximately 20% of patients with classic PAN are positive for p-ANCA. According the American College of Rheumatology, the diagnosis of PAN can be made if the patient has 3 of the 10 following signs: 1. Weight loss greater than/equal to 4 kg 2. Livedo reticularis, which appears as a mottled, purple discoloration over the extremities or torso 3. Testicular pain in males (orchitis) 4. Myalgias or weakness 5. Elevated BUN and creatinine 6. Hepatitis-positive serology 7. Central or peripheral neuropathy 8. Diastolic blood pressure greater than 90 mm Hg 9. Arteriogram showing aneurysms in small or medium-sized vessels 10. Biopsy-positive tissue

Answer A: Renal artery stenosis (RAS) is typically due to atherosclerotic disease or fibromuscular dysplasia, and most often presents with accelerated or hard to control hypertension. Other features that may indicate RAS include: onset of hypertension at an age younger than 30 or older than 55, decreasing renal function with initiation of a renin-angiotensin blocker, an abdominal bruit and recurrent flash pulmonary edema. Presence of renal artery narrowing does not necessarily indicate clinical consequences. Imaging will reveal narrowing of the renal arteries.


Answer B: In cases of Crohn’s disease look for patients complaining of crampy abdominal pain that is relieved with defecation. Symptoms of Crohn's are typically chronic in nature, unlike in the above case. Furthermore, Crohn's would not explain many of the findings in this patient such as the aneurysms and fevers.


Answer C: Churg-Strauss syndrome is a vasculitis affecting small vessels that is associated with asthma and eosinophilia. The classic presentation is the triad of eosinophilia, granulomatous inflammation, and vasculitis. Laboratory studies may reveal a positive perinuclear antineutrophil cytoplasmic antibody (p-ANCA) targeting myeloperoxidase (MPO). Treatment is with corticosteroids and immunosuppressive agents.


Answer E: Acute pancreatitis is most commonly elicited by gallstones or alcohol ingestion. Severe upper abdominal pain, with radiation to the back, is one of the hallmark findings of acute pancreatitis, although nausea and vomiting are usually present as well.




Bottom Line: Polyarteritis nodosa is a vasculitis of small and medium-size vessels that presents with a vast array of signs/symptoms involving the skin, joints, peripheral nerves, kidneys, and gastrointestinal tract.

For more information, see:

"Polyarteritis nodosa" on Medscape

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 45-year-old Caucasian male presents to the office with the complaint of periumbilical pain for one week. History reveals headaches, weight loss, fevers, weakness, and malaise during this time. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 37.6ºC (99.7ºF), a blood pressure of 145/90 mmHg, and a heart rate of 86/min. Physical examination reveals an ill-appearing male who appears older than his stated age. The abdomen is diffusely tender to palpation without rebound or guarding. Angiogram of the abdomen reveals multiple aneurysms in the mesenteric and renal arteries. 
Question 2 of 3 in this set
The confirmatory test for this diagnosis is

1. erythrocyte sedimentation rate
2. ultrasound
3. tissue biopsy
4. circulating anti-neutrophil cytoplasmic antibodies (c-ANCA)
5. perinuclear anti-neutrophil cytoplasmic antibodies (p-ANCA)

The correct answer is: C
Tissue biopsy remains the confirmatory test for the diagnosis of polyarteritis nodosa (PAN). The biopsy sample should include medium-sized arteries. Positive findings include focal necrotizing arteritis within the vessel wall. There are no specific lab tests for diagnosing polyarteritis nodosa.

Answer A: The erythrocyte sedimentation rate (ESR) may be elevated in cases of polyarteritis nodosa. However, an elevated ESR indicates inflammation in general and is not a sensitive test.


Answer B: Ultrasound is not useful for diagnosis of this disease. CT angiography should be performed, as was done in the case, to evaluate for medium vessel aneurysms. Ultrasound will not give adequate information to identify these aneurysms.


Answer D: Circulating anti-neutrophil cytoplasmic antibodies (c-ANCA) are less commonly found in polyarteritis nodosa patients. C-ANCA is most commonly associated with Wegener's granulomatosis.


Answer E: Perinuclear antineutrophil cytoplasmic antibodies (p-ANCA) is more closely associated with microscopic polyangiitis, which has some overlap with PAN.




Bottom Line: Tissue biopsy is the confirmatory test for polyarteritis nodosa.

COMBANK Insight : Whether you’re taking COMLEX Level 2 CE or COMLEX Level 3, you will encounter numerous questions asking you to differentiate between screening and confirmatory diagnostic tests. These questions tend to be very common when dealing with GI disorders but pop up in other systems as well. For example, everyone knows that abdominal ultrasound is the best screening test for acute cholecystitis. Another favorite question asks the confirmatory test (or the definitive test) for pulmonary embolism, which is pulmonary angiography. You will be better off if you have a strong understanding of various screening and confirmatory tests and their clinical use.

For more information, see:
"Polyarteritis nodosa" on Medscape

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 45-year-old Caucasian male presents to the office with the complaint of periumbilical pain for one week. History reveals headaches, weight loss, fevers, weakness, and malaise during this time. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 37.6ºC (99.7ºF), a blood pressure of 145/90 mmHg, and a heart rate of 86/min. Physical examination reveals an ill-appearing male who appears older than his stated age. The abdomen is diffusely tender to palpation without rebound or guarding. Angiogram of the abdomen reveals multiple aneurysms in the mesenteric and renal arteries. 
Question 3 of 3 in this set
This patient's diagnosis is closely associated with

1. diabetes
2. celiac sprue
3. hemochromatosis
4. hepatitis B
5. Wilson's disease

The correct answer is: D
Polyarteritis nodosa (PAN) can be acquired as a complication of hepatitis B (HBV). HBV associated PAN can occur at any time during or after HBV infection, but it usually occurs within 6 months of infection. It is thought that hepatitis virus replication may injure the vessel walls, although the connection is not entirely understood.

Answer A: Diabetes is a disorder of either insulin production (type I) or insulin resistance (type II). It is not associated with PAN.


Answer B: Celiac sprue is a hypersensitivity to gluten which results in symptoms of malabsorption and is not associated with PAN.


Answer C: Hemochromatosis is a condition characterized by excessive absorption of dietary iron leading to cirrhosis, heart failure, and diabetes. It is not associated with PAN.


Answer E: Wilson's disease is a disorder of copper accumulation leading to liver disease and neurologic/psychiatric symptoms and is not associated with PAN.




Bottom Line: All patients diagnosed with polyarteritis nodosa should also be worked up for hepatitis.

Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 69-year-old male with a history of hypertension and diabetes presents to the office with the complaint of weakness for four months’ duration. History reveals difficulty ambulating and accomplishing tasks that are progressively worsening. The patient reports falling three times in the past week and yesterday, he developed difficulty swallowing. Physical examination reveals an elderly male in no acute distress. Neurological examination reveals decreased strength, stiffness, and hyperreflexia of the lower extremities bilaterally. The upper extremities are twitching with atrophy and decreased reflexes but sensation is normal. The most appropriate treatment is

1. doxycycline
2. intravenous immunoglobulin (IVIG)
3. prednisone
4. riluzole
5. rituximab

The correct answer is: D
In its classic form, amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS) affects motor neurons at two or more levels supplying multiple regions of the body. It affects lower motor neurons that reside in the anterior horn of the spinal cord and in the brain stem, corticospinal upper motor neurons that reside in the precentral gyrus, and prefrontal motor neurons that are involved in planning or orchestrating the work of the upper and lower motor neurons. Loss of lower motor neurons leads to progressive muscle weakness and wasting (atrophy). Loss of corticospinal upper motor neurons may produce stiffness (spasticity), abnormally active reflexes, and pathological reflexes. Loss of prefrontal neurons may result in special forms of cognitive impairment that include, most commonly, executive dysfunction. The classic form of sporadic ALS usually starts as dysfunction or weakness in one part of the body and spreads gradually within that part and then to the rest of the body. Ventilatory failure results in death, on average, three years after the onset of focal weakness. The only FDA approved medication for ALS is riluzole (brand name Rilutek). This medication has been proven to retard progression of the disease and increase the number of days to ventilator dependence. However, it only extends life expectancy by two to three months and is quite expensive. It should be prescribed on a case-by-case basis after careful discussion with the family and his or her family.

Answer A: Doxycycline and the other medications listed (among many others) have all been studied as possible treatments for ALS. None other than riluzole has shown to extend life expectancy. Doxycycline is a tetracycline antibiotic. It is frequently used in atypical pneumonia and Lyme disease.


Answer B: IVIG is thought to be effective in treatment of autoimmune diseases such as Guillain Barre Syndrome or myasthenia gravis. Use in ALS has not been shown to be effective.


Answer C: Prednisone is an anti-inflammatory medication and is used extensively in a number of other neurologic and medical diseases. Again though, it has not been proven to be effective in treatment of ALS.


Answer E: Rituximab is a monoclonal antibody against CD20 on B cells. It is an immune modulating medication. It is widely used in hematologic cancers, rheumatologic disorders and autoimmune diseases. Neurologically it is sometimes used in autoimmune encephalitis, and neuromyelitis optica. It is not been proven to work in ALS.




Bottom Line: Clinically definite ALS involves upper motor neuron (UMN) and lower motor neuron (LMN) signs in at least three body segments. Riluzole is the only FDA approved medication for ALS.

COMBANK Insight : ALS is a somewhat rare disease and you are unlikely to be questioned about it in detail. Make sure to review that basic presentation, testing and treatment, but do not get bogged down in particulars. Remember that exams will ask about typical presentation of rare diseases or atypical presentation of common diseases. 

For more information, see:
For more information, see: Amyotrophic Lateral Sclerosis on Medscape Updated: August 27, 2013


Last Updated: 9/26/2016

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 55-year-old African-American male presents to the office with the complaint of cough. History reveals the cough is productive of yellow sputum and has been present for four months' duration. One month ago he presented to the emergency department with the same symptoms and was treated with amoxicillin/clavulanic acid and azithromycin, with minimal relief. Last year, he had a similar productive cough for four months' duration. He states he has been treated for pneumonia five times over the past two years. He has a 60-pack-year smoking history. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 134/92 mmHg, a heart rate of 82/min, a temperature of 37.3ºC (99.2ºF), and a respiratory rate of 20/min. Physical examination reveals mild dyspnea, diffuse rhonchi, expiratory wheezing bilaterally, and retractions. He does not have digital clubbing. Examination of the lower extremities reveals 2+ pitting bilaterally. The most likely diagnosis is

1. asthma
2. atypical pneumonia
3. chronic bronchitis
4. emphysema
5. lung cancer

The correct answer is: C
The patient in this scenario has had a productive cough for at least three months per year in two consecutive years, which meets the criteria for chronic bronchitis. Chronic bronchitis is a form of chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), usually due to smoking. Symptoms are often minimal until the disease has advanced. Many patients have signs consistent with emphysema as well, but patients with isolate chronic bronchitis are classified as the classic “blue bloater.” They may present with cyanosis, mild dyspnea, a productive cough, obesity, peripheral edema, rhonchi, barrel chest, increased work of breathing/accessory muscle use, jugular venous distension, end-expiratory wheezing, and distant breath sounds. Patients may have signs of chronic hypercapnea (respiratory acidosis) and hypoxemia on blood gases. Radiographic imaging may show hyperinflation, decreased lung markings, and a thin mediastinum.

Answer A: Although this patient has expiratory wheezing, his history of productive cough is more consistent with chronic bronchitis than asthma. It is also unlikely for asthma to first present at his current age.


Answer B: Atypical pneumonia is usually treatable with antimicrobial agents such as azithromycin, which this patient has already completed.


Answer D: Emphysema is often found in conjunction with chronic bronchitis; however, this patient clearly meets clinical criteria for chronic bronchitis due to his history of cough productive of sputum.


Answer E: Although lung cancer is a possibility in this patient, he has no signs of systemic illness – fever, night sweats, or weight loss.




Bottom Line: Chronic bronchitis is characterized by a productive cough for at least three months per year in two consecutive years.

For more information, see:
"Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease" on Medscape.

Link: "Hypokalemia" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Consciousness Alterations
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 21-year-old female presents to the emergency department after a syncopal episode. History reveals that the patient has experienced several near-syncopal episodes over the past week associated with palpitations, blurred vision, and nausea. The patient denies past medical or surgical history and takes no medications. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 90/60 mmHg, a heart rate of 60/min, a respiratory rate of 18/min, an oxygen saturation of 94% on room air, and a temperature of 35°C (95°F). Physical examination reveals a thin, malnourished appearing female. The oropharynx is dry with angular cheilitis. Cardiovascular examination is without murmur, and a thorough neurologic examination is unremarkable except diffuse hyporeflexia. A bedside hemoglobin results at 13.1 g/dL. An ECG is obtained and shown in the exhibit. The most likely cause of this patient’s symptoms is

1. hypercalcemia
2. hyperkalemia
3. hypocalcemia
4. hypokalemia
5. preexcitation of an accessory conduction pathway

The correct answer is: D
This patient is most likely suffering from an eating disorder such as anorexia nervosa. In this case, the patient is very thin appearing, hypothermic, relatively hypotensive, and has angular cheilitis, all of which are indicative of malnutrition. In addition, the ECG shown is consistent with hypokalemia due to the U waves in leads V2-V5. U waves are additional upright waves that appear after T waves. In the workup of syncope or near syncope, an ECG is extremely important to screen for various arrhythmias and/or evidence of electrolyte abnormalities.  Hypokalemia can cause QT prolongation, U waves, T wave inversion, or ST segment flattening. In addition, it can affect the automaticity of the pacemaker cells in the heart leading to bradycardia, AV block or even torsades de pointes.

Answer A: Hypercalcemia is most commonly caused by malignancy and hyperparathyroidism, and is diagnosed at a serum level over 10.2 mg/dL. This is usually asymptomatic, but classically presents as bones (fractures), stones (kidney stones), groans (abdominal pain, constipation), and psychiatric overtones (weakness, confusion). Hypercalcemia causes a shortened QT interval on ECG.


Answer B: Hyperkalemia is defined as a serum K > 5 mEq/L. Most commonly caused by renal failure, rhabdomyolysis, acidosis, hypoaldosteronism, or ACE inhibitors, elevated potassium can cause nausea/vomiting, weakness, paresthesias, or be entirely asymptomatic. ECG findings include peaked T waves, wide QRS, PR prolongation, or ventricular fibrillation.


Answer C: Hypocalcemia in adults arises from malnutrition, pancreatitis, hypoparathyroidism, and pseudohypoparathyroidism. Levels are usually below 8.5 mg/dL. It can present as muscle cramps/spasms, perioral numbness, carpopedal spasm, and have exam findings such as a Chvostek or Trousseau sign. An ECG may show a prolonged QT interval, but should not show U waves unless a concurrent hypokalemia is occurring (which is common).


Answer E: Wolf-Parkinson-White syndrome is a congenital abnormality involving abnormal conduction between the atria and ventricles in association with supraventricular tachycardia (SVT). It occurs during preexcitation, where an atrial impulse is not conducted down the atrioventricular pathway, but down an accessory pathway, most commonly down the bundle of Kent. ECG findings most commonly include a short PR interval (<120 ms) and a wide QRS (>120 ms) with a slurred onset of the R-wave (known as delta wave). This ECG does not show any of these findings.




Bottom Line: Hypokalemia is a common electrolyte abnormality in malnourished patients such as those suffering from an eating disorder. The most common ECG findings in hypokalemia include QT prolongation, U waves, T wave inversion, and/or ST segment flattening.

For more information, see:
"Hypokalemia" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 30-year-old male presents to the emergency department complaining of substernal chest pain. History reveals a gradual onset of non-radiating, burning chest pain that has been constant for the past eight hours and began when he went to sleep. The patient denies diaphoresis, dizziness, or nausea. Past medical history reveals a knee injury two months prior for which his is taking over the counter pain medications. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 130/80 mmHg, a heart rate of 80/min, a respiratory rate of 14/min, an oxygen saturation of 97% on room air, and a temperature of 37°C (98.6°F). Physical examination is remarkable for tenderness to palpation in the epigastrum. An ECG is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most likely etiology of this patient’s symptoms is

1. acute coronary syndrome
2. esophageal perforation
3. esophageal tear
4. gastritis
5. pulmonary embolus

The correct answer is: D
This patient is most likely experiencing gastritis. A recent history of a musculoskeletal injury should lead you to suspect that the patient is taking non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs). Ibuprofen, aspirin, and naproxen are three common over the counter drugs that work as nonspecific cyclooxygenase inhibitors (COX) and create an increased acidic environment in the stomach. Over time, increased acidity in the stomach may create generalized inflammation of the stomach lining (gastritis), or focal ulcers. This patient has the onset of symptoms occur while going to sleep, which is common as the stomach contents may reflux into the esophagus in a supine position causing an increase in symptoms. The key to answering this question correctly and confidently is excluding more life threatening causes of chest pain by history, physical, and ECG findings.

Answer A: This patient has a normal ECG. This rules out ST-elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI), and regional ischemia. Importantly here, the patient complains of chest pain without associated symptoms (radiation, nausea, diaphoresis) and also objectively has epigastric tenderness. In addition, his age and lack of cardiac risk factors makes unstable angina and non-ST segment myocardial infarction (NSTEMI) extremely unlikely.


Answer B: Esophageal perforation, also known as Boerhaave's syndrome, typically presents as severe chest pain in the setting of forceful retching. Tearing of the esophagus will often cause hematemesis, and spillage of gastric contents into the mediastinum will cause mediastinitis. These patients are often critically ill appearing, febrile, tachycardic, and require urgent surgical consultation. Given the well appearance of this patient and an unremarkable exam, esophageal perforation is unlikely.


Answer C: An esophageal tear is commonly associated with hematemesis. This syndrome is known as Mallory-Weiss syndrome/tear. Typically, patients will complain of nonbloody vomiting followed by an episode of blood-tinged vomitus. This patient has no such complaints and therefore an esophageal tear is unlikely.


Answer E: The patient describes a recent knee “injury” 8 weeks ago. Any patient who is post-operative with chest pain should have pulmonary embolism (PE) in their differential. However, a sprain or contusion of the knee is low risk for development of a deep vein thrombosis (DVT) and is not considered equivalent to a patient with a total knee replacement/knee surgery. One can use the PE rule out criteria in this case (PERC rule) to exclude PE from the differential. If the patient has none of the following, PE is less than 1.8% likely to be the cause of symptoms: 1) Age > 50, 2) Heart rate > 100/min, 3) Hypoxemia (02 sat < 95%), 4) History of prior PE/DVT, 5) Recent surgery/trauma (past 6 weeks), 6) Hemoptysis, 7) Exogenous estrogen use, 8) Unilateral leg swelling. This patient has none of these criteria and therefore can have PE ruled out without use of D-dimer testing.




Bottom Line: Suspect gastritis in a patient with a recent musculoskeletal injury complaining of abdominal or chest pain, as NSAIDs are a common cause of these symptoms.

For more information, see:
"Acute Gastritis" on Medscape.
