Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 5-year-old male presents to the office with a rash on his face. The mother states she noticed a small cut on his nose a few days prior when she picked him up from soccer practice. Yesterday she noticed several red spots surrounding the cut on his nose with a few small pustules. This is the first time she has noticed a rash like this. Vitals reveal a blood pressure of 102/70 mmHg, heart rate of 88/min, a respiratory rate of 20/min, and a temperature of 37.1ºC (98.8ºF). Physical examination reveals a well appearing child in no distress. There are erythematous patches with superficial erosions and honey yellow crusts limited to the nares only.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
If this rash were to become bullous, what toxin would most likely be responsible?

1. Exfoliative toxin A
2. Pyrogenic toxin
3. Streptolysin O
4. Streptolysin S
5. Toxic shock syndrome toxin-1

The correct answer is: A
The most likely diagnosis is impetigo. Staphylococcus aureus is the most common organism causing impetigo, with Streptococcus pyogenes being the second most common. There are three types of impetigo, the first being the nonbullous form, the most common, making up nearly 70% of the cases. This type starts as papules and develops into vesicles, which break down forming a thick honey-crust appearance, which was originally seen in this patient. The second type is the bullous form. It results from strains of S. aureus that produce exfoliative toxin A. This exotoxin targets desmoglein and has pathophysiology similar to the pemphigus autoimmune disease. Fragile bullae develop filled with yellow fluid but are typically seen as an erythematous area surrounded by a rim once they burst. The least common form is ecthyma, which is the ulcerative type. Deep ulcers penetrate deep into the dermis and are covered with a yellow crust.

Answer B: Pyrogenic exotoxins are produced by Streptococcus pyogenes and are considered a superantigen, which are substances that initiate an uncontrolled proliferation of T-cells without going through macrophages for activation. This results in lymphocyte blastogenesis, possible endotoxin-induced shock, fever induction and suppression of antibody synthesis. This exotoxin has been associated with scarlet fever and streptococcal toxic shock syndrome.


Answer C: Streptolysin O is another virulence factor of Streptococcus pyogenes that is toxic to a wide variety of cell types, including the myocardium, and is highly immunogenic. Anti-streptolysin O (ASO) titer is a tool used to help differentiate whether a patient with acute renal failure or signs of rheumatic fever has been exposed to S. pyogenes as a cause for the problem.


Answer D: Streptolysin S is a virulence factor from Streptococcus pyogenes that is capable of damaging leukocytes and cellular organelles, but it does not appear to be immunogenic.


Answer E: Toxic shock syndrome toxin-1 is a superantigen produced by Staphylococcus aureus. This is the main toxin that is responsible for toxic shock syndrome. This toxin can activate the immune system by bypassing the normal sequence, resulting in the release of inflammatory cytokines. These cytokines cause capillary leakage and tissue damage, which leads to shock and multiorgan failure.




Bottom Line: Staphylococcus aureus is the most common organism causing impetigo. The most common type of this disease is the nonbullous form. However, the bullous form can be seen and is a result of the exfoliative toxin A.

For more information, see:
"Impetigo" on Medscape


Last Updated: 9/25/2016

Link: "Impetigo" on Medscape



Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 5-year-old male presents to the office with a rash on his face. The mother states she noticed a small cut on his nose a few days prior when she picked him up from soccer practice. Yesterday she noticed several red spots surrounding the cut on his nose with a few small pustules. This is the first time she has noticed a rash like this. Vitals reveal a blood pressure of 102/70 mmHg, heart rate of 88/min, a respiratory rate of 20/min, and a temperature of 37.1ºC (98.8ºF). Physical examination reveals a well appearing child in no distress. There are erythematous patches with superficial erosions and honey yellow crusts limited to the nares only.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
What is the most appropriate treatment?

1. Hydrogen peroxide
2. Intravenous fluids
3. Mupirocin ointment
4. Penicillin v
5. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole

The correct answer is: C
A topical antibiotic, such as mupirocin ointment, is the first-line treatment option for non-bullous impetigo. Topical medications are equally effective to oral antibiotics for localized impetigo and provide a treatment option with fewer adverse effects compared to an oral antibiotic.

Answer A: Gentle cleansing with diluted hydrogen peroxide, chlorhexidine, or diluted sodium hypochlorite (bleach) and removal of the honey-colored crusts of non-bullous impetigo may prevent the transmission of impetigo and prevent recurrences. However, the efficacy of hydrogen peroxide alone as the treatment for impetigo has not been proven.


Answer B: Intravenous fluids would be indicated in patients with extensive areas of involvement with bullous impetigo. Severe dehydration may arise in these patients as a result of ruptured bullae, leaving denuded skin which allows excessive water loss.


Answer D: Oral antibiotics would be indicated to treat impetigo that is widespread or associated with systemic symptoms, such as fever or diarrhea. Oral agents must provide coverage against both Staphylococcus aureus and Streptococcus pyogenes. Antibiotics typically used include beta-lactamase resistant penicillins (i.e. amoxicillin/clavulanate and dicloxacillin) or a first generation cephalosporin (i.e. cephalexin).


Answer E: Certain antibiotics like trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole, clindamycin, and vancomycin, should be reserved for treatment-resistant patients suspected of having impetigo due to methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus. Given this is the first incidence of impetigo in this patient and the disease is localized, suspicion for MRSA is low and a topical antibiotic is adequate treatment.




Bottom Line: A topical antibiotic, such as mupirocin ointment, is the first-line treatment option for non-bullous impetigo.

For more information, see:
"Impetigo" in Clinical Dermatology: A color guide to diagnosis and therapy. 5th ed.

Patient Presentation: Services Rendered
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 16-year-old male with a history of hemophilia presents to the office for approval to participate in high school sports. Review of systems is negative. Vital signs and physical examination are unremarkable. You clear him for participation and review the team’s first aid protocol for pain management. The most appropriate medication you recommend for pain management in this patient is

1. acetaminophen
2. diclofenac
3. enteric coated aspirin
4. ibuprofen
5. naproxen

The correct answer is: A
Acetaminophen provides pain relief and does not affect bleeding. Hemophilia A is an inherited, X-linked, recessive disorder caused by deficiency of factor VIII. The treatment of hemophilia may involve management of hemostasis, management of bleeding episodes, use of factor replacement products and medications, treatment of patients with factor inhibitors, and treatment and rehabilitation of patients with hemophilia synovitis.

Answers B & D & E: Diclofenac, naproxen, and ibuprofen are NSAIDs associated with gastrointestinal bleeding and should be avoided in patients with hemophilia.


Answer C: Aspirin is an NSAID that irreversibly affects platelet function and would increase the risk of bleeding.




Bottom Line: Acetaminophen is the preferred pain reliever for patients with hemophilia.

COMBANK Insight : Understand the common side-effects of over the counter pain relievers. Other questions that may arise on the COMLEX relate to avoidance of these medications in liver failure (acetaminophen) and kidney failure (NSAIDs).

Link: "Intrapartum fetal heart rate assessment" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 23-year-old G1 female at 38 weeks' gestation presents to the emergency department in labor. The fetal heart rate tracing is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most likely cause of these findings is

1. decreased oxygen delivery to the fetus
2. fetal head compression
3. maternal systemic lupus erythematosus
4. physiologic response to fetal movement
5. umbilical cord spasm

The correct answer is: B
The tracing demonstrates early decelerations. Early decelerations are U-shaped decreases in fetal heart rate that begin with the onset of a contraction, nadir with the peak of contraction, and return to baseline as the contraction subsides. This pattern may occur during active labor between 4-7 cm cervical dilation. As the fetus descends and the skull is compressed, a change in cerebral blood flow or dura stimulation activates the vagal nerve and causes a transient decrease in fetal heart rate. As the contraction subsides, the vagal stimulation is removed and the fetal heart rate returns to baseline.

Answer A: Decreased oxygen delivery to the fetus results in late decelerations. A late deceleration is a decrease in fetal heart rate that begins after the uterine contraction begins, decelerates to 30-40 beats per minute below baseline, and then slowly returns. In contrast to variable decelerations, late decelerations are not associated with accelerations that may occur before or after the heart rate change. The delay is likely due to the complex reflex changes. As hypoxia develops, oxygen sensors in the brain increase sympathetic activity, which raises blood pressure. Fetal baroreceptors respond to the increased pressure by decreasing fetal heart rate. There are a number of causes of uteroplacental insufficiency that result in decreased oxygen delivery, but the most common is excessive uterine contraction, often due to oxytocin administration.


Answer C: Maternal systemic lupus erythematosis can affect the placental vasculature, reducing oxygen delivery to the fetus, and cause late decelerations. SLE, along with other collagen vascular diseases, is cause for uteroplacental insufficiency.


Answer D: An acceleration is a physiological response to fetal movement and represents an active, well-oxygenated fetus.


Answer E: Umbilical cord spasm or compression results in variable decelerations. These are abrupt decreases in fetal heart with rapid return to baseline. They may be associated with accelerations in fetal heart rate before and after the decelerations. As the cord is compressed, the thinner umbilical vein is occluded first which leads to decreased cardiac return. There is a reflex tachycardia and acceleration on the fetal heart rate tracing. With further compression, the artery is occluded, systemic resistance and fetal blood pressure increases. A protective reflex bradycardia occurs and there is a deceleration on the heart rate tracing. As the compressive force is removed, the pattern is reversed.




Bottom Line: Fetal head compression causes early decelerations.

COMBANK Insight : Fetal heart rate tracings are likely to show up on the exam. Make sure you can recognize the different types of periodic changes (accelerations, early decels, late decels and variable decels), the underlying cause, and what to do when they occur.

For more information, see:
"Intrapartum fetal heart rate assessment" on UpToDate.

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 68-year-old Caucasian male arrives at his family medicine physician's office with right knee pain.  He states that the pain is throbbing and worse during the evening and tends to swell at the end of the day.  The patient is a retired carpenter with a history of hypertension and chronic kidney disease.  Physical examination reveals a slightly restricted passive range of motion on flexion with tenderness.  X-ray reveals right knee distal femur subchondral bone sclerosis.  The best initial management in addition to judicious exercise and reduction of weight on the painful joint is

1. acetaminophen
2. oral corticosteroids
3. NSAIDs
4. right knee arthroplasty
5. intra-articular hyaluronic acid injections

The correct answer is: A
This patient has osteoarthritis given his age, history of carpentry, and x-ray findings.  Symptoms include morning stiffness usually less than 15 minutes and pain and swelling at the end of the day.  Physical examination may show joint restriction, tenderness, and swelling without erythema.  The DIP joint is classically affected, and laboratory results are oftentimes normal.  X-rays will show osteophyte formation, thinning of the joint space, and subchondral bone sclerosis.  First-line treatment is with acetaminophen along with judicious exercise and reduction of weight on painful joints. NSAIDS would be used if acetaminophen were not effective.

Answer B: Systemic corticosteroids are not indicated for osteoarthritis.


Answer C: NSAIDS are indicated for osteoarthritis but would be contraindicated in patients with chronic kidney disease. They are second line after acetaminophen.


Answer D: Arthroplasty involves remodeling, replacing, or realigning the articular surface of a musculoskeletal joint. It is too early to consider this patient for this procedure.


Answer E: Intra-articular hyaluronic acid injections can be used for relief of osteoarthritis but are expensive and not first-line treatments.




Bottom Line: Osteoarthritis is associated with advanced age, and/or a history of prolonged strenuous exercise or work.  Diagnosis is with history and physical examination with possible x-ray of the affected joint.  First-line treatment is with acetaminophen. NSAIDS would be used if acetaminophen were not effective.

For more information, see:
Practical Guide to the Care of the Medical Patient. 7th Ed. Mosby Elsevier. Philadelphia, PA, pp. 370-373.


Last Updated: 10/3/2016
56 of 200

Link: "Crohn Disease" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 25-year-old female presents to the emergency department with a two-day history of intermittent abdominal pain and bloody diarrhea. She had three similar episodes in the past six months, for which she did not seek medical attention. She has lost 8 lbs. (3.6 kg) in this time period. She has no significant past medical history and is sexually active with one monogamous partner. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 120/80 mmHg, a heart rate of 90/min, and a temperature of 38.4ºC (101.1ºF). Physical examination reveals her abdomen is soft and non-distended with tenderness in the right lower quadrant. Pelvic examination is normal. A pregnancy test is obtained and is negative. Perianal examination discloses two-fistula orifices anterolateral to the anus with expression of white material with palpation. The most likely diagnosis is

1. appendiceal carcinoid tumor
2. Crohn's disease
3. Meckel's diverticulum
4. perforated appendicitis
5. ulcerative colitis

The correct answer is: B
This patient has Crohn's disease (CD), which is an idiopathic disease of chronic intestinal inflammation. It is a type of inflammatory bowel disease (IBD), which constitutes ulcerative colitis (UC) and Crohn's disease (CD). Crohn disease has variable manifestations. Perianal disease occurs in approximately one-quarter of patients with the formations of fistulas at any point in the bowel. Fistulas are abnormal connections between the bowel and adjacent organs. Abscess may form, and the fistula may act as a natural drainage system. Symptoms from fistulas can range from occasional painless drainage to painful cycles of abscess formation. Other features of CD depend on the location of the disease. If the colon is affected then diarrhea and bleeding may occur. If the small bowel is involved then postprandial pain and obstructive type symptoms will be present. Colonoscopy will show transmural involvement, skip lesions, cobblestone appearance, fistules, creeping fat, and perianal disease. Diagnosis is often made with colonoscopy with intubation of the terminal ileum in order to determine the presence of ileocolonic CD. On endoscopy, many of the characteristic features of Crohn’s disease, as detailed above can be visualized. Biopsies often confirm the diagnosis and show focal ulcerations and acute and chronic inflammation. Granulomas are also noted on biopsy in up to 30% of patients with CD.

Answer A: Carcinoid tumor is the most common neoplasm of the appendix, and these tumors clinically present with episodes of flushing and diarrhea once metastasized to the liver. Grossly, the lesions are typically submucosal and yellow in appearance from high lipid content. This is unlikely in this patient given the clinical presentation.


Answer C: Meckel’s diverticulum is a remnant of the omphalomesenteric duct. It is usually located within two feet of the ileocecal valve and is approximately two inches in length along the antimesenteric border. They typically contain gastric mucosa and are, therefore, a common cause of painless rectal bleed in children less than two years of age.


Answer D: Patients with perforated appendicitis have an acute presentation with right lower quadrant abdominal pain followed by nausea and vomiting. Abdominal exam typically reveals peritoneal signs such as guarding and rebound tenderness. Lack of the above signs and symptoms make appendicitis low on the differential. In addition, evidence of perianal disease is not the typical presentation of Crohn's disease.


Answer E: Ulcerative colitis (UC) causes mucosal inflammation, which is isolated to the colon. UC always starts at the rectum and can proceed to the sigmoid or descending colon or continue proximal to the splenic flexure. Perianal pain and evidence of fistulas are not the usual presentation of UC. Patients typically present with bowel urgency secondary to rectal inflammation. Abdominal pain is an unusual manifestation of UC and can suggest a complication such as toxic megacolon. Given this patient presentation, UC is an unlikely diagnosis.




Bottom Line: Crohn’s disease can involve any part of the gastrointestinal tract from the mouth to the anus. Classic findings include deep, transmural ulcers with skip lesions, which differentiate Crohn’s from ulcerative colitis.

For more information, see:
"Crohn Disease" on Medscape.

Link:
Progressive Multifocal Leukoencephalopathy in HIV


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 38-year-old male presents to the emergency department with shortness of breath. History reveals the patient has been rapidly deteriorating over the last few days. Past medical history reveals multiple sclerosis. Physical examination reveals a obtunded patient with cyanosis of the distal extremities. Magnetic resonance imaging of the brain shows large areas of hyperintensity on T2 weighted imaging in the parieto-occipital regions at the gray matter-white matter interface and extending to the periventricular area. Despite intubation and other emergency measures, the patient dies. The most likely cause for his rapid deterioration and death is

1. azathioprine
2. beta-interferon
3. cyclophosphamide
4. glatiramer
5. natalizumab

The correct answer is: E
The cause of this patient's death is progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy (PML). PML is a rapidly progressive and usually fatal demyelination of the CNS due to destruction of the oligodendrocytes caused by the JC virus. The JC virus is extremely common, and serologies are positive in 70-90% of the population. PML is seen most often in AIDS patients; however, it can also be seen in those taking immunosuppressive therapies as well, allowing the virus to go unchecked. Natalizumab is an immunosuppressant indicated for relapsing multiple sclerosis to help prevent accumulation of disability and reduce the frequency of exacerbations. It is a monoclonal antibody that acts as an inhibitor of alpha-4 integrin. This medication has been linked to reactivation of the JC virus. It is extremely rare for PML to develop in those who have been taking the medication for less than one year, but the incidence increases the longer a patient is on the medication. Because of the risk for PML, patients are required to enter into a rigorous screening and follow-up protocol. The MRI findings in the question stem are classically associated with PML.

Answers A & B & C & D: All of these medications are used in the treatment of multiple sclerosis to help prevent relapse and progression, however PML is only associated with natalizumab. Glatiramer and beta-interferon are the best initial choice for prevention of relapse.




Bottom Line: Natalizumab is a monoclonal antibody used in the treatment of multiple sclerosis. Its use has been linked to a progressive demyelination of the central nervous system called progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy. It is caused by reactivation of the JC virus and is often fatal.

For more information, see:

Progressive Multifocal Leukoencephalopathy in HIV

Link: "Idiopathic Thrombocytopenic Purpura" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 23-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of a two-day history of a rash involving both lower extremities. She also reports some gingival bleeding that she first noticed a couple of days ago. She states she just got over a bout of severe flu, but is feeling better. Her past medical history is unremarkable, and she takes no medications. Vital signs are normal.  Physical examination reveals small non-palpable punctate purple lesions involving bilateral lower extremities. Laboratory analysis reveals: 

Hemoglobin  14.0 g/dL
Hematocrit  42%
Leukocytes  7,000/mm3
Platelet count     10,000/mm3 
Prothrombin time 10 sec
Partial thromboplastin time 35 sec
INR  1.5

Fibrinogen and fibrinogen degradation products are within normal limits.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. aplastic anemia
2. disseminated intravascular coagulation
3. idiopathic thrombocytopenic purpura
4. thrombotic thrombocytopenic purpura
5. Von Willebrand’s disease

The correct answer is: C
Idiopathic thrombocytopenic purport (ITP) is characterized by isolated thrombocytopenia. Acute ITP often follows an acute infection and has a spontaneous resolution within 2 months. Chronic ITP persists longer than 6 months without a specific cause. Clinical presentation usually includes purpura or petechiae, mucosal bleeding, and menometrorrhagia and metrorrhagia in females. It is believed to be an autoimmune disorder in which IgG antibodies bind to platelets and lead to their excessive destruction in the spleen.

Answer A: This patient only has isolated thrombocytopenia, but otherwise normal CBC counts, therefore aplastic anemia is not the correct option.


Answer B: Fibrinogen and fibrinogen degradation products are both normal in this patient, therefore, DIC, which is associated with low fibrinogen level and increased FDP levels, is not a correct answer.


Answer D: TTP, which can also present with thrombocytopenia and petechial bleeding in lower extremities, is less likely since the patient does not have neurological symptoms, renal failure, or symptoms of hemolytic anemia characteristic of this syndrome.


Answer E: Coagulation studies are normal; therefore, Von Willebrand’s disease is not a correct option.




Bottom Line: ITP presents as isolated thrombocytopenia and is common after viral illness.

COMBANK Insight : Prehospital care focuses on the ABCs, which include providing oxygen, controlling severe hemorrhage, and initiating intravenous (IV) fluids to maintain hemodynamic stability.

For more information, see:
"Idiopathic Thrombocytopenic Purpura" on Medscape

Link: "Idiopathic Thrombocytopenic Purpura" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 23-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of a two-day history of a rash involving both lower extremities. She also reports some gingival bleeding that she first noticed a couple of days ago. She states she just got over a bout of severe flu, but is feeling better. Her past medical history is unremarkable, and she takes no medications. Vital signs are normal.  Physical examination reveals small non-palpable punctate purple lesions involving bilateral lower extremities. Laboratory analysis reveals: 

Hemoglobin  14.0 g/dL
Hematocrit  42%
Leukocytes  7,000/mm3
Platelet count     10,000/mm3 
Prothrombin time 10 sec
Partial thromboplastin time 35 sec
INR  1.5

Fibrinogen and fibrinogen degradation products are within normal limits.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most appropriate management is

1. allogeneic bone marrow transplant
2. no treatment is necessary
3. factor VIII concentrate
4. fresh frozen plasma and cryoprecipitate
5. prednisone

The correct answer is: E
As established in the previous question, this patient has Idiopathic Thrombocytopenic Purpura (ITP). The drug of choice for all adult patients with platelet count less than 50,000/mm3 is prednisone 1-2 mg/kg/day. Second line drugs used in treatment of ITP include IVIG, rituximab and thrombopoietic agents such as Promacta (eltrombopag) and Nplate (romiplostim). In refractory cases, splenectomy can be recommended. Adults with severe thrombocytopenia (ie, a platelet count below 30,000/microL) at the time of diagnosis are almost always treated even if they are asymptomatic or have only minor bleeding symptoms, because the course of the disease and the risk for future bleeding cannot be known. This contrasts with the management of children who are often not treated even with severe thrombocytopenia and petechiae, because spontaneous remissions often occur in children. Avoid unnecessary treatment of asymptomatic patients with mild to moderate thrombocytopenia (ie, a platelet count greater than 30,000 to 50,000/microL)

Answer A: Allogeneic bone marrow transplant is a treatment used in diseases involving bone marrow such as aplastic anemia, certain lymphomas, and leukemia and is not a correct answer.


Answer B: If the platelet count is >30,000/mm3 and they are asymptomatic, no treatment is necessary; however, this patient’s platelet count is < 30,000/mm3 and she is having mucosal bleeding, therefore not giving her any treatment is not a correct answer.


Answers C & D: Factor VIII concentrate is a treatment of Von Willebrand’s disease and will not be useful in this patient. FFP and cryoprecipitate are treatments of DIC and have no role in treatment of ITP.




Bottom Line: In ITP if patient is asymptomatic, and the platelet count is >30,000/mm3, treatment can be held off. The mainstay of the treatment in symptomatic patients is glucocorticoids (prednisone) and IVIG. The goal of the treatment is to keep platelets >50,000/mm3.

COMBANK Insight : You are often asked to differentiate between ITP and TTP on COMLEX level 2 CE. Know their characteristic features, diagnostic tests, and treatment options.

For more information, see:
"Idiopathic Thrombocytopenic Purpura" on Medscape

Link: "Primary Biliary Cirrhosis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 47-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of worsening fatigue and generalized pruritus for three months' duration. History reveals she has excessive daytime somnolence and takes frequent naps. She denies changes in mood, hair, or bowel habits. She is otherwise healthy, takes no medications, and does not smoke, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. She has never been previously exposed to blood products. Vital signs are unremarkable. Physical examination reveals her neck is supple without thyromegaly. Her abdomen is soft and non-tender. Her liver edge is palpable below the costal margin. Her skin is hyperpigmented with excoriations. Laboratory studies are shown below.

Alkaline phosphatase  400 U/L 
AST  40 U/L 
ALT  42 U/L 
Bilirubin, Total  1.6 mg/dL 
Albumin  4.0 g/dL 

Which of the following laboratory studies is most likely to be abnormal?

1. α1-Antitrypsin
2. antimitochondrial antibody
3. hepatitis B surface antibody
4. hepatitis C antibody
5. thyroid-stimulating hormone

The correct answer is: B
The most likely diagnosis is primary biliary cirrhosis (PBC). PBC is an autoimmune disorder that results in T-lymphocyte mediated destruction of the small intrahepatic bile ducts, eventually causing cholestasis. Patients are typically middle-aged women that present with pruritus, skin hyperpigmentation, hepatomegaly, and fatigue with a cholestatic pattern of liver function tests. The clinical presentation of PBC is different from most other liver diseases, making it very likely to be the diagnosis if a middle-aged woman presents with cholestatic liver function tests, pruritis, skin changes, and hepatomegaly. The antimitochondrial antibody (AMA) is a highly sensitive test and is positive in nearly 95% of patients with the disease with only a 2% false positive rate. If the AMA is positive, a liver biopsy is usually done to stage the disease and rule out other liver disorders. Treatment involves ursodeoxycholic acid (UDCA) to slow the progression of the disease and eventual liver transplantation.

Answer A: α-antitrypsin deficiency can affect the liver, lungs, or skin. Disease in the liver is due to the accumulation of the variant protein within hepatocytes. Typically, this would present with abnormal aminotransferase concentrations and not a cholestatic picture, as this patient has.


Answer C: The hepatitis B surface antibody can be seen in patients with either prior vaccination against hepatitis B or after having cleared an acute hepatitis B infection. This patient does not have risk factors for hepatitis B, and her clinical presentation is not typical.


Answer D: This patient has no risk factors for hepatitis C such as injection drug use or blood transfusion. Acute cases of hepatitis would present with jaundice, right upper quadrant tenderness, and elevated or markedly elevated AST/ALT.


Answer E: Despite her fatigue, she lacks other symptoms of hypothyroidism: skin changes, hair changes, decreased bowel movements. Furthermore, her examination and provided laboratory profile suggests a hepatic etiology.




Bottom Line: Primary biliary cirrhosis is associated with antimitochondrial antibody. Patients are typically middle-aged women that present with jaundice, pruritus, and fatigue.

For more information, see:
"Primary Biliary Cirrhosis" on Medscape.

Link: "Clinical manifestations and diagnosis of colonic diverticular disease." on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 50-year-old male presents to the emergency department with a three-day history of left lower quadrant abdominal pain. The pain is constant and is not relieved with defecation. History reveals he has associated nausea and anorexia. Past medical history is significant for hypertension and diverticulosis. His only medication is hydrochlorothiazide. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 130/70 mmHg, a heart rate of 80/min, and a temperature of 38.5°C (101.3°F). Physical examination reveals his stool is heme positive. Laboratory analysis reveals his WBC is 17,000 cells/cm3. He is started on intravenous antibiotics. Three days later the patient remains febrile with no clinical improvement. Abdominal examination is significant for tenderness in the left lower quadrant, but no guarding or rebound tenderness. The next best step in management is

1. abdominal computed tomography scan
2. abdominal radiograph
3. barium enema
4. colonoscopy
5. urinalysis

The correct answer is: A
This patient's left lower quadrant abdominal pain, fever, and leukocytosis coupled with a history of diverticulosis suggest diverticulitis. Diverticulitis is the result of obstruction at the neck of a diverticulum, usually by fecal matter and this complication usually occurs in 15-25% of patients with diverticulosis. The most sensitive imaging modality for diverticulosis is abdominal computed tomography (CT) scan with contrast. A contrast CT scan is also the most sensitive imaging modality to evaluate for any of the possible complications of diverticulitis, including perforation, abscess, obstruction, and fistula. Given that this patient has not had clinical improvement with IV antibiotics, a CT scan is indicated at this time to rule out a possible abscess. While small abscesses may be managed successfully with bowel rest, treatment of most abscesses usually consists of CT-guided percutaneous drainage.

Answer B: Abdominal x-ray would be useful in diagnosing perforations. While perforations are a known complication of diverticulitis, this patient does not have any peritoneal signs, such as guarding or rebound tenderness to suggest an acute perforation.


Answers C & D: Barium enema and colonoscopy are the two tests that risk perforation in acute episodes of diverticulitis since the colon is very friable due to infection and inflamed diverticula. Neither test should be performed at this time.


Answer E: A urinalysis is not indicated at this time as the most likely source of infection is in the abdomen.




Bottom Line: In acute episodes of diverticulitis, colonoscopy is contraindicated, and if there is no therapeutic response to antibiotics, a CT scan with contrast should be ordered.

For more information, see:
"Clinical manifestations and diagnosis of colonic diverticular disease." on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 70-year-old male patient presents to the office with his wife who states he has been confused for the past 2 days.  History also reveals lethargy. Past medical history is significant for Parkinson's disease. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 37.3ºC (99.1ºF), a blood pressure of 130/80 mmHg, and a heart rate of 82/min. Physical examination reveals dry oral mucus membranes and dilated pupils. Which of the following is most likely causing his symptoms?

1. Amantadine
2. Benztropine
3. Levodopa
4. Risperidone
5. Selegiline

The correct answer is: B
Confusion, delirium, dry mouth and dilated pupils are all anticholinergic effects, which could be caused by muscarinic blockers such as benztropine. Benztropine is an anticholinergic medication used to treat Parkinson's disease. It can be used to improve resting tremors and rigidity but it has little effect on bradykinesia.

Answer A: Amantadine is associated with livedo reticularis and not delirium. It is used for Parkinson's disease and the influenza virus.


Answer C: Levodopa can cause confusion and hallucinations but not dry mouth or dilated pupils. Levodopa is a dopamine precursor. It is usually combined with a drug that inhibits its breakdown in the periphery.


Answer D: Risperidone is an anti-psychotic drug that can cause Parkinson-like symptoms.


Answer E: Selegiline is an MAO-B inhibitor associated with malignant hypertension. It may be used to decrease the need for levodopa.




Bottom Line: Anticholinergic drugs can cause delirium in the elderly.

COMBANK Insight : While pharmacology is not a huge component of your level 2 exam, you still need to be familiar with the medications that are used to treat the most common disorders.

For more information, see:
"Pharmacologic treatment of Parkinson's disease" on UpToDate.

Link: "Hyperkalemia in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Abnormal Findings
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 54-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaints of weakness and palpitations. Past medical history reveals hypertension and type-2 diabetes for which he has been taking the same medications for 2 years. Physical examination reveals a normal rate and rhythm and lungs clear to auscultation. An ECG is obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
Which of the following is most likely causing his symptoms?

1. acetazolamide
2. digoxin
3. furosemide
4. lisinopril
5. metoprolol

The correct answer is: D
The ECG shows peaked T-waves, most obvious in the anterior chest leads. This is characteristic of hyperkalemia. ACE inhibitors such as lisinopril are common causes of hyperkalemia. These drugs block the formation of angiotensin thus decreasing the secretion of aldosterone. This can lead to elevated potassium. ACE inhibitors are used in diabetic patients to treat hypertension. ACE inhibitors protect the kidneys in hypertensive diabetic patients who are at double risk of kidney injury.

Answer A: Acetazolamide causes hyperchloremic metabolic acidosis. It is a carbonic anhydrase inhibitor that is used to treat glaucoma, epileptic seizures, pseudotumor cerebri, and altitude sickness.


Answer B: Digoxin can also cause heart block and other arrhythmias. It is primarily used to treat atrial fibrillation and atrial flutter with rapid ventricular response. However, beta-blockers or calcium channel-blockers are the first-line treatment. The side effects of digoxin are more common in patients with hypokalemia as digoxin competes with potassium ions for the same binding site on the sodium-potassium ATPase pump.


Answer C: Furosemide causes hypokalemia (potassium wasting) and metabolic alkalosis. It is a loop diuretic used in the treatment of congestive heart failure and edema.


Answer E: Metoprolol is a beta-1 receptor blocker that is also known to cause hyperkalemia. ACE inhibitors are more common causes of hyperkalemia and are also more frequently utilized in diabetic patients due to the renal-protective properties than beta-blockers.




Bottom Line: Hyperkalemia is associated with peaked T-waves on ECG and ACE inhibitors.

COMBANK Insight : Pharmacology is not tested as heavily on step II compared with step I but you still need to know the most common side effects of common medications.

For more information, see:
"Hyperkalemia in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.

Link: "Hyperkalemia in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Abnormal Findings
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 54-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaints of weakness and palpitations. Past medical history reveals hypertension and type-2 diabetes for which he has been taking the same medications for 2 years. Physical examination reveals a normal rate and rhythm and lungs clear to auscultation. An ECG is obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
Which of the following is the best initial treatment?

1. calcium gluconate
2. glucagon
3. immunoglobulin
4. nitroglycerine
5. spironolactone

The correct answer is: A
This patient most likely has hyperkalemia. This causes depolarization and an increased resting potential in cardiac tissue that can lead to peaked T-waves and a wide QRS interval. Calcium reverses this effect on the membrane potential. Calcium gluconate is the first step in treating hyperkalemia because it stabilizes the heart and allows time for other medications to decrease the potassium level.

Answer B: Glucagon is the antidote for beta-blocker overdose.


Answer C: Immunoglobulin in the form of anti-digoxin antibodies is the antidote for digoxin toxicity.


Answer D: Nitroglycerine is indicated for angina and myocardial infarction.


Answer E: Spironolactone may exacerbate hyperkalemia because it is a potassium sparing diuretic.




Bottom Line: Calcium reverses the effects of potassium on the membrane potential and is the most rapid way to correct arrhythmia associated with hyperkalemia.

COMBANK Insight : Pharmacology is not tested as heavily on step II compared with step I but you still need to know the most common side effects of common medications.

For more information, see:
"Hyperkalemia in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.

Link: Clinical features and diagnosis of hairy cell leukemia. UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine

A 73-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of weakness, fatigue and malaise. Vitals signs reveal a temperature of 98.3F, a blood pressure of 143/83 mmHg, a heart rate of 89/min, and a respiratory rate of 19/min. Physical examination reveals splenomegaly, and no lymphadenopathy. Laboratory analysis and peripheral blood smear are obtained and are abnormal. Flow cytometry is obtained for confirmation and confirms a group of cells that stain positive for tartrate-resistant acid phosphatase. 


Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. chronic lymphocytic leukemia
2. chronic myelogenous leukemia
3. hairy cell leukemia
4. Hodgkin lymphoma
5. multiple myeloma

The correct answer is: C
The most likely diagnosis is hairy cell leukemia. Hairy cell leukemia is a malignant disorder of well-differentiated B lymphocytes. It is a rare type of adult leukemia that usually affects the elderly male population. HCL often presents with splenomegaly, weakness, fatigue, petechiae, abdominal pain, early satiety and weight loss. Symptoms are similar to those seen in patients with CLL except that HCL patients rarely have lymphadenopathy. HCL is diagnosed based on history, physical exam and labs but the diagnosis is confirmed through the identification of hairy cells in the blood, blood marrow or spleen. TRAP (tartrate resistant acid phosphatase) stain and flow cytometry are helpful in distinguishing the pathognomonic hairy cells (see below). Peripheral smear shows hairy cells, also described as mononuclear cells with abundant pale cytoplasm and cytoplasmic projections. Ten percent of patients with HCL will never require treatment as they have a benign disease course. However, treatment should begin when patients become symptomatic or cytopenic. Nucleoside analogs are the initial treatment of choice.

Answer A: CLL is a malignant clonal proliferation of functionally incompetent lymphocytes. This is the most common type of leukemia and is commonly a diagnosis of elderly males. Most cases of CLL involve well-differentiated B lymphocytes although a very rare T cell type of CLL has been described. First-degree relatives of patients with CLL are three times more likely to develop the disorder. CLL is often asymptomatic but many patients may present with infection, fatigue, lymphadenopathy, splenomegaly, anemia, granulocytopenia and thrombocytopenia. CBC usually shows an abundance of small, normal appearing lymphocytes and ruptured smudge cells on peripheral smear. Bone marrow biopsy is rarely required for diagnosis or staging but may provide prognostic information and help assess response to therapy. Treatment is usually withheld until the patient becomes symptomatic. When symptomatic, CLL is treated with chemotherapy and radiation. The Rai staging system is commonly used for CLL, which correlates with survival.


Answer B: CML involves a clonal expansion of myeloid progenitor cells leading to leukocytosis with excess granulocytes and basophils. It may present with anemia, splenomegaly and constitutional symptoms. CBC analysis will show a very high WBC, often greater than 100,000.


Answer D: Hodgkin disease is a predominately B-cell malignancy. It may have an association with EBV. It usually presents in the third decade or sixth decade. Nodular sclerosing type is the most common type in patients in their thirties while lymphocyte depleted type is more common in patients in their sixties. HD usually presents with cervical adenopathy but it may also present with a mediastinal mass. Early in its presentation, it is present above the diaphragm with infradiaphragmatic involvement suggesting more disseminated disease. Other presenting signs include systemic symptoms, pruritus and hepatosplenomegaly. Pel-Ebstein fevers and alcohol induced nodal pain are rare but specific signs of Hodgkin’s disease. Diagnosis of HD is best made with excisional lymph node biopsy because fine-needle biopsy is usually non-diagnostic. Histology is considered positive when Reed-Sternberg cells are observed, which are abnormal giant cells with bilobar nuclei and eosinophilic nucleoli. The most common subtype of HD is nodular sclerosing type.


Answer E: Multiple myeloma is due to a clonal expansion of malignant plasma cells with excessive production of monoclonal immunoglobulins or immunoglobulin fragments. It is mainly a disease of the elderly population and has a peak incidence in the seventh decade. It usually presents with signs of anemia, bone pain, lytic bone lesions, and signs of hypercalcemia such as polyuria, constipation and confusion. The classic triad for MM is greater than 10% plasma cells in the bone marrow, M protein in the urine and evidence of lytic bone lesions. Because multiple myeloma is a osteoclastic process, a bone scan that detects osteoblastic activity, may be negative. MM treatment consists of chemotherapy, often with melphalan and prednisone.




Bottom Line: Hairy cell leukemia is a malignant disorder of well-differentiated B lymphocytes that stain positive with the TRAP stain. 

COMBANK Insight : Some questions on COMLEX exams will have only one logical answer choice. Do not overthink these types of questions.

For more information, see:
Clinical features and diagnosis of hairy cell leukemia. UpToDate.


Last Updated: 9/25/2016

Link: Clinical features and diagnosis of hairy cell leukemia, UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine

A 73-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of weakness, fatigue and malaise. Vitals signs reveal a temperature of 98.3F, a blood pressure of 143/83 mmHg, a heart rate of 89/min, and a respiratory rate of 19/min. Physical examination reveals splenomegaly, and no lymphadenopathy. Laboratory analysis and peripheral blood smear are obtained and are abnormal. Flow cytometry is obtained for confirmation and confirms a group of cells that stain positive for tartrate-resistant acid phosphatase. 


Question 2 of 2 in this set
Which of the following is would be expected to see on peripheral blood smear in this patient?

1. giant cells with bilobular nuclei and eosinophilic nucleoli
2. lacunar cells with collagen banding
3. mononuclear cells with abundant pale cytoplasm and cytoplasmic projections
4. PAS staining IgM deposits around the nucleus of plasma cells
5. plasma cells with a fried egg appearance

The correct answer is: C
The most likely diagnosis is hairy cell leukemia. Hairy cell leukemia is a malignant disorder of well-differentiated B lymphocytes. HCL is diagnosed based on history, physical exam and labs but the diagnosis is confirmed through the identification of hairy cells in the blood, blood marrow or spleen. TRAP (tartrate resistant acid phosphatase) stain and flow cytometry are helpful in distinguishing the pathognomonic hairy cells. Peripheral smear shows hairy cells, also described as mononuclear cells with abundant pale cytoplasm and cytoplasmic projections.

Answer A: Giant cells with bilobar nuclei and eosinophilic nucleoli are also known as Reed-Sternberg cells and are associated with Hodgkin’s disease. These cells are necessary but not sufficient for making a diagnosis of HD.


Answer B: Lacunar cells with collagen banding are associated with nodular sclerosing Hodgkin’s lymphoma, which is the most common type of Hodgkin’s disease.


Answer D: Dutcher bodies are PAS staining IgM deposits around the nucleus and may be seen within plasma cells of marrow aspirates. They are associated with Waldenstrom's macroglobulinemia.


Answer E: Plasma cells with a fried egg appearance are associated with multiple myeloma.




Bottom Line: Mononuclear cells with abundant pale cytoplasm and cytoplasmic projections are often seen on peripheral blood smear in patients with hairy cell leukemia. 

For more information, see:
Clinical features and diagnosis of hairy cell leukemia, UpToDate.

Link: "Sinus bradycardia" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Abnormal Findings
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 70-year-old male presents to the emergency department accompanied by his wife who reports that he has been sleeping all day and not answering questions appropriately. History reveals the patient has early-onset dementia and hypertension for which he takes hydrochlorothiazide and metoprolol. Physical examination reveals an unresponsive elderly man with strong carotid pulses and spontaneous respirations. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 80/40 mmHg, a heart rate of 42/min, a respiratory rate of 14/min, and an oxygen saturation of 89% on room air. An ECG is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most appropriate initial treatment is

1. atropine
2. dopamine
3. epinephrine
4. transcutaneous pacing
5. transvenous pacing

The correct answer is: A
This patient is presenting with unstable bradycardia (hypotension, hypoxemia) and should be treated with IV atropine as first line therapy per the 2010 AHA ACLS guidelines. A possible etiology in this scenario may be excessive/repeated dosing of metoprolol which commonly occurs in patients with dementia. Atropine works to increase heart rate through vagolytic effects, causing increase in cardiac output. Sinus bradycardia is a rhythm in which fewer than the normal number of impulses arise from the sinoatrial (SA) node, usually described at a rate less than 50/min (60/min in some texts). This can occur in normal individuals, be associated with sinus node dysfunction, or be the result of medications such as beta-blockers or calcium channel blockers. In this ECG, every sinus P-wave has a subsequent ventricular depolarization and QRS complex. The PR interval is normal, and the rate is 42/min.

Answer B: Dopamine is a potent vasopressor used as a chronotropic and inotropic medication in ACLS. Dopamine is not recommended as first line treatment of symptomatic bradycardia (or any ACLS condition for that matter). Dopamine stimulates dopamine, β, and α adrenergic receptors that effectively increases cardiac output and peripheral resistance leading to increased blood pressure and perfusion to tissues. It is used more commonly in the treatment of septic or cardiogenic shock.


Answer C: Epinephrine is a chronotropic medication that mainly stimulates β-1 adrenergic receptors, although has β-2 activity and slight α-1 activity as well. It is used as first line medication in pulseless ventricular tachycardia, ventricular fibrillation, or pulseless electrical activity (PEA) algorithms. In symptomatic bradycardia, it is used as an infusion after atropine has been used and while transcutaneous pacing is being arranged.


Answer D: Transcutaneous pacing is indicated for symptomatic bradycardia when atropine has failed improve the clinical situation. Pacing pads are placed on the patient’s chest and back and electricity is delivered through the chest wall to simulate myocardial contraction. Transcutaneous pacing is an effective way of temporarily maintaining an adequate heart rate and blood pressure but should only be used as a bridge toward more definitive care.


Answer E: Transvenous pacing is indicated when transcutaneous pacing and medications fail to produce an appropriate, perfusing cardiac output. Transvenous pacing involves placing an electrode into the heart through the superior vena cava and stimulating myocardial contraction from within the heart much like a typical pacemaker.




Bottom Line: Symptomatic bradycardia can present as altered mental status, chest pain, hypotension, or hypoxemia. First line treatment is atropine.

COMBANK Insight : Be familiar with ACLS guidelines, especially the initial treatment for common cardiac arrhythmias. They are often tested to the first level of treatment but rarely further down the algorithm.

For more information, see:
"Sinus bradycardia" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 67-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of abdominal pain for 3 weeks' duration. She also states she has experienced a 20 pound unintentional weight loss, decreased appetite, and fatigue. Her past medical history is significant for type I diabetes, hypertension, and peripheral neuropathy. She has a 46 pack-year smoking history but quit 10 years ago, and she drinks one glass of wine daily. Her current medications include insulin, gabapentin, lisinopril, and hydrochlorothiazide. Physical examination is significant for mild abdominal tenderness and jaundice. A CT scan of the abdomen is significant for a mass in the head of the pancreas. The most appropriate laboratory test to confirm the diagnosis is

1. alpha-fetoprotein
2. bombesin
3. Ca-125
4. Ca-19-9
5. S-100

The correct answer is: D
The patient in the above scenario is most likely suffering from pancreatic cancer. Pancreatic cancer presents with weight loss, anorexia, jaundice and abdominal pain, or it can be asymptomatic. Risk factors for the development of pancreatic cancer include diabetes, smoking and alcoholism. Tumor markers for pancreatic cancer include CEA, which is non-specific, and Ca-19-9.

Answer A: Alpha-fetoprotein is elevated in hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC) and can be used as a screening tool in patients at increased risk of developing HCC, such as those with hepatitis B or hepatitis C. Alpha-fetoprotein may also be elevated in certain germ cell tumors of the testis.


Answer B: Bombesin is a tumor marker for neuroblastomas as well as some lung and stomach cancers.


Answer C: Ca-125 is a marker of ovarian cancer.


Answer E: S-100 is a tumor marker for some neural tumors and melanomas




Bottom Line: Ca-19-9 is a tumor marker used to aid in the diagnosis of pancreatic cancer.

Link: "Acetaminophen Toxicity" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 29-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of abdominal pain and nausea. History reveals she ingested an entire bottle of acetaminophen over 12 hours ago in a suicide attempt. Past medical history is significant for depression. Physical examination reveals tenderness to the right upper quadrant. Her urine pregnancy test is positive. The most appropriate next step in management is

1. activated charcoal
2. ipecac syrup
3. N-acetylcysteine
4. observation
5. whole bowel irrigation

The correct answer is: C
Acetaminophen overdose is a common toxicologic emergency. It is the most common cause of acute hepatic failure in the United States. The clinical course typically follows four phases of progression. In phase 1 patients are usually asymptomatic or complain of anorexia, nausea and vomiting. There is a slight elevation in transaminases. This phase is from 0-24 hours after ingestion. Phase 2 arises 18-72 hours after ingestion and involves right upper quadrant pain, tachycardia and hypotension. Phase 3 is the most lethal stage and occurs at hours 72-96. It involves hepatic necrosis, coagulopathy, hypoglycemia, hepatic encephalopathy and renal failure. Phase 4 occurs after 4 days assuming patients recovered from phase 3. It involves a resolution of organ failure and symptoms. N-acetylcysteine is the drug of choice in treatment of acetaminophen toxicity. It is pregnancy class B and works by acting as a sulfhydryl donor to restore glutathione in the liver. Ideally, N-acetylcysteine should be initiated prior to the elevation in alanine aminotransferase but even with late presentations it has been found to be protective of the liver.

Answer A: Activated charcoal is used in certain toxic overdoses when the ingestion has occurred within the past 30 minutes to one hour. It would be of no benefit in an overdose occurring 24 hours prior.


Answer B: Ipecac syrup was once used a pro-emetic substance to induce evacuation of the stomach after ingestion of toxic substances. It is no longer used in this manner as it carries a risk of aspiration.


Answer D: Observation would not be an appropriate treatment in a symptomatic patient with admitted acetaminophen ingestion. The only time it may be acceptable is if serum acetaminophen levels were undetectable.


Answer E: Whole bowel irrigation involves administration of large amounts of osmotically balanced polyethylene glycol in order to flush the gastrointestinal tract and prevent absorption of toxic substances. It is not indicated 24 hours after an ingestion and is not commonly used in acetaminophen overdose.




Bottom Line: N-acetylcysteine is the drug of choice for treatment of acetaminophen toxicity.

For more information, see:
"Acetaminophen Toxicity" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 65-year-old female presents to the office for review of a recent DEXA scan. She is found to have osteoporosis. Past medical history reveals she had knee surgery five years ago. After the surgery, she developed a DVT and was put on warfarin for six months. Her mother died of breast cancer, her maternal aunt has endometrial cancer, and her paternal aunt has ovarian cancer. She does not wish to take bisphosphonates because of her reflux symptoms and would like to consider raloxifene. The most appropriate next step is

1. prescribe raloxifene
2. tell her it is contraindicated due to breast cancer risk
3. tell her it is contraindicated due to endometrial cancer risk
4. tell her it is contraindicated due to her previous deep vein thrombosis
5. tell her it is contraindicated due to ovarian cancer risk

The correct answer is: D
Raloxifene is a mixed agonist/antagonist of estrogen receptors, acting as an antagonist in breast/vaginal tissues and an agonist in bone. Raloxifene has been shown in a large meta-analysis to increase the risk of thromboembolism (DVT and pulmonary embolism), and should therefore be avoided in women with a past history of thrombosis. Raloxifene does not increase the risk of endometrial cancer, unlike tamoxifen. The risk of breast cancer decreases with raloxifene use. There is no definitive data showing raloxifene modifies the risk of ovarian cancer.

Answer A: Raloxifene should be avoided in women with a past history of thrombosis.


Answer B: The risk of breast cancer decreases with raloxifene use.


Answer C: Raloxifene does not increase the risk of endometrial cancer, unlike tamoxifen.


Answer E: There is no definitive data showing raloxifene modifies the risk of ovarian cancer.




Bottom Line: Raloxifene should be avoided in women with a past history of thrombosis.

COMBANK Insight : The selective estrogen receptor modulators (SERMs) have different effects on various tissues. These differences make for useful board questions, as the medications are not interchangeable. Clomiphene is used in anovulation and acts as an antagonist at the hypothalamus. Tamoxifen is used in breast cancer (antagonism in breast) and acts as agonists at the uterus and bone. Raloxifene is used in breast cancer (antagonist in breast) and osteoporosis (agonist in bone), plus it acts as an antagonist at the uterus thus is not associated with endometrial cancer risk.

For more information, see:
Adomaityte, et al. 2008. Effect of raloxifene therapy on venous thromboembolism in postmenopausal women. A meta-analysis. Thromb Haemost. 99(2):338-42.

Link: "Kallman Syndrome and Idiopathic Hypogonadotropic Hypogonadism" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 15-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of amenorrhea. History reveals she has never had her period. Her past medical history is unremarkable, and she does not take any medications. Physical examination reveals absent breast development and no pubic or axillary hair growth. Further examination also reveals cranial nerve I dysfunction. Serum FSH is 2 mIU/L (normal 4-30 mIU/L). An ultrasound is obtained and reveals a uterus and two ovaries. This patient's karyotype is most likely

1. 45 XO
2. 45 XY
3. 46 XX
4. 46 XY
5. 47 XXY

The correct answer is: C
The most likely diagnosis is Kallmann’s syndrome, which is characterized by a congenital absence of GnRH secretion and anosmia. Patients have a normal XX genotype and normal female internal reproductive organs. They present with amenorrhea and absent secondary sexual characteristics (ie. breast development and axillary hair), in addition to anosmia, which is the absence of smell. The first cranial nerve is the olfactory nerve, and the examination notes dysfunction of this nerve. The FSH and LH levels are low, which is in contrast to the elevated levels in primary ovarian failure.

Answer A: Patients with Turner’s syndrome have a 45 XO karyotype. This syndrome is characterized by gonadal dysgenesis and various physical abnormalities such as short stature, broad chest, low hairline, low-set ears, and webbed necks.


Answer B: Patients with Kallmann's syndrome have a 46 XX genotype. A 45 XY genotype means would be found in a male with an absent somatic chromosome.


Answer D: 46 XY is a normal male genotype. The ultrasound demonstrates normal female reproductive organs, and the history is more consistent with Kallman syndrome.


Answer E: 47 XXY is the karyotype found in patients with Klinefelter’s syndrome. These patients are males and will have hypogonadism (low testosterone, high FSH and LH levels), gynecomastia, and lanky (scaphoid) bodies.




Bottom Line: If a patient presents with amenorrhea and anosmia, Kallmann’s syndrome is the most likely diagnosis. Patients will have normal genotypes.

For more information, see:
"Kallman Syndrome and Idiopathic Hypogonadotropic Hypogonadism" on Medscape.

Link: "Chylothorax" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 74-year-old male presents to the emergency department after a motor vehicle collision. The patient is able to converse but is visibly short of breath and complains of having difficulty breathing. Vital signs reveal tachycardia and tachypnea. Physical examination reveals decreased breath sounds in the left middle and lower lung fields. Plain radiographs are obtained and reveal a left pleural effusion. Thoracentesis drains odorless fluid. Fluid analysis reveals a ratio of pleural fluid cholesterol to triglyceride that is less than 0.75. The most likely diagnosis is

1. chylothorax
2. congestive heart failure
3. empyema
4. pneumonia
5. pseudochylothorax

The correct answer is: A
The most likely diagnosis in this patient is a chylothorax. A chylothorax is associated with trauma, recent thoracic surgery, and malignancy. Thoracentesis with triglycerides > 110 mg/dL is almost always pathognomonic of lymphatic fluid leak into the pleural space from the thoracic duct. A ratio of pleural fluid cholesterol to triglyceride of less than 1 is also diagnostic. Recall, the thoracic duct is the left-sided structure that filters the majority of the lymphatic fluid back into venous circulation. A pleural effusion can be serous fluid (hydrothorax), blood (hemothorax), chyle (chylothorax), or pus (pyothorax/empyema).

Answer B: Congestive heart failure usually causes straw-colored fluid with mononuclear cell predominance.


Answer C: Empyema pleural fluid typically has a leukocytosis, low pH (<7.20), low glucose (<60 mg/dL), high LDH (lactic dehydrogenase), elevated protein and is malodorous.


Answer D: Pneumonia is contained within the lung unless it causes the accumulation of pus in the pleural cavity (empyema).


Answer E: Pseudochylothorax, or cholesterol pleurisy, must be distinguished from chylothorax. Pseudochylothorax results from an accumulation of cholesterol crystals in a chronic existing effusion. The most common cause of pseudochylothorax is chronic rheumatoid pleurisy, followed by tuberculosis and poorly treated empyema.





Bottom Line: Chylothorax pleural fluid is milky white with high triglycerides.

COMBANK Insight : Pleural fluid analysis aids in differentiation between diseases. Determining transudate versus exudate using Light's criteria may come up on test day. A transudate by Light's criteria is defined as pleural protein/serum protein < 0.5, pleural LDH/serum LDH < 0.6, or pleural LDH < 2/3 of normal LDH.

For more information, see:
"Chylothorax" on Medscape.

Link: "Neonatal Brachial Plexus Palsies" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Pediatrics
You are called to assess a newborn in the nursery. Documented history reveals that the infant was full term and born via a spontaneous vaginal delivery in vertex presentation. The infant's birth weight was 10.0 pounds. Physical examination reveals the infant is having difficulty moving his left arm. The left arm is held in an adducted and internally rotated position with the forearm extended and pronated. Strength is preserved in the hand and wrist and the infant is able to grasp. The nerve roots most likely affected are

1. C5 to C6
2. C5 to C8
3. C5 to T1
4. C7 to T1
5. C8 and T1

The correct answer is: A
Erb’s palsy accounts for 50% of brachial plexus syndromes in the newborn. The most common cause is from dystocia, a difficult childbirth, where the infant’s head and neck is pulled stretching the brachial plexus. Often C5 and C6 are injured, although in some cases C7 will also be involved. This results in deltoid and infraspinatus weakness, and the classic “waiter’s tip” position described above. The distal muscles are preserved and the infant is able to grasp objects with his fingers.

Answers B & C & D & E: The other answers include C8 to T1, which would manifest with hand weakness. The case above describes proximal weakness only, with preserved hand and wrist strength indicating an upper plexus injury.




Bottom Line: Erb’s palsy is a brachial plexus injury involving cervical nerve roots C5 and C6.

For more information, see:
"Neonatal Brachial Plexus Palsies" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 75-year-old female presents to the emergency department with sudden-onset shortness of breath and chest pain. Past medical history is significant for metastatic breast cancer, and a 30 pack-year smoking history. History reveals that the chest pain is worse with deep inspiration. Physical examination reveals a diaphoretic and cachectic female in sinus tachycardia. An arterial blood gas is obtained and is most likely to reveal

1. decreased alveolar-arterial oxygen gradient
2. decreased diffusion limited carbon monoxide
3. increased alveolar-arterial oxygen gradient
4. increased diffusion limited carbon monoxide
5. normal alveolar-arterial oxygen gradient

The correct answer is: C
The most likely diagnosis is pulmonary embolus. The patients risk factors include a medical history of malignancy and tobacco history. The five major mechanisms of hypoxia include; decrease fractionally inspiration oxygen, hypoventilation, diffusion impairment, ventilation/perfusion (V/Q) mismatch, and shunt. A pulmonary embolism is an example of a ventilation/perfusion mismatch. Another example of V/Q mismatch is pneumonia. The mechanism of hypoxia can be determined by evaluating the alveolar-arterial (A-a) oxygen gradient, the PCO2 level, the effect of administered oxygen, and the diffusion limited carbon monoxide capacity (DLCO). V/Q mismatch is characterized by a high or normal PCO2, an increased A-a gradient, a positive effect of administered oxygen, and a normal DLCO. The A-a gradient represents the difference in PAO2 (alveolar) and PaO2 (arterial). A normal A-a gradient is around 8-12 mmHg and increases with age. It is increased in cases of pulmonary embolism because the embolism is blocking the blood flow to the lungs for gas exchange to occur resulting in higher O2 levels in the alveoli as opposed to the artery.

Answers A & E: The expected change in A-a gradient would be an increase in V/Q mismatch. An increase in A-a gradient can also be seen in cases of diffusion impairment and shunting.


Answers B & D: DLCO is normal in cases of V/Q mismatch. A decreased DLCO is seen in cases of diffusion impairment across the alveoli (i.e. pulmonary fibrosis), or in cases of decreased blood transit time through the alveoli (i.e. anemia).




Bottom Line: A pulmonary embolism presents with an increase in A-a gradient.

COMBANK Insight : Remember the five mechanisms of hypoxia include; decrease fractionally inspiration oxygen, hypoventilation, diffusion impairment, ventilation/perfusion (V/Q) mismatch, and shunt. Each is characterized by different findings. Hypoventilation is characterized by the hallmark of increased PCO2 as seen on arterial blood gas. The hallmark of diffusion impairment is a decreased DLCO, and the hallmark of shunt is a negligible effect of oxygen administration on hypoxia.

Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Surgery
A 34-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaints of left eye pain, tearing, decreased vision, and photophobia for 2 hours. Past medical history reveals psoriatic arthritis that is treated with topical steroids. Slit lamp exam is significant for cells in the anterior chamber of the left eye. Laboratory tests are positive for HLA B-27. In addition to an association with psoriatic arthritis, HLA B-27 is associated with

1. Churg-Strauss syndrome
2. Hashimoto's thyroiditis
3. reactive arthritis
4. rheumatoid arthritis
5. systemic lupus erythematosus

The correct answer is: C
This patient has anterior uveitis (iritis/iridocyclitis) which is an autoimmune disease, where symptoms include sudden pain, redness, photophobia, decreased vision, and excessive tearing. Slit lamp examination will show cells and flare in the anterior chamber, ciliary flush, and keratic precipitates. It is important to check intra-ocular pressure as this can lead to blindness. Treatment is aimed at reducing the inflammatory response with steroid eye drops, and giving systemic steroids if the condition does not improve. HLA B-27 is test that can be performed that classically is associated with psoriatic arthritis, reactive arthritis (formerly Reiter's syndrome), and ankylosing spondylitis.

Answer A: Churg-Strauss syndrome is a vasculitis that causes eosinophilia, peripheral neuropathy, glomerulonephritis, and respiratory symptoms like dyspnea and hemoptysis.


Answer B: Hashimoto's thyroiditis causes hypothyroidism with positive labs being detected for antimicrosomal, antithyroglobulin, and/or anti-thyroid peroxidase antibodies that destroy thyroid cells.


Answer D: There is no laboratory test that excludes or proves the diagnosis of rheumatoid arthritis. Helpful labs include rheumatoid factor, erythrocyte sedimentation rate, C-reactive protein, antinuclear antibodies, and anti-cyclic citrullinated peptide antibody.


Answer E: There is no laboratory test that excludes or proves the diagnosis of systemic lupus erythematosus. Helpful labs include antibodies to double-stranded DNA, to the Smith nuclear antigen (anti-Sm), and/or antinuclear antibodies (ANA).




Bottom Line: HLA B-27 is associated with reactive arthritis (formerly, Reiter's syndrome), ankylosing spondylitis, and psoriatic arthritis.

COMBANK Insight : Know the symptoms, management, and association of anterior uveitis to autoimmune conditions, as well as the association of HLA B-27 to certain conditions.

For more information, see:
The Wills Eye Manual. 5th Ed. Lippincott Williams & Wilkins. Philadelphia, PA, pp. 334-339.

Link: "The approach to the painful joint" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 54-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of knee pain. Past medical history reveals hypertension, hyperlipidemia, osteoarthritis, and three knee arthroscopies. Medications include hydrochlorothiazide and simvastatin. Physical examination reveals an erythematous, swollen, tender knee. The most appropriate management is

1. acetaminophen and indomethacin
2. magnetic resonance imaging
3. oral antibiotic therapy for one month
4. plain radiographs
5. synovial fluid analysis

The correct answer is: E
Regardless of suspected cause, any acutely inflamed joint should be aspirated. Aspiration with synovial fluid analysis is the most likely to give a definitive diagnosis. Synovial fluid WBCs should be less than 200/mcL with < 25% neutrophils. Trauma, osteoarthritis, and lupus < 3000 WBC/mcL. Gout and rheumatoid arthritis > 4000 WBC/mcL. Septic > 60,000 WBC/mcL (these are all rough cut-points).

Answer A: Acetaminophen is used for osteoarthritis and indomethacin can be used for acute gout, but aspiration is necessary to confirm the diagnosis.


Answer B: Magnetic resonance imaging is primarily used to identify soft tissue damage to the joint such as meniscal or ligamentous pathology. Physical examination did not reveal ligamentous laxity and there is no history of injury that would indicate such a problem.


Answer C: Oral antibiotics should be continued for about a month in septic arthritis, but that diagnosis has not been confirmed.


Answer D: Plain radiographs are primarily used for bony abnormalities of the joint. They can be used to look for fractures and dislocations, and to assess for arthritis. This patient already has known osteoarthritis so the next best step would be to determine if the cause of knee swelling is new.




Bottom Line: The next step in management for an acutely swollen knee is joint aspiration with synovial fluid analysis.

For more information, see:
"The approach to the painful joint" on Medscape.

Link: "Borderline personality disorder" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Psychiatry
Discipline: Psychiatry
A 32-year-old female presents to your clinic for a routine visit. While collecting her history, she reports that her family has all turned against her and she feels like people are always out to get her. She reports her boyfriend recently broke up with her and she feels lost without him and continues to call him multiple times daily. Social history reveals she lives with a roommate, but has difficulty keeping roommates due to mood swings and inappropriate irritability. She mentions to you that you are the best doctor she has ever had, but that you should fire your colleague whom she has seen in the past as she is terrible at her job and rude. Physical examination reveals lacerations on the left forearm and right hip area. The most appropriate treatment is

1. dialectical behavioral therapy
2. lithium 600 mg orally twice daily
3. no treatment is necessary
4. lorazepam 1mg orally twice daily
5. risperidone 1mg orally once daily

The correct answer is: A
This patient displays signs of borderline personality disorder. She exhibits paranoia, mood lability, irritability, fear of abandonment, self harm, and splitting (where she sees you as "all good", but another doctor as "all bad"). Borderline personality disorder is more common in women. Extensive psychotherapy with dialectical behavioral therapy (DBT) is the treatment of choice for borderline personality disorder. In this form of therapy, the therapist must be supportive and directive, and the patient is given specific assignments and homework to help their problem solving skills and improve their interpersonal skills and overall coping. DBT focuses on teaching the patient mindfulness, distress tolerance without impulsivity, emotional regulation, and interpersonal effectiveness. The goal and focus of this therapy is to reduce the impulses to self-mutilate. DBT is the only treatment for borderline personality disorder with data to support its efficacy. It is used in both outpatient and inpatient settings.

Answer B: Lithium is a mood stabilizer used in the treatment of bipolar disorder. It is occasionally used as an adjunctive treatment for borderline personality disorder, but is not the most effective treatment when used alone.


Answer C: Treatment is indeed necessary to decrease the future risk of self harm or self-mutilation.


Answer D: Lorazepam is a benzodiazepine anxiolytic that increases GABA inhibition. This drug class may be used to break panic attacks or to reduce acute anxiety, but it should not be used to treat borderline personality disorder.


Answer E: Risperidone is an atypical antipsychotic agent. It is not the treatment of choice for borderline personality disorder. Antipsychotics are occasionally used as an adjunctive treatment for borderline personality disorder, but are not the most effective treatment when used alone.




Bottom Line: Dialectical behavioral therapy is the treatment for borderline personality disorder.

For more information, see:
"Borderline personality disorder" on Medscape.
