Link: "Carpal tunnel syndrome" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 50-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of right wrist pain and numbness. History reveals the pain began after she started a new job as a secretary, and is significantly worse during the night. The patient admits to occasionally having difficulty gripping objects with her right hand. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 98.4 F, a blood pressure of 120/84 mmHg, a heart rate of 63/min, a respiration rate of 18/min and an oxygen saturation of a 100% on room air. Physical examination reveals decreased sensation on the right thenar eminence compared to the left. Grip strength is 4/5 on the right and 5/5 on the left. The patient is asked to press the palmar surfaces of her hands together in forced extension at the wrists. After thirty seconds she reports reproduction of symptoms in her right wrist. This test is also known as

1. Adson's test
2. prayer test
3. Phalen’s test
4. provocation test
5. Tinel’s test

The correct answer is: B
The prayer test is a diagnostic test for carpal tunnel syndrome and consists of pressing the palmar surfaces of the hands together in forced extension at the wrists to reproduce symptoms. It increases pressure in the carpel tunnel significantly when compared to Phalen's test, and may have more sensitivity as a screening test.

Answer A: Adson's test is used for diagnosing thoracic outlet syndrome. The physician feels for the radial artery while the patient ipsilateral rotates the head and deeply inhales. If the radial pulse decreases or disappears the test is positive.


Answer C: Phalen’s test is a diagnostic test for carpal tunnel syndrome and consists of having the patient hold his/her wrists in complete and forced flexion (i.e., pressing the dorsal surfaces of the hands together) for about a minute and noting any burning, tingling, or numbness in the median nerve distribution of the hand. If the patient complains of any of these symptoms during the test, the test is considered to be positive. Phalen's test has a specificity of 80% but sensitivity is lower.


Answer D: The provocation test is yet another method for carpal tunnel diagnosis, and consists of compressing and holding pressure over the transverse carpal ligament of the wrist.


Answer E: Tinel's test is also a test for carpal tunnel syndrome and is performed by lightly tapping over the transverse carpal ligament of the wrist. A positive test illicit tingling or numbness in the distribution of the median nerve.




Bottom Line: The prayer test is an excellent screening tool for detecting carpel tunnel syndrome. It is performed by having the patient put their hands together in forced flexion. Reproduction of symptoms is considered diagnostic. 

COMBANK Insight : The more common a disorder is in the community the more likely it will show up on your level 2 exam. Be sure to review all of the special physical examination tests especially for the wrist, knee, and shoulder.

For more information, see:
"Carpal tunnel syndrome" on Medscape.

Link: "Sickle Cell Anemia Treatment and Management" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Services Rendered
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 1-week-old male presents to the office for an initial visit. Family history reveals both parents have sickle cell disease. The patient's mother inquires which vaccinations will be recommended for her son in addition to routine childhood vaccines. The most appropriate answer is

1. hepatitis A
2. meningococcal, pneumococcal and parvovirus B19
3. no additional vaccines are required
4. parvovirus B19
5. pneumococcal and meningococcal

The correct answer is: E
Sickle cell disease is an autosomal recessive disease. Since both parents have sickle cell disease, the child must also have sickle cell disease. Children with sickle cell disease are susceptible to infection with encapsulated bacteria including Streptococcus pneumoniae and Neisseria meningitidis. In addition to the routine childhood vaccinations, these children should also receive the pneumococcal vaccine at age 2 and a booster shot at age 5, as well as a one time meningococcal vaccine given after age 2. Current guidelines also recommend annual influenza vaccinations in all children with sickle cell disease as they have a 56-fold higher chance of contracting influenza than children who do not have sickle cell disease.

Answer A: Hepatitis A vaccine is recommended for all children at 1 year of age.


Answers B & D: There is not currently a vaccine available for parvovirus B19.


Answer C: Additional vaccinations are very important as infections are a major cause of death in children with sickle cell disease.




Bottom Line: Children with sickle cell disease must be vaccinated for pneumococcal and meningococcal bacteria because these encapsulated bacteria pose a serious risk of infection in asplenic individuals.

"Sickle Cell Anemia Treatment and Management" on Medscape.

80 of 200

Link: "Third degree heart block"


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
An 80-year-old male presents to the emergency department with chest pressure that began twelve hours ago while at rest. This pressure is associated with right arm discomfort. History reveals no previous cardiac problems and he currently takes no medications. Physical examination reveals a well appearing elderly male in no distress. Vitals reveal a blood pressure of 167/90 mmHg, a heart rate of 75/min, a respiratory rate of 14/min, and a room air oxygen saturation of 94%. A troponin level ten minutes later is elevated. While you are explaining your current plan to the patient, he becomes diaphoretic and unresponsive. His telemetry strip is shown in the exhibit. Which of the following best describes his telemetry strip?

1. multifocal atrial tachycardia
2. sinus bradycardia
3. third degree AV block
4. type I mobitz 2nd degree AV block
5. type II mobitz 2nd degree AV block

The correct answer is: C
Third degree AV block (complete heart block) is defined as complete dissociation between the atrium and ventricular conduction systems. This is noted on the surface ECG by P-wave and QRS complexes having no relation to one another. In this question, the P-waves are regularly appearing at a rate of 75/min and the ventricular rate is 43/min. Note that occasionally there are p-waves “hidden” in T-waves and also occurring after the QRS complex. This pattern of atrioventricular dissociation is common during myocardial ischemia (as this patient is currently experiencing) or infarction due to underperfusion of the AV conduction system.

Answer A: Multifocal atrial tachycardia (MAT) is a tachydysrhythmia that is characterized by an irregularly irregular rhythm, rate >100/min, and 3 different consecutive atrial morphologies. In this question, the rate is regular, slow, and the atrial waves are similar.


Answer B: Sinus bradycardia is defined as a rate less than 50/min with atrioventricular association. For every sinus P-wave, there is a subsequent ventricular depolarization and QRS complex. In this question, there is a dissociation between P-waves and QRS complexes.


Answer D: Type I mobitz 2nd degree AV block is also known as Wenckebach block. This arrhythmia is defined as regularly irregular, with a progressive lengthening of the PR interval until a ventricular beat is not conducted. Following the non-conducted beat, the following PR interval is noted to be shortened. In the question there is no association between the P-wave and the QRS complex therefore there is no lengthening of the PR interval.


Answer E: Type II mobitz 2nd degree AV block is characterized on a surface ECG by intermittently non-conducted P-waves not preceded by PR prolongation and not followed by PR shortening. In this question, there is no association between P-waves and QRS complexes, therefore all P-waves are non-conducted by definition.




Bottom Line: Third degree heart block is a condition in which there is complete dissociation between P-waves and QRS complexes due to complete loss of electrical conduction between the atria and the ventricles.

COMBANK Insight : Have a system for interpreting ECGs. Start with rate (determine regular, tachycardia or bradycardia), and rhythm (determine regularity or irregularity). Next determine whether P-waves are “talking” to the QRS complexes: for every P-wave, there should be a QRS following close behind. Next determine axis, followed by looking at PR and QT intervals. Finally, assess the ECG for repolarization - ie, ST segment elevation/depression or T-wave inversion. If you complete this systematically on every ECG, you will not overlook any critical part of the tracing.

For more information, see:
"Third degree heart block"
Wolfson, Allan B et al. Harwood-Nuss’ Clinical Practice of Emergency Medicine. 5th Ed. Philadelphia, PA: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins; 2010. Chapters 83-84.


Last Updated: 9/22/2016

Link: "Bloodborne Pathogens/ Occupational Exposure" on Center for Disease Control and Prevention.


Patient Presentation: Services Rendered
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention

Discipline: Surgery
A general surgeon is closing an abdominal incision of a patient who is HIV positive. He accidentally sustains a needle stick injury during the procedure. His approximate risk of acquiring the infection is

1. 0.03%
2. 0.3%
3. 13%
4. 3%
5. 33%

The correct answer is: B
The average risk of transmission of HIV to a health care worker after percutaneous exposure is approximately 0.3% with no prophylaxis. It is estimated that this risk is reduced by ~80% when post exposure prophylaxis is administered. Post exposure treatment of choice is Tenofovir/emtricitabine for low risk exposures (patient source does not have detectable virus).

Answers A & C & D & E: The first step to a percutaneous exposure is to wash the area thoroughly with soap and water. The health care worker should immediately report the exposure to employee health or to the department listed under hospital protocol.




Bottom Line: A health care worker has a ~0.3% of contracting HIV after percutaneous exposure to HIV infected blood.

Link: "Acute Pancreatitis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 30-year-old male presents to the emergency department with severe abdominal pain, nausea, and vomiting for the last few hours' duration. History reveals he was eating pizza and drinking beers with a few friends when the pain began. The pain radiates from his epigastrium directly to his back and is constant. He has never had similar abdominal pain in the past. The patient has no known past medical history and takes no medications except for an over-the-counter vitamin supplement. He smokes socially and drinks approximately 3 beers once a week with his friends. His family history is significant for a father that died of a myocardial infarction at the age of 45, and a grandfather with coronary artery disease. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 105/65 mmHg, a heart rate of 115/min, a temperature of 38°C (100.4°F), and a respiratory rate of 20/min.  Physical examination reveals there are several xanthomas under his eyes. Bowel sounds are hypoactive. There is mid-epigastric tenderness to palpation and mild abdominal distension.  His laboratory values are shown below:

Leukocyte count 18,000 cells/mm3 
Lipase  195 U/L
 AST  70 U/L
 ALT 55 U/L
 Alkaline phosphatase 80 U/L  

The most likely cause of this patient's symptoms are

1. alcohol
2. cigarette smoking
3. factitious disorder
4. gallstones
5. hypertriglyceridemia

The correct answer is: E
This patient's clinical presentation and laboratory studies are highly suggestive of acute pancreatitis. While pancreatitis most commonly occurs secondary to gallstones and alcohol, this patient is unlikely to have either of these causes. Other causes of pancreatitis include drugs, hypercalcemia, infectious causes, ischemia, vasculitis, malignancy, and dyslipidemia. The most likely etiology of his pancreatitis is hypertriglyceridemia given his family history and the notable xanthomas on physical exam, suggesting high serum triglyceride levels. When confirmed, it is important to treat the patient's hypertriglyceridemia to prevent future episodes of pancreatitis. Patients with acute pancreatitis typically present with sudden onset of epigastric pain, which can radiate to their back. Oftentimes, abdominal pain is accompanied by nausea, vomiting, fever, and tachycardia. On physical examination, patients usually will have abdominal tenderness to palpation, particularly in the epigastric region. Abdomen is often times distended and hypoactive bowel sounds are present. Diagnosis is made with laboratory studies that will typically show an amylase and lipase level that are at least three times the upper limit of normal. However, degree of elevation of pancreatic enzymes does not correlate with how severe the disease is. Moreover, such as is the case with this patient, in approximately one-third of patients, the enzyme levels will not be three times above the upper limit of normal. Also, high levels of amylase can be caused by a variety of other disorders including salivary glands, fallopian tubes, perforated ulcers, intestinal ischemia, or chronic renal insufficiency. Computed tomography (CT) can often aid in making the diagnosis and is the most specific test. CT scan will often show enlargement of the pancreas, peripancreatic inflammation, and fluid collections. Management consists of bowel rest, intravenous fluids, and pain control.

Answer A: Alcohol is one of the most common causes of pancreatitis, but it is typically the result of chronic alcoholism. While this patient admits to drinking, he only endorses drinking socially, and approximately three beers per week, making alcohol abuse an unlikely cause of his acute pancreatitis.


Answer B: Recent studies have documented a link between cigarette smoking and acute pancreatitis, but it occurs as a result of long-term, consistent usage, not the social smoking described by this patient.


Answer C: In factitious disorder, the patient fakes or causes a condition for primary gain of being taken care of in the sick role. This patient, however, has no risk factors for factious disorder, such as recurrent hospitalizations. Moreover, his laboratory studies and xanthomas suggest acute pancreatitis secondary to high triglyceride levels.


Answer D: Gallstones are an extremely common cause of pancreatitis. Although this patient should certainly receive a right upper quadrant ultrasound to search for any stones, his chronic hypertriglyceridemia is a more likely cause. Gallstones usually present with right upper quadrant pain preceding the episode of pancreatitis, and the risk factors for gallstones are usually obesity, female sex, fertility, and age over 40.




Bottom Line: Hypertriglyceridemia is a known cause of pancreatitis.  It should be suspected in patients with a family history and by physical exam findings, such as xanthomas.

For more information, see:
"Acute Pancreatitis" on Medscape.

83 of 200

Link:
Methemoglobinemia on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 2-year-old male infant is brought to the emergency department by his mother because she feels that the child appears dusky. History reveals that he may have ingested his grandfather's sublingual nitroglycerin.  Physical examination reveals cyanosis and dyspnea with clear lungs on auscultation.  When obtaining an arterial blood sample, the technician notes that the blood appears brown. The results of the sample yield a normal pO2. The most effective therapy for this patient is

1. 100% oxygen
2. epinephrine
3. furosemide
4. hyperbaric oxygen
5. methylene blue

The correct answer is: E
This patient likely has methemoglobinemia which occurs when hemoglobin is in the oxidized ferric state rather than the normal ferrous state.  When this occurs the hemoglobin will not bind oxygen and results in a brown appearance of the patient's blood.  The symptoms vary depending on the percentage of hemoglobin in the ferric state, but patients may present with dyspnea and cyanosis, headache, confusion, and seizures, and/or metabolic acidosis.  There is a normal pO2 on arterial blood gas.  It may be congenital, which typically presents from birth with asymptomatic cyanosis, or may be acquired due to methemoglobin-inducing medications.  Some common medications associated with methemoglobinemia include benzocaine and other anesthetics, nitrites and nitroglycerin, as well as dapsone.  Well water is a common source of nitrites due to runoff from fertilizers.  It is most common in infants under four months old because the primary protective mechanism against oxidative stress to the erythrocyte is the nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide (NADH) system which is generally not fully matured before four months of age and the NADH methemoglobin reductase activity and concentrations are low.  The most effective and first-line therapy is IV methylene blue which acts to decrease the half-life of methemoglobin.  Methylene blue, however, should be avoided in those with glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase (G6PD) deficiency.

Answer A: 100% oxygen is the best initial therapy while waiting for methylene blue, but it does not reverse the underlying change in hemoglobin's oxidative state.


Answer B: Epinephrine is not used in the treatment of methemoglobinemia. It is used in the advanced cardiac life support algorithm for cardiac arrest, severe asthma, and anaphylaxis.


Answer C: Furosemide is a loop diuretic. It is not used in the management of methemoglobin. It is indicated for the management of edema, resistant hypertension, acute pulmonary edema, hypertensive crisis, increased intracranial pressure, and for hyperkalemia and hypermagnesemia in advanced cardiac life support.


Answer D: Hyperbaric oxygen treatment is a second-line option for patients with severe methemoglobinemia whose condition does not respond to methylene blue or who cannot be treated with methylene blue, such as those with glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase (G6PD) deficiency.




Bottom Line: Methemoglobinemia may present with dyspnea and cyanosis with a normal pO2, headache, confusion, seizures, and/or metabolic acidosis.  First line therapy is with methylene blue.

For more information, see:

Methemoglobinemia on Medscape

84 of 200

Link: "Acute Mesenteric Ischemia" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 68-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of abdominal pain. History reveals that she developed an acute onset of severe, constant, cramping, periumbilical pain one-hour prior to arrival. Past medical history is significant for an irregular heartbeat, high blood pressure, and early-onset dementia. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 160/95mmHg, a heart rate of 75/min, a respiratory rate of 30/min, and a temperature of 37ºC (98.6ºF). Physical examination reveals an elderly woman who appears extremely uncomfortable lying in bed. She has an irregularly irregular heart rhythm and clear lungs by auscultation. Abdominal examination reveals a soft, minimally tender abdomen without rebound, guarding, detectable mass, or surgical scars. Femoral pulses are normal bilaterally with brisk distal perfusion. The most likely etiology of this patient’s current condition is

1. acute myocardial infarction
2. arterial embolism
3. perforated viscus
4. ruptured abdominal aortic aneurysm
5. small bowel obstruction

The correct answer is: B
This elderly female with a history of atrial fibrillation is most likely experiencing acute mesenteric ischemia from an arterial embolus. This patient is high risk to be non-compliant with her medications, one of which is probably warfarin, thus making her high risk for developing a thrombus. Atrial fibrillation (irregularly irregular rhythm) predisposes to left atrial or ventricular thrombi that may become dislodged and travel through the arterial system such as the carotid arteries causing acute ischemic stroke. If the embolus travels through the descending aorta, it may wind up obstructing the celiac artery, superior mesenteric artery (most commonly), inferior mesenteric artery, or a distal extremity artery. Acute blockage of an artery supplying the mesentery and intestines can cause bowel necrosis, multi-organ failure, sepsis, and death if not diagnosed in a timely fashion. It is critical to notice that this patient has pain out of proportion to her physical examination - she appears uncomfortable yet has a relatively benign abdominal examination. Diagnose mesenteric ischemia with an abdominal CT angiography study and arrange for surgical consultation immediately if suspicion is high enough. Treatment is surgical thrombectomy or catheter directed clot lysis via interventional radiology.

Answer A: Abdominal pain in an elderly woman is notorious for an atypical presentation of chest pain and acute coronary syndrome (ACS). There should be a high suspicion of ACS in women, diabetics, and the elderly who present with abdominal pain. An ECG should be obtained early in the workup to rule an ST elevation myocardial infarction out, especially with a patient presenting in atrial fibrillation. However, in this scenario, close attention to the history and physical exam findings make mesenteric ischemia more likely.


Answer C: A perforated viscus (intestine) can occur in any patient population and should be high on the differential in those presenting with an acute abdomen. These patients typically have pain in proportion to their exam, and signs of peritonitis (abdominal rigidity, guarding, and rebound tenderness) due to air and intestinal contents spilling into the peritoneal cavity. Patients with a history of peptic ulcer disease, gastritis, inflammatory bowel disease (Crohn's or ulcerative colitis) or those on chronic steroids are at higher risk of intestinal perforation. This patient does not have any predisposing condition to make this answer choice most likely. In addition, this patient clearly has pain out of proportion to her physical exam making a perforation of the intestine less likely.


Answer D: A ruptured abdominal aortic aneurysm (AAA) is a life threatening emergency. It typically presents as an acute onset of abdominal pain that radiates to the back due to blood spilling into the retroperitoneum. A pulse deficit in the lower extremities or a pulsatile mass may be found on physical exam, which are both notably absent in this scenario. AAA should be on the differential in this patient scenario, however is less likely than mesenteric ischemia based on history of atrial fibrillation and pain out of proportion to exam.


Answer E: A small bowel obstruction (SBO) is a common occurrence in elderly patients and may present in many different ways. Typically, patients complain of acute or subacute abdominal pain, followed by nausea, vomiting, and inability to defecate or pass flatulence. Physical exam typically reveals a distended, tympanitic abdomen with high-pitched bowel sounds. The most common causes of SBO is prior abdominal surgery (adhesions), hernias, inflammatory bowel disease, and malignancy. This patient neither has the history or physical exam findings to make SBO likely (absence of abdominal scars, non-distended abdomen, etc).




Bottom Line: Suspect acute mesenteric ischemia from an arterial embolism in elderly patients with atrial fibrillation who present with abdominal pain that is out of proportion to their physical exam.

COMBANK Insight : Pain out of proportion to exam should make you think of 3 things: 1) mesenteric ischemia, 2) necrotizing fasciitis, and 3) compartment syndrome. If you remember these three conditions and apply them correctly, you will not miss these important, high-yield topics.

For more information, see:
"Acute Mesenteric Ischemia" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 33-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of visual disturbance. Past medical history reveals a positive HIV status, an unknown CD4 cell count, and no current medications. Fundoscopic examination reveals yellow infiltrates around the macula with perivascular exudates and hemorrhages. The most appropriate pharmacological management is

1. ganciclovir

The correct answer is: A
The above findings are consistent with CMV retinitis. CMV retinitis is seen in AIDS patients with CD4 < 50. You should suspect this diagnosis in AIDS patients who present with progressive visual disturbances in the presence of yellow infiltrates with hemorrhage on funduscopic examination. Treatment of this disorder includes oral ganciclovir, foscarnet, and cidofovir.

Link: "Polycystic Ovarian Syndrome" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 24-year-old female presents with irregular menses. History reveals she has been trying to conceive for the past 16 months' duration. She states she has increased hair growth on her chin that has to be removed monthly. Family history reveals an aunt who died of ovarian cancer at age 50. Vital signs reveal a BMI of 30. Physical examination is unremarkable. A urine B-hCG is obtained and is negative. Her LH:FSH ratio is 3:1. Thyroid stimulating hormone is normal. Which of the following is true concerning this patient's most likely diagnosis?

1. clomiphene is unlikely to help her
2. dexamethasone suppression test will confirm the diagnosis
3. her infertility is due to hypothalamic dysfunction
4. she has a decreased risk for ovarian cancer
5. she has an increased risk for endometrial cancer

The correct answer is: E
This patient most likely has polycystic ovarian syndrome (PCOS) due to the typical symptoms (hirsutism, irregular menses and infertility) and her LH/FSH ratio is > 3:1. PCOS increases the risk of endometrial cancer because the patient is in a high estrogen state, which stimulates endometrial hyperplasia. Her infertility is likely due to anovulatory cycles from unopposed estrogen stimulation, thus clomiphene is likely to help.

Answer A: Clomiphene stimulates ovulation and thus can help infertility due to ovarian failure. Clomiphene is the drug of choice for infertility secondary to PCOS.


Answer B: Dexamethasone suppression testing suggests a diagnosis of Cushing's disease, which does not account for the LH/FSH ratio > 3:1.


Answer C: Her infertility is most likely due to anovulatory cycles rather than hypothalamic dysfunction.


Answer D: PCOS does not decrease the risk of ovarian cancer (the cysts are benign).




Bottom Line: PCOS increases the risk of endometrial cancer and infertility due to a high-unopposed estrogen state.

COMBANK Insight : The major features of polycystic ovarian syndrome (PCOS) include menstrual dysfunction, anovulation, and signs of hyperandrogenism.

For more information, see:
"Polycystic Ovarian Syndrome" on Medscape.

87 of 200

Link: Lactose Intolerance" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 16-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of diarrhea for six months' duration. History reveals she has multiple episodes of bulky, high-volume, non-bloody stools daily. Diarrhea typically occurs after meals, and is associated with flatulence, bloating, and mild, diffuse crampy abdominal pain, which is relieved by defecation. Otherwise, she is generally healthy and takes no medications. Physical examination reveals the abdomen is not distended and there is no tenderness to palpation. Bowel sounds are normoactive. A breath hydrogen test is completed and is positive at 25 ppm. The best initial management of this patient is

1. increasing dietary fiber
2. increasing dietary protein
3. reduction of dietary complex carbohydrates
4. reduction of dietary glucose
5. reduction of dietary lactose

The correct answer is: E
This patient most likely has lactose intolerance, which has a fairly high prevalence in the United States. Lactose intolerance is the result of low intestinal lactase levels, which are either secondary to mucosal injury or decreased genetic expression of the lactase enzyme, which hydrolyzes lactose at the intestinal brush border. In patients with lactase deficiency, up to 70% of the lactose passes unabsorbed through the small intestine into the colon. In the colon, lactose is converted into short-chain fatty acids and hydrogen gas by the bacterial flora. The excess hydrogen gas produced by the colonic flora is the basis for the hydrogen breath test that is used to diagnose lactose intolerance. Clinically, patients present with crampy abdominal pain that is usually periumbilical or in the lower quadrants. They will also usually have bloating, flatulence, vomiting, and bulky, frothy and watery stools (the excess lactose in the colon results in an osmotic diarrhea). Diagnosis is made with, as mentioned above, the lactose breath hydrogen test. During the test, the patient is given oral lactose while fasting, a sample breath hydrogen is taken at baseline and at 30 minute intervals for a total of 3 hours. A hydrogen level of 10 ppm (parts per million) is normal, 10-20 ppm is usually indeterminate, > 20 ppm is diagnostic of lactose intolerance. Treatment for these patients primarily consists of decreasing lactose in their diet and maintaining appropriate levels of calcium and vitamin D. Lactase enzyme supplements are also available, but there is not currently enough data to support their efficacy.

Answer A: Increasing dietary fiber has been shown to be useful for patients with diverticulosis and constipation, but plays no role in management of patients with lactose intolerance


Answer B: Increasing dietary protein has no role in the treatment of lactose intolerance, as these patients' primary problem is the inability to digest dietary lactose.


Answers C & D: Reduction of dietary glucose and complex carbohydrates are unnecessary as patients with lactose intolerance are only unable to digest dietary lactose, due to deficiency in the enzyme lactase, but are able to digest other forms of dietary glucose and complex carbohydrates.




Bottom Line: Lactose intolerance is the results of low intestinal levels of the enzyme lactase. Reduction of dietary lactose is the best treatment for reduction of symptoms.

For more information, see:
Lactose Intolerance" on Medscape.


Last Updated: 10/2/2016
88 of 200

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 15-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of mid-back pain. History reveals the pain has been steadily increasing over the last year since she had a growth spurt. Physical examination reveals a rib hump from T4-T9 on the right with forward flexion. Sidebending to the right has no change on the rib hump. What is the most likely diagnosis?

1. T4-T9 left functional scoliosis
2. T4-T9 left structural scoliosis
3. T4-T9 right functional scoliosis
4. T4-T9 right moderate scoliosis
5. T4-T9 right structural scoliosis

The correct answer is: E
Scoliotic curves are named for the spinal convexity. Since scoliosis is a group curve, it is more likely to exhibit Type 1 mechanics with sidebending and rotation occurring in opposite directions. A scoliotic curve that is convex right is sidebent left, which would cause rotation to the right. The right rotation pushes the rib heads posteriorly, resulting in the rib hump. The rib hump is always on the side of convexity. Scoliotic curves are also classified as levoscoliosis (convex left) or dextroscoliosis (convex right). A functional scoliosis is a scoliotic curve that is secondary to another cause, such as a short leg. These curves are flexible and will go away with sidebending toward the convexity. In a structural scoliosis, the rib hump will remain as in the above scenario because the curve is rigid.

Answer A: This patient exhibits a right scoliosis because the rib hump is on the right. Also, it is a structural curve since it does not resolve with sidebending toward the convexity.


Answer B: T4-T9 left structural scoliosis would be correct if the rib hump were on the left, not the right. Scoliotic curves are named for the side of convexity, which is opposite of the side to which the curve sidebends.


Answer C: T4-T9 right functional scoliosis would be the correct answer choice if the curve resolved with sidebending right, indicating a functional curve. However, the curve did not change with sidebending right, therefore, it is a structural scoliosis.


Answer D: Moderate scoliosis is defined based on the Cobb angle of the curve. A Cobb angle between 20-45 degrees is considered to be moderate scoliosis. A radiograph is necessary to determine the Cobb angle and, therefore, to determine the severity of the curve.




Bottom Line: Scoliosis is named for the side of the convexity.

COMBANK Insight : Scoliosis is a common topic that may show up as an OMM question, a respiratory or cardiac question, or even a surgery question.

Link: Pathophysiology and clinical features of primary aldosteronism


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 53-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of headache and muscle weakness. Review of systems reveals increased urination without increased thirst. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 146/96 mmHg. Physical examination reveals tetany. Hyperaldosteronism is suspected as the primary diagnosis. Which of the following laboratory analysis patterns is most likely to be seen?

1. hyperkalemia, hyponatremia, metabolic alkalosis, hypomagnesemia and an increased aldosterone to plasma renin activity ratio
2. hypokalemia, hypernatremia, metabolic acidosis, hypomagnesemia and an increased aldosterone to plasma renin activity ratio
3. hypokalemia, hypernatremia, metabolic alkalosis, hypomagnesemia and a decreased aldosterone to plasma renin activity ratio
4. hypokalemia, hypernatremia, metabolic alkalosis, hypomagnesemia and an increased aldosterone to plasma renin activity ratio
5. hypokalemia, hyponatremia, metabolic alkalosis, hypomagnesemia and an increased aldosterone to plasma renin activity ratio

The correct answer is: D
The most likely diagnosis is hyperaldosteronism. Aldosterone is secreted from the zona glomerulosa of the adrenal cortex in response to decreased circulating plasma volume, hyperkalemia or hyponatremia. Pathologic hyperaldosteronism is usually secondary to adrenocortical hyperplasia but can also result from a unilateral adrenal adenoma, also referred to as Conn’s syndrome. Patients often present with hypertension, increased urination, muscle weakness, parasthesias or headache. Physical exam may reveal tetany, peripheral edema or diastolic hypertension.

Labs in patients with hyperaldosteronism will show hypokalemia, hypernatremia, metabolic alkalosis, hypomagnesemia and an increased aldosterone to plasma renin activity ratio. The latter occurs because aldosterone is being inappropriately secreted while renin is being suppressed via negative feedback inhibition.

The diagnosis is supported with CT or MRI scanning which may reveal an adrenal mass or enlarged adrenal glands consistent with adrenal hyperplasia. Adrenal tumors are best treated with laparoscopic or open adrenalectomy while bilateral adrenal hyperplasia is best treated with spironolactone or other aldosterone receptor blockers.

Answer A: Hypokalemia and hypernatremia, not hyperkalemia and hyponatremia, occur in patients with hyperaldosteronism because of increase sodium reabsorption.


Answer B: Metabolic alkalosis, not acidosis, occurs in patients with hyperaldosteronism as a result of increased bicarbonate reabsorption.


Answer C: Aldosterone: renin ratios are increased in patients with hyperaldosteronism because aldosterone is being inappropriately secreted while renin is being suppressed via negative feedback inhibition.


Answer E: Hypernatremia, not hyponatremia occurs in patients with hyperaldosteronism because of increase sodium reabsorption.


Bottom Line: Labs in patients with hyperaldosteronism will show hypokalemia, hypernatremia, metabolic alkalosis, hypomagnesemia and an increased aldosterone to plasma renin activity ratio.



COMBANK Insight : COMLEX Level 2 CE Insight: Interpreting lab values for specific pathologies is an important concept that will allow you to correctly diagnose certain conditions.

For more information, see:
Pathophysiology and clinical features of primary aldosteronism


Last Updated: 6/20/2017

Link: "Treatment of hairy cell leukemia" on UpToDate


Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 73-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of weakness, fatigue, and malaise. Vital signs reveals a temperature of 98.3ºF, a blood pressure of 143/83 mmHg, a heart rate of 89/min, and a respiratory rate of 19/min. Physical examination reveals splenomegaly, and no lymphadenopathy. Laboratory analysis and peripheral blood smear are obtained and are abnormal. Flow cytometry is obtained for confirmation and confirms a group of cells that stain positive for tartrate-resistant acid phosphatase. Which of the following if the most appropriate pharmacological management of this patient's condition?

1. all-trans retinoic acid
2. imatinib
3. interferon-alpha
4. nucleoside analogs
5. splenectomy

The correct answer is: D
The most likely diagnosis is hairy cell leukemia. Hairy cell leukemia is a malignant disorder of well-differentiated B-lymphocytes. It is a rare disease that accounts for less than 2% of adult leukemia cases which usually affects the elderly male population. HCL often presents with splenomegaly, weakness, fatigue, petechiae, abdominal pain, early satiety and weight loss. Symptoms are similar to those seen in patients with CLL except that HCL patients rarely have lymphadenopathy. HCL is diagnosed based on history, physical exam and labs but the diagnosis is confirmed through the identification of hairy cells (see below) in the blood, blood marrow or spleen. TRAP (tartrate resistant acid phosphatase) staining and flow cytometry are helpful in distinguishing the pathognomonic hairy cells. Peripheral smear shows hairy cells, also described as mononuclear cells with abundant pale cytoplasm and cytoplasmic projections. Ten percent of patients with HCL will never require treatment as they have a benign disease course. However, treatment should begin when patients become symptomatic or cytopenic. Nucleoside analogs are the initial treatment of choice.



Answer A: ATRA (all-trans retinoic acid) is used to treat acute promyelocytic leukemia (APL).


Answer B: Imatinib is the Philadelphia chromosome inhibitor also known as gleevec, which is used in the treatment of CML.


Answers C & E: Splenectomy and interferon-alpha may improve blood counts in patients with hairy cell leukemia but nucleoside analogs are the initial treatment of choice because they have been shown to effectively induce remission.




Bottom Line: Nucleoside analogs are the initial treatment of choice for patients with hairy cell leukemia.

For more information, see:
"Treatment of hairy cell leukemia" on UpToDate

Link: Medication-Induced Dystonic Reactions on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Psychiatry
Discipline: Psychiatry

A schizophrenic female has been admitted to the inpatient unit of the hospital for 6 days' duration. She has been gradually improving with less hallucinations and paranoid delusions while her medications are being adjusted. She suddenly begins to develop involuntary contraction of her neck muscles, involuntary eye blinking, diplopia, and an upward deviation of her eyes.  


Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. acute dystonia
2. lithium toxicity
3. meningitis
4. neuroleptic malignant syndrome
5. phencyclidine intoxication

The correct answer is: A
Acute Dystonia is a disorder of involuntary muscle contraction that develops within days of starting antipsychotic medications, often involving torticollis and oculogyric crisis. It is a result of the dopamine blockage actions of the antipsychotic medications

Answer B: The most common symptoms of lithium toxicity include nausea, emesis, diarrhea, ataxia, confusion, polyuria, seizures, and coma. This is not the clinical picture presented here. Also, there was no mention of mood disorder or treatment for Bipolar disorder, so it should not be suspected that this patient is being treated with Lithium.


Answer C: Patients with meningitis will present typically with fever, headache, nuchal rigidity, or altered mental status.


Answer D: Neuroleptic malignant syndrome is life threatening condition that occurs from the use of antipsychotic medications and a result of their dopamine blockade effects. Patients will have muscular rigidity, dystonia, obtundation, and autonomic instability with fever, unstable blood pressure, or unstable heart rate.


Answer E: Nystagmus, hallucinations, paranoia, euphoria, aggressive and suicidal behaviors are effects of phencyclidine (PCP intoxication). The patient in the above scenario has been hospitalized for five days, therefore it would be unlikely she has had access to or been using illicit substances.




Bottom Line: Acute reactions of muscular contractions in the context of treatment of a psychotic disorder with antipsychotic medication are most likely due to acute dystonia.

For more information, see:
Medication-Induced Dystonic Reactions on Medscape

Diphenhydramine is the treatment of choice for acute dystonia – its anticholinergic properties are very effective in relieving the muscular contractions, especially in the case of oculogyric crisis that occurs and causes diplopia.

Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 64-year-old male presents for a follow up appointment from a hospital stay for fatigue. He states that he was hospitalized two days prior for an episode of diabetic ketoacidosis, which was the initial presentation of his diabetes. He mentions that during his hospitalization he was diagnosed with hemochromatosis. He denies any past medical history and he just started taking insulin for his newly diagnosed diabetes. He states that he drinks 6 cans of beer daily and has for the last 50 years.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
Which of the following sets of laboratory results is most likely in this patient?

1. increased ferritin, increased TIBC, increased transferrin saturation
2. increased ferritin, decreased TIBC, increased transferrin saturation
3. increased ferritin, decreased TIBC, decreased transferrin saturation
4. decreased ferritin, increased TIBC, decreased transferrin saturation
5. decreased ferritin, increased TIBC, increased transferrin saturation

The correct answer is: B
Hemochromatosis is a disorder of excess iron absorption in the GI tract. It can be genetic or acquired by alcoholism or excess blood transfusions. It presents with increased skin pigmentation, diabetes, micronodular cirrhosis and congestive heart failure. Laboratory analysis in hemochromatosis will reveal an elevated ferritin, decreased total iron binding capacity (decreased TIBC) but an increased transferrin saturation.

Answer A: TIBC is decreased in hemochromatosis.


Answer C: Iron saturation is increased in hemochromatosis because of the excess iron in the body and the decreased TIBC.


Answers D & E: Ferritin is increased in hemochromatosis as an attempt by the body to store all of the excess iron.




Bottom Line: Ferritin and transferrin saturation are increased in hemochromatosis whereas TIBC is decreased.

Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 64-year-old male presents for a follow up appointment from a hospital stay for fatigue. This patient should be screened regularly by monitoring

2. annual colonoscopies

The correct answer is: A
Patients with hemochromatosis are at an increased risk for developing hepatocellular carcinoma and just like patients with hepatitis B and C, they should be screened with alpha-fetoprotein levels.

Link: "Wegner Granulomatosis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Bleeding
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 63-year-old male presents to the emergency department after coughing up several teaspoons of blood and blood-tinged sputum. History reveals associated shortness of breath a sinus infection for the past month that has failed to improve with antibiotics. He smokes one pack of cigarettes per day and occasionally drinks alcohol. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 144/90 mmHg, a pulse of 80/min, a temperature of 38.0ºC (100.4ºF), and a respiratory rate of 14/min. Physical examination reveals decreased breath sounds over the right middle lobe and left lower lobe as well as dullness to percussion in these areas. Mild wheezing is appreciated over the central anterior chest wall. A chest radiograph reveals several large nodular lesions, some with central cavitation, and areas of diffuse alveolar opacity. A urinalysis is positive for blood and sediment reveals several red blood cell casts. A C-ANCA is positive. The most appropriate treatment is

1. ampicillin-sulbactam
2. corticosteroids
3. cyclophosphamide plus corticosteroids
4. dialysis
5. intravenous immunoglobulin

The correct answer is: C
The patient in this vignette has hemoptysis, hematuria, and a positive c-ANCA, which is consistent with the diagnosis of granulomatosis with polyangiitis (GPA). GPA, formerly known as Wegener’s granulomatosis, is a necrotizing vasculitis of small vessels, affecting predominantly the upper airways, tracheobronchial tree, and renal glomeruli. The first-line treatment for GPA is a combination of cyclophosphamide and corticosteroids for induction of remission. Rituximab can be used in place of cyclophosphamide with high dose corticosteroids.

Answers A & D & E: Antibiotics, dialysis, and intravenous immunoglobulin are not indicated in the treatment of Wegener's granulomatosis; however, patients may require dialysis in late disease if it leads to renal failure.


Answer B: As described above, the first line treatment for GPA is a combination of cyclophosphamide and corticosteroids. Since the introduction of cyclophosphamide in the treatment of this disease, mortality has substantial improved. Corticosteroids alone only increase survival by a mean of approximately eight months and are not the appropriate choice.


Answer F: GPA must be distinguished from Goodpasture syndrome, another inflammatory disease with both renal and pulmonary manifestations. Goodpasture syndrome requires more aggressive therapy with plasmapheresis in addition to cyclophosphamide and corticosteroids. Goodpasture syndrome is a rare autoimmune disease characterized by the triad of (1) diffuse pulmonary hemorrhage, (2) rapidly progressive glomerulonephritis, and (3) the presence of circulating anti-basement membrane autoantibodies. A positive c-ANCA and mild pulmonary and renal manifestations point towards the diagnosis of GPA, not Goodpasture syndrome.




Bottom Line: The treatment of GPA is with a combination of cyclophosphamide and corticosteroids.

For more information, see:
"Wegner Granulomatosis" on Medscape.

Link: "Acute severe asthma exacerbations in children: Intensive care unit management" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
An 18-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaint of shortness of breath and wheezing for the last few hours. History reveals he has been to the emergency department twice in the last three weeks for similar complaints. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 110/80 mmHg, a pulse of 120/min, a temperature of 37.7°C (100°F), and a respiratory rate of 32/min. Physical examination reveals expiratory wheezing and use of his accessory respiratory muscles. An albuterol treatment is administered and he experiences mild improvement. An arterial blood gas while on 100% O2 at 4 L/min nasal cannula is shown below.

pH   7.40 
pCO2           40 mm Hg
pO2  60 mm Hg          
HCO3  22 mmol/L

The next best step in management is

1. 100% oxygen via face mask
2. admission and repeat albuterol nebulizer
3. discharge and oral corticosteroids
4. intravenous antibiotics
5. prepare intubation equipment

The correct answer is: E
This patient with an acute asthma exacerbation that has been occurring for the last few hours is becoming tired. In an acute exacerbation, patients should have respiratory alkalosis due to increased ventilatory rate. As they tire, they will develop respiratory acidosis. This patient’s PCO2 should be lower than the normal value of 40 mm Hg, indicating that he is currently at the cross over between these two states. Respiratory failure may be imminent, and intubation should be performed as needed.

Answer A: This patient has impending respiratory failure, necessitating intubation. Switching from nasal cannula to face mask oxygen would be insufficient to treat his condition.


Answer B: This patient should receive continued nebulization, but he is not respiring sufficiently as evidenced by his normal pCO2. More interventions are required.


Answer C: Discharge from the hospital in this state of pending hypoventilation would be dangerous.


Answer D: This patient does not present with signs of infection, which would require antibiotics.




Bottom Line: Asthma exacerbations are expected to produce respiratory alkalosis as a result of hyperventilation. In an asthma attack before respiratory collapse, the pH normalizes because of tiring of respiratory muscles.

For more information, see:
"Acute severe asthma exacerbations in children: Intensive care unit management" on UpToDate.
"Emergency airway management in acute severe asthma" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 5-year-old male presents to the emergency department with fever, headache and vomiting for 24 hours' duration. History reveals recent placement of a shunt for hydrocephalus. Complete blood cell count reveals a leukocytosis with a left shift. Which of the following is most likely associated with the patient's infection?

1. coagulase-negative Staphylococcus
2. viral meningitis
3. gram-negative bacteria
4. viral encephalitis
5. acid-fast positive cerebrospinal fluid

The correct answer is: A
The leukocytosis with a left shift (excess neutrophils) indicates a bacterial infection. The most likely source of infection is the child's hydrocephalus shunt. The most common bacterial infection for a hydrocephalus shunt is Staphylococcus epidermidis which is gram-positive and coagulase-negative.

Answers B & D: Viral infections present with a leukocytosis with a right shift (excess lymphocytes).


Answer C: Gram-negative bacteria are not a common cause of hydrocephalus shunt infections.


Answer E: Acid-fast CSF would be suggestive of Tuberculosis meningitis.




Bottom Line: The most common bacterial infection for a hydrocephalus shunt is Staphylococcus epidermidis which is a gram-positive coagulase-negative bacteria.

Link: "Septic Shock" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Fever
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 50-year-old female presents to the Emergency Department with the complaints of fever, body aches, and left-sided back pain for 1 day. Past medical history is remarkable for poorly controlled diabetes and hypertension. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 88/55 mmHg, a heart rate of 120/min, a respiratory rate of 30/min, an oxygen saturation of 92% on room air, and a temperature of 39°C (102.2°F). Physical examination reveals an ill-appearing female with an unremarkable HEENT exam, a supple neck, clear breath sounds, and no abdominal tenderness. There is severe tenderness with percussion of the left costovertebral angle. Laboratory analysis is remarkable for a serum leukocytosis of 15,000 cells/uL and >100 WBC/HPF detected on urinalysis. After a 2-liter sodium chloride bolus, the patient’s blood pressure is 85/50 mmHg with no change in her heart rate. The most likely diagnosis is

1. multiple organ dysfunction syndrome
2. sepsis
3. septic shock
4. severe sepsis
5. systemic inflammatory response syndrome

The correct answer is: C
Septic shock is defined hypotension resulting in inadequate tissue/organ perfusion in the setting of sepsis that persists after appropriate fluid resuscitation. In adults, a typical appropriate fluid challenge is 2 liters of crystalloid (saline or lactated ringers). Sepsis is defined as the presence of SIRS (requires 2/4 criteria met) and evidence of infection. This patient has septic shock due to meeting sepsis criteria and continued hypotension despite fluid resuscitation.

Answer A: Multiple organ dysfunction syndrome (MODS) is a syndrome characterized by the development of progressive dysfunction in two or more organ systems that is induced by a variety of acute insults to the body. MODS is an ongoing process rather than a single event and can vary widely from a mild organ dysfunction to irreversible organ failure. This patient likely has acute pyelonephritis as the insult to her body and physiology. However, she is only manifesting one organ system dysfunction: hypotension. Progression to altered mental status, renal insufficiency, or evidence of disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC) would indicate another organ system dysfunction.


Answer B: Sepsis is defined as the presence of the systemic inflammatory response syndrome (SIRS) [requires 2/4 criteria met: 1) temperature < 36ºC or > 38ºC, 2) respiratory rate > 20/min (or PaCO2 <32 mmHg), 3) heart rate > 90/min, and 4) WBC < 4,000 cells/uL or >12,000 cells/uL (or with >10% bands)] and evidence of infection. This patient is presenting with 4 out of 4 SIRS criteria: tachypnea, tachycardia, leukocytosis, and fever. She also has evidence of an infection by physical exam and urinalysis. By the available data, this patient meets criteria for sepsis. However, she also has hypotension and has not been responsive to initial fluid resuscitation; therefore, she meets criteria for septic shock.


Answer D: Severe sepsis is defined as sepsis complicated by end-organ dysfunction, as signaled by altered mental status, hypotension, elevated creatinine, or evidence of disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC). This patient is presenting with 4 out of 4 SIRS criteria: tachypnea, tachycardia, leukocytosis, and fever. In addition, she has a focus of infection (urine) and is hypotensive. This patient is further down the spectrum of illness than severe sepsis due to her persistent hypotension.


Answer E: Only two of the four following criteria are required to make the diagnosis of SIRS. SIRS is a normal reaction to an insult/stress on the body and it is sensitive for detecting a stress response, but is nonspecific to the cause. SIRS does not always equal infection. For example, pancreatitis can cause SIRS. This patient has SIRS, although has a better definable clinical syndrome due to her hypotension despite fluids.




Bottom Line: Septic shock is defined hypotension resulting in inadequate tissue/organ perfusion in the setting of sepsis that persists after appropriate fluid resuscitation.

For more information, see:
"Septic Shock" on Medscape.


Last Updated: 10/2/2016

Link: "Sarcoidosis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 33-year-old African-American female presents to the office with the complaints of blurred vision and weakness for several months' duration. Physical examination reveals nodules on the anterior portion of her legs that are painful to palpation. A chest radiograph is obtained and reveals bilateral enlargement of perihilar lymph nodes. Which of the following associated findings is most likely to be seen?

1. caseous granulomas with giant cells
2. decreased FEV1/FVC
3. diffuse alveolar damage
4. elevated serum ACE level
5. hyperplasia of mucus secreting glands

The correct answer is: D
The most likely diagnosis is sarcoidosis. This is an idiopathic disease most commonly occurring in the African-American population. It is commonly found incidentally on chest radiograph but may also present with systemic symptoms such as fever, cough, and weakness. In the above scenario the patient presents with blurred vision is a manifestation of anterior uveitis and the nodules on her legs are characteristic of erythema nodosum. These are two classic extrapulmonary findings of sarcoidosis. Sarcoidosis is associated with characteristic chest radiograph findings. In the first stage, bilateral hilar adenopathy is appreciated. In the second stage, the hilar adenopathy is still present as are infiltrates. The third stage reveals lung involvement only with resolution of the hilar adenopathy. Lastly, the fourth stage reveals chronic lung scarring. Non-caseating granulomas, as seen on biopsy, are highly suggestive of the diagnosis as the diagnosis is primarily made clinically. Elevated serum ACE levels are appreciated in up to 50% of patients with sarcoidosis.

Answer A: Sarcoidosis is associated with non-caseating granulomas on tissue biopsy.


Answer B: Sarcoidosis is a restrictive lung disease, characterized by normal to slightly elevated FEV1/FVC ratios. Remember, FEV1/FVC ratios are decreased in obstructive lung processes.


Answer C: Diffuse alveolar damage is associated with respiratory distress syndrome.


Answer E: Hyperplasia of mucus glands is associated with chronic bronchitis. It can be quantified by measuring the Reid index.




Bottom Line: Sarcoidosis is a systemic disease with a restrictive pattern in the lungs. Increased ACE levels are a common finding.

COMBANK Insight : It is important to distinguish the differences between rashes and to know their associations. The rash of erythema nodosum consists of red, painful, tender lumps most commonly located in the front of the legs below the knees. Erythema nodosum is often seen with the following conditions: sarcoidosis, bacterial infections (Streptococcal, TB, Yersinia, Salmonella, Legionella), fungal infections (Coccidioidomycosis, Histoplasma, Blastomycosis), drugs reactions (sulfonamides, oral contraceptives), enteropathies (Crohn’s disease, ulcerative colitis), Hodgkin’s lymphoma, Behcet's disease, and pregnancy.

For more information, see:
"Sarcoidosis" on Medscape.

Link: "Type 2 Diabetes Mellitus" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 37-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of polyuria, polydipsia and weight gain. He tells you he gets little exercise and eats a poor diet. Physical examination is unremarkable. Initial labs reveal a fasting blood glucose of 156 and 164 on two separate occasions, a HbA1C of 12.2%, a BUN of 45 and creatinine of 4.7. The best initial treatment for this patient’s condition is

1. diet modification and weight loss
2. diet modification, weight loss and exercise
3. diet modification, weight loss, exercise and insulin
4. diet modification, weight loss, exercise and metformin
5. diet modification, weight loss, exercise, insulin and metformin

The correct answer is: C
The most likely diagnosis is type II diabetes mellitus. This patient has symptoms of hyperglycemia. Polyuria occurs secondary to increased glucose levels being processed by the kidneys, which leads to an osmotic diuresis. Polydipsia occurs secondary to the osmotic diuresis as an attempt to rehydrate. Type II DM is diagnosed with a fasting glucose level greater than 126 on two separate occasions, a random glucose greater than 200 with symptoms of hyperglycemia or with a two-hour postprandial glucose level greater than 200. The American Diabetes Association (ADA) now suggests using a HbA1C of greater than 6.5% as criteria for diagnosing diabetes. The preprandial glucose goal for diabetics is 90-130 while the postprandial goal level is < 180. According to the ADA the goal HbA1C for diabetics is < 7.0%. Lifestyle interventions such as weight loss, exercise and diet modifications are considered first line treatments. The cornerstone of medical therapy for diabetes is metformin. It is usually well tolerated and the most common side effects are gastrointestinal in nature. Severe renal dysfunction (GFR less than 30 mL/min) is a contraindication to the use of metformin. Abnormal BUN and Cr can also be indicative of renal dysfunction that precludes the use of metformin. The American Diabetes Association recommends using insulin as a first line medication in patients with a HbA1C greater than 10.0%.

Answers A & B & D & E: Diet modification, weight loss and exercise are considered first line treatments for most patients with diabetes. However, in patients with a HbA1C greater than 10.0%, these treatments are unlikely to be effective and insulin therapy is recommended. Furthermore, metformin should not be used in patients with renal dysfunction.




Bottom Line: Insulin is considered a first line medication in patients with a HbA1C greater than 10.0%.

COMBANK Insight : Diabetes is a commonly tested subject. As new guidelines are created, new questions on COMLEX exams are written. Your board review books may lag behind current recommendations by a couple of years. Diabetes and cancer prevention are two subjects that you should be familiar with, even going beyond knowing what is in the board review books.

For more information, see:
"Type 2 Diabetes Mellitus" on Medscape.
"Initial management of blood glucose in adults with type 2 diabetes mellitus" on UpToDate.

Link: USPSTF Lung Cancer Screening


Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 53-year-old male with a history of hypertension and smoking presents to his primary care office for routine follow-up. He has a 40 pack-year smoking history and is currently smoking. Further questioning reveals he is in the pre-contemplative stages of quitting. He does not have any new complaints at today's visit. Screening for lung cancer should begin when this patient is

1. now, he is overdue
2. 55-years old
3. 60-years old
4. 65-years old
5. 80-years old

The correct answer is: B
The new 2014 guidelines according to the U.S. Preventive Services Task Force (USPSTF) state that patients should be screened between the age of 55-80 years old that have a 30-pack year smoking history and that are either currently smoking or have quit in the past 15 years. An annual low-dose CT scan of the chest is the screening method.

Answers A & C & D & E: According to the U.S. Preventive Services Task Force (USPSTF) screening should start in patients that are 55-80 years old who have a 30 pack-year smoking history and currently smoke or have quit within the past 15 years.




Bottom Line: The new 2014 guidelines for lung cancer screening is low dose CT of the chest in patients that are 55-80 years old and that are currently smoking or have quit in the past 15 years.

USPSTF Lung Cancer Screening

102 of 200

Link: "Turner Syndrome Workup" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 16-year-old female presents to the office with concerns she has never had a menstrual period. History reveals she is sexually active with one partner and does not use contraception. Physical examination reveals normal pubic hair but no breast development. Laboratory analysis reveals elevated serum FSH. Urine beta-hCG is negative. An abdominal ultrasound is obtained and reveals a horseshoe kidney. The next best step in management is

1. CT of abdomen and pelvis
2. karyotyping
3. serum ß-hcg
4. serum prolactin
5. serum TSH

The correct answer is: B
This patient presents with primary amenorrhea that is likely due to premature ovarian failure. While there are several causes of premature ovarian failure, one must rule out chromosomal abnormalities in a 16-year-old female. Turner’s syndrome is the most likely diagnosis given the constellation of elevated FSH, lack of breast development, and horseshoe kidney. Pubic and axillary hair development is typically normal in patients with Turner syndrome.

Answer A: CT Abdomen/Pelvis could be useful for identifying Müllerian structures; however, karyotyping would be the next best step given the many characteristics of Turner syndrome present in this patient. One would expect to find ovarian streaks in a Turner’s patient.


Answer C: Serum beta-hCG is not indicated given the negative urine beta-hcg.


Answer D: Serum prolactin would be the next step in the diagnostic work-up should the FSH have come back low or normal. This would be indicative of a malfunction of the hypothalamic-pituitary axis.


Answer E: Turner’s syndrome patients may be affected by hypothyroidism. Evaluation of thyroid function with TSH would be indicated once a diagnosis of Turner’s syndrome has been established.




Bottom Line: Turner’s syndrome characteristics include amenorrhea secondary to premature ovarian failure. This is evidenced by elevated serum FSH. Other characteristics of Turner’s syndrome include horseshoe kidney, webbed neck, wide spaced nipples, lack of breast development, lymphedema, bicuspid aortic valve, and coarctation of the aorta.

For more information, see:
"Turner Syndrome Workup" on Medscape.

Link: "Pleural Effusion" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 55-year-old male with a known history of congestive heart failure presents to the office for worsening shortness of breath. History reveals he is unable to walk more than a block without having to stop to catch his breath but he does not have symptoms at rest. He admits that he has not taken his hydrochlorothiazide and furosemide for the past week. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 140/80 mmHg, a pulse of 90/min, a respiratory rate of 14/min, and an oxygen saturation of 98% on room air. Physical examination reveals bilateral crackles at the lung bases with diminished breath sounds. Auscultation of heart reveals S1, S2, and S3. His abdomen is soft and non-tender. He has 1+ edema bilaterally in the lower extremities. A chest radiography is obtained and revealed cephalization of the pulmonary vessels and bilateral pleural effusions. A thoracentesis will most likely reveal which of the following laboratory results?

1. pleural LDH > 90 U/L (NL 45-90 U/L)
2. pleural LDH >900 U/L (NL 45-90 U/L)
3. pleural LDH : serum LDH >0.6
4. pleural protein : serum protein >0.5
5. pleural protein : serum protein <0.5

The correct answer is: E
This patient has a pleural effusion from a congestive heart failure (CHF) exacerbation. Pleural effusions can be transudative, caused by an increase in hydrostatic pressure with an intact capillary network, or exudative, caused by inflammatory or invasive processes that increase capillary permeability. Transudative effusions are protein poor filtrates of serum, whereas exudates are protein rich. The most common causes of transudative effusions are volume overloaded states: congestive heart failure, cirrhosis, and nephrotic syndrome. Exudative effusions are seen with pneumonias and cancer. Sampling of the effusion and comparison to serum laboratories can provide insight into the etiology of the effusion. Light’s criteria states that an effusion is exudative if the ratio of pleural protein to serum protein is >0.5, the ratio of pleural to serum LDH is > 0.6, or if the absolute pleural LDH is > 2/3 of the upper limit of normal for serum. Generally speaking, bilateral pleural effusions with symptoms of a CHF exacerbation can be managed by a trial of diuresis without thoracentesis or sampling of the pleural fluid.

Answers A & B & C & D: These choices are all consistent with an exudative pleural effusion, not transudative.




Bottom Line: Light’s criteria states that an exudative pleural effusion has either: a ratio of pleural protein to serum protein >0.5, ratio of pleural to serum LDH > 0.6, absolute pleural LDH is > 2/3 of the upper limit of normal for serum.

COMBANK Insight : COMLEX Insight Light’s criteria for pleural effusions and the serum albumin ascites gradient are similar concepts that can help narrow down a differential diagnosis based on the composition of the fluid sample.

For more information, see:
"Pleural Effusion" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 5-day-old female is brought to the office by her mother with blood-tinged vaginal discharge. History reveals this began after being discharged from the hospital following an uncomplicated spontaneous vaginal delivery. The mother states the child is exclusively breast-fed. She has been feeding well and there have been no other abnormalities. The most appropriate course of action is

1. admission to the hospital
2. consult a hematologist
3. contact Child Protective Services to report suspected abuse
4. hemoglobin and hematocrit
5. reassurance this is normal

The correct answer is: E
Pseudo-menses is a common finding in newborn females. Vaginal bleeding occurs due to endometrial sloughing that takes place following the rapid decline of circulating maternal estrogens that were once present in utero. It is appropriate in this scenario to reassure the mother of the benign nature of this finding. Bleeding is normal and usually resolves within 10 days following birth.

Answers A & D: Further work-up and testing is not required.


Answer B: No consultations are required.


Answer C: Contacting Child Protective Services is not appropriate, as there is no information that would imply abuse.




Bottom Line: Vaginal bleeding is common in newborn females and is a normal, self-limiting event that usually resolves within 10 days following birth.

Link: "Clinical presentation and diagnosis of urinary incontinence" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 73-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of urinary incontinence. History reveals that she experiences a sudden and strong urge to void. This is followed by leaking urine prior to reaching the toilet, then complete bladder emptying with conscious voiding. She denies leaking urine when laughing, coughing, or sneezing. Past medical history is significant for hypertension, which is treated with lifestyle modification. The most likely etiology of her condition is

1. bladder outlet obstruction
2. detrusor-sphincter dyssynergia
3. detrusor overactivity
4. impaired detrusor activity
5. impaired urethral sphincter function

The correct answer is: C
Urinary incontinence occurs when there is involuntary leakage of urine. Women who have had children, have particular comorbid conditions (i.e., diabetes, neurologic disease), and are over age 65 years are at increased risk for incontinence. Urge incontinence occurs when involuntary leakage is accompanied by urgency and need to “rush to the toilet.” Stress incontinence is the involuntary leakage of urine with effort, exertion, sneezing, or coughing. Mixed incontinence is combined urge and stress incontinence. Incomplete emptying can result in overflow incontinence. The patient described above is experiencing urge incontinence, which is most commonly the result of detrusor overactivity.

Answers A & B: Detrusor-sphincter dyssynergia can result in bladder outlet obstruction. This results in overflow incontinence. Patients with this condition will describe dribbling and/or continuous leakage associated with being unable to completely empty the bladder.


Answer D: Impaired detrusor activity can result in incomplete bladder emptying. This in turn results in overflow incontinence.


Answer E: Impaired urethral sphincter function results in stress incontinence. It occurs when an increase in intraabdominal pressure overcomes sphincter closure mechanisms.




Bottom Line: Urge incontinence is the result of detrusor overactivity and is characterized by a strong, sudden urge to void preceding leakage of urine.

For more information, see:
"Clinical presentation and diagnosis of urinary incontinence" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 51-year-old African-American male presents to the office with the complaint of a rash. The patient has a history of systemic lupus erythematosus for 20 years with lung and renal involvement within the past 4 years. History reveals the patient has been treated for primary immune thrombocytopenic purpura with steroids and intravenous immunoglobulin several times for the past 9 months with no success. Physical examination reveals a purpuric rash over the lower extremities. Laboratory analysis reveals a Hgb of 11 g/dL, leukocytes 5,500/mm3, and platelet count of 9,000/mm3.  The most appropriate treatment is

1. autologous hematopoietic stem cell transplantation
2. fresh frozen plasma
3. granulocyte colony stimulating factor
4. splenectomy
5. vasopressin

The correct answer is: D
This patient has asymptomatic primary immune thrombocytopenic purpura which is an autoimmune destruction of platelets by the reticuloendothelial system.  The patient has not responded to first-line therapy which includes steroids, anti-Rh(D), or intravenous immunoglobulin therapy.  For patients who have persistent disease >6 months, splenectomy is the best option.

Answer A: Autologous hematopoietic stem cell transplantation is an investigational procedure for refractory cases of primary immune thrombocytopenic purpura that have not responded to splenectomy.


Answer B: Fresh frozen plasma will not correct for a low platelet count or help for cases of symptomatic primary immune thrombocytopenic purpura.


Answer C: Granulocyte colony stimulating factor is not indicated for primary immune thrombocytopenic purpura.


Answer E: Vasopressin is indicated for von Willebrand's disease by inducing endothelial release of von Willebrand factor.




Bottom Line: Treat primary immune thrombocytopenic purpura first with steroids, IVIG, and/or anti-Rh(D).  For refractory cases, splenectomy is the treatment of choice.

For more information, see:
The Massachusetts General Hospital Handbook of Internal Medicine. 4th Ed. Lippincott Williams & Wilkins: Philadelphia, PA 2011, pp. 5-7 - 5-8.
