Link: "Kawasaki Disease " on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 5-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaints of several days of high fever and rash. History reveals he was previously healthy until six days prior when he developed a fever of 104 ºF (40 ºC). Vital signs reveal a temperature of 103 ºF (39.4 ºC), a blood pressure of 130/70 mmHg, a heart rate of 120 beats/min, and a respiratory rate of 26/min. Physical examination reveals an ill appearing child with a generalized erythematous rash, a densely erythematous tongue, bilateral conjunctival injection, and tender cervical lymphadenopathy. The most appropriate treatment is

1. high dose aspirin and intravenous immunoglobulin
2. hospitalization with supportive care only
3. intravenous ceftriaxone
4. intravenous vancomycin
5. transfer to burn center for further care

The correct answer is: A
Kawasaki's disease (KD) is an acute febrile vasculitic syndrome of early childhood also known as mucocutaneous lymph node syndrome. KD is a clinical diagnosis based on fever lasting longer than 5 days and 4 of the 5 following features: 1) Peripheral extremity involvement including erythema of the palms and soles, or desquamation of the finger and toe tips. 2) Polymorphous rash that is usually generalized. 3) Oropharyngeal involvement such as erythema, fissuring, crusting of the lips, or strawberry tongue. 4) Bilateral, nonexudative, painless conjunctival injection. 5) Acute nonpurulent cervical lymphadenopathy, usually unilateral. The patient in this scenario has prolonged fever, conjunctival injection, strawberry tongue, rash, and lymphadenopathy. He therefore meets clinical criteria for KD. Treatment consists of hospitalization, high dose aspirin, and intravenous immunoglobulin (IVIG) to prevent cardiac complications such as coronary artery aneurysm.

Answer B: Hospitalization is required for the treatment of Kawasaki disease, although supportive care only would not be sufficient to treat the disease adequately. To reduce the incidence of cardiac complications such as coronary artery aneurysm and myocardial infarction, the recommended treatment is high dose aspirin and IVIG.


Answers C & D: The signs and symptoms of KD have some overlap with those of toxic shock syndrome (TSS). TSS is a toxin-mediated acute life-threatening illness, due to prior or current infection with either Staphylococcus aureus or group A Streptococcus. Treatment for this syndrome includes rapid removal of the source of infection (tampon, abscess) and initiation of broad-spectrum antibiotics (vancomycin and penicillin/cephalosporin). This patient does not have risk factors for either bacterial forms of TSS and has no desquamating rash associated. Importantly, this patient is hemodynamically stable, which is important in differentiating TSS from Kawasaki disease.


Answer E: Transfer to a burn unit would be the appropriate treatment for toxic epidermal necrolysis (TEN). TEN is the most severe form of immune-complex-mediated hypersensitivity reaction and carries a high mortality (10-70%). The skin manifestation begins as a poorly defined, erythematous macular rash that may quickly form flaccid blisters and generalized epidermal detachment. Mucous membrane erosions generally precede the skin lesions by 1-3 days and most frequently affects the oropharynx, followed by the eyes and genitalia. This patient is overall well appearing with strawberry tongue, conjunctivitis and generalized rash without desquamation and therefore is unlikely to be TEN.




Bottom Line: Kawasaki disease presents as an acute febrile illness in children with a generalized rash, conjunctival injection, cervical lymphadenopathy and other symptoms. It is treated with high dose aspirin and IVIG.

COMBANK Insight : The mnemonic “CRASH and Burn” is helpful to distinguish KD from TSS or SJS. Conjunctivitis, Rash, Adenopathy, Strawberry tongue, Hands/feet red, and Burn = high prolonged fever.

For more information, see:
"Kawasaki Disease " on Medscape.
“Toxic Epidermal Necrolysis” on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Consciousness Alterations
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 17-year-old male presents to the emergency department for evaluation of syncope. History reveals he collapsed while running earlier that day and regained consciousness after a few moments. Review of systems reveals occasional chest pain and trouble breathing during baseball practice. Physical examination reveals an S4 gallop and a systolic ejection murmur heard best at the left lower sternal border that is accentuated by standing and valsalva, and lessened by squatting, leg raising, and the handgrip maneuver. Electrocardiography demonstrates septal Q waves in the inferior and lateral leads. The most likely diagnosis is

1. dilated cardiomyopathy
2. hypertrophic cardiomyopathy
3. hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy
4. myocardial infarction
5. restrictive cardiomyopathy

The correct answer is: C
Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy is a congenital disorder characterized by an asymmetrical thickening on the ventricular septum resulting in an anatomic obstruction to the aortic outflow tract.  It is the most common cardiomyopathy and the most frequent cause of sudden cardiac death in young patients.  Patients may be asymptomatic or may present with symptoms of heart failure.  Physical examination may be normal at rest with auscultatory findings only present with an increase in heart rate (such as exercise, dehydration, etc.) or with a decrease in left ventricular chamber size (i.e. valsalva and standing due to a decrease in venous return).  A systolic ejection murmur may be auscultated along the left sternal border.  Electrocardiography may be normal, or may demonstrate nonspecific ST and T wave changes, septal Q waves in the inferior and lateral leads and/or left ventricular hypertrophy.  Echocardiography will reveal asymmetric hypertrophy of the ventricular septum, and systolic anterior motion (SAM) is classic.  Treatment is with beta-blockers, calcium-channel blockers, or disopyramide when patients are symptomatic.  A subset of patients with increased risk for sudden death should undergo defibrillator implantation.

Answer A: Dilated cardiomyopathy may be caused by ischemia, a previous myocardial infarction, alcohol, post-viral myocarditis, toxins (i.e. doxorubicin), radiation, or chagas disease. It presents with the typical signs and symptoms of congestive heart failure (dyspnea on exertion, edema, etc.). This would be highly unlikely in a healthy, young athlete with a negative past medical history.


Answer B: Hypertrophic cardiomyopathy must be distinguished from hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy. While hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy is a congenital disorder and the most common cardiomyopathy in younger patients, hypertrophic cardiomyopathy is an acquired cardiomyopathy. It is most often caused by long-standing, uncontrolled hypertension. The heart becomes hypertrophied in order to pump against the high blood pressure. As hypertrophy progresses, the heart stiffens and cannot relax completely during diastole eventually leading to heart failure. It is unlikely that a patient of this age would have acquired hypertrophic cardiomyopathy.


Answer D: At first glance, seeing Q waves on the electrocardiogram may be worrisome for a myocardial infarction. However, as stated above, hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy may commonly display Q waves in the inferior and lateral leads on electrocardiography. These are "normal" in this condition and not indicative of a myocardial infarction.


Answer E: Restrictive cardiomyopathy results from the myocardium becoming infiltrated with substances that result in immobility and impairment in both systole and diastole. Diseases known to be associated with restrictive cardiomyopathy include scleroderma, hemochromatosis, sarcoidosis, amyloidosis, and endomyocardial fibrosis. Patients typically present with right sided-heart failure leading to jugular venous distension, ascites, edema, and hepatosplenomegaly.




Bottom Line: Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy is the most common cardiomyopathy in young patients.  Look for a history of dyspnea, chest pain, presyncope, syncope, or sudden cardiac death during exercise in a young person.

For more information, see:

Fischer C, August E, Gonzalez-Mayo A, et al. Master the Boards USMLE Step 2 CK. New York, NY: Kaplan Publishing; 2011: 94

Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 67-year-old male presents to the clinic for routine examination.  He has a past medical history of hypertension and a 35 pack-year smoking history. His last colonoscopy was 5 years ago, and was normal.  He received all of his childhood vaccinations and has not had any recent travel. The most appropriate preventative evaluation at this time is

1. abdominal ultrasound
2. capsule endoscopy
3. colonoscopy
4. dual-energy X-ray absorptiometry scan
5. meningococcal vaccination

The correct answer is: A
All men over the age of 65 with any smoking history should be screened once with an abdominal ultrasound to exclude an abdominal aortic aneurysm.  Abdominal aortic aneurysms should be repaired if their width exceeds 5 cm.  This patient should also receive counseling regarding his smoking habits and should be encouraged to quit.

Answer B: Capsule endoscopy is not a preventative screening tool. It is used in the detection of small bowel bleeding, and is not a cancer screening method.


Answer C: Colonoscopy is the best screening modality for colon cancer. According to the CDC, it is recommended every 10 years between the ages of 50 and 75. This patient had his last screening colonoscopy 5 years ago, and it was normal. He is not due for another colonoscopy for 5 years. Additional screening in other clinical situations is as follows:

1) If there is a family history of familial adenomatous polyposis or Gardner syndrome, a colonoscopy and genetic testing are recommended. Colonoscopy should be performed every 12 months beginning at age 10-12 years until age 35-40 years if negative. Because of the nearly 100% risk of developing cancer with familial adenomatous polyposis, total colectomy should be considered in the event of a positive colonoscopy.

2) If there is a first-degree relative who was diagnosed with colon cancer or adenomas and age younger than 60 years, or with multiple first-degree relatives diagnosed at any age, the patient should have a colonoscopy every 3-5 years beginning at the age 10 years younger than the youngest affected relative does.

3) Hereditary non-polyposis colorectal cancer has a roughly 70% lifetime risk of developing cancer and thus patients in whom there is such a diagnosis should undergo colonoscopy every 1-2 years, beginning at the age of 20-25.


Answer D: Dual energy x-ray absorptiometry scan is indicated for all women at the age of 65 to screen for osteoporosis.


Answer E: A meningococcal vaccination is given routinely at the age 11 visit to the pediatrician. This vaccination is also indicated for adults with the following circumstances: terminal compliment deficiency, residents of college dormitories, military recruits, asplenia, or travelers to Mecca or Medina in Saudi Arabia for the Hajj (pilgrimage).




Bottom Line: All male patients over the age of 65 with a smoking history should be screened with an abdominal ultrasound for an abdominal aortic aneurysm.

COMBANK Insight : The COMLEX will certainly be testing topics concerning preventative medicine.  Being familiar with and reviewing these topics before test day will provide you with more confidence with your answers.

For more information, see:

Fischer C, August E, Gonzalez-Mayo A, et al. Master the Boards USMLE Step 2 CK. New York, NY: Kaplan Publishing; 2011: 357-361

Link: Hemolytic-Uremic Syndrome


Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Pediatrics
An 8-month-old male is brought to the emergency department by his mother for fever and confusion. His mother states that he suffered a gastrointestinal illness with diarrhea and vomiting a few days ago. During that time, the boy was fussy but still somewhat playful and able to drink fluids. However, today he has been more lethargic and irritable. She noted that he had only 1-2 wet diapers in the past 24-hour period. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 102/68 mmHg, a heart rate of 100/min, a temperature of 38.6ºC (101.5ºF), and a respiratory rate of 18/min. There is scleral icterus and the abdomen is soft and non-tender. A urine sample is obtained by straight catheterization, revealing a small amount of dark-colored urine. Renal function tests reveal a BUN and creatinine of 33 mg/dL and 1.9 mg/dL, respectively. The most likely etiology of this patient’s current condition is

1. Campylobacter jejuni
2. Escherichia coli 0157:H7
3. Henoch-Schonlein purpura
4. Shigella flexneri
5. Staphylococcus aureus enterotoxin

The correct answer is: B
This child most likely has hemolytic-uremic syndrome (HUS), which is caused by infection with Escherichia coli 0157:H7. This particular strain of E. coli produces Shiga-like toxin, which causes colitis, allowing systemic absorption of both Shiga-like toxin and LPS from the gastrointestinal (GI) tract. These toxins promote inflammation and microthrombosis in the glomerular capillaries, leading to a local intravascular coagulopathy. Renal insufficiency, microangiopathic hemolytic anemia and destructive thrombocytopenia all result largely from these toxin-induced glomerular abnormalities and explain this patient’s clinical presentation.

Answer A: Campylobacter jejuni infection is associated with Guillain Barre Syndrome, not hemolytic-uremic syndrome.


Answer C: Henoch-Schonlein purpura is a small vessel vasculitis characterized by palpable purpura of the lower extremities as well as arthritis, arthralgias and renal failure. Approximately ½ of cases are preceded by an upper respiratory tract infection, not a GI illness.


Answer D: Infection with Shigella flexneri causes a hemorrhagic diarrhea, but it is not associated with a post-infectious acute hemolytic syndrome as seen in this patient. Shigella dysenteriae produces Shiga toxin and therefore may also cause with HUS; however, E. coli O157:H7 is the most common cause of HUS in the United States.


Answer E: Staphylococcal enterotoxin leads to acute food poisoning but is not associated with HUS.


Answer F: Post streptococcal glomerulonephritis may present with renal failure; however, the other features of this patient’s condition are not consistent with a post-streptococcal immune-mediated illness.




Bottom Line: The causative agent in hemolytic uremic syndrome is Shiga-like toxin, which is classically associated with a preceding colitis caused by E. coli O157:H7 infection.

For more information, see:
Hemolytic-Uremic Syndrome

Link: "Lumbosacral Spondylolisthesis" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 20-year-old male with no previous medical history presents to the office with a chief complaint of low back pain. History reveals the patient is a college student who plays in an intermural football team. The pain began after a strenuous practice about two months ago. It is worse with exercise and better with rest and ibuprofen. A radiograph of the lumbar spine reveals a grade II spondylolisthesis of L5 on S1. The next best step in management is

1. bed rest and lumbar bracing to prevent flexion
2. exercise program to strengthen abdominal muscles and decrease lumbar lordosis
3. further imaging with a CT or MRI
4. surgical intervention since the patient has >50% slippage of L5 vertebrae on S1
5. surgical intervention since the patient has >75% slippage of L5 vertebrae on S1

The correct answer is: B
Spondylolisthesis is a forward slippage of one vertebral body on the one below it. In the above scenario, the patient has L5 slippage on S1 vertebral body. Commonly, patients with spondylolisthesis will also have spondylolysis (unilateral or bilateral separation in the vertebral pars interarticularis; typically described as the collar around the neck of a "scottie dog" in an oblique lumbar spine x-rays). Spondylolysis and spondylolisthesis in young people often occurs among those who play sports that require repetitive flexion/extension of the spine such as gymnastics, volleyball, and football, as is the case with the patient in the above scenario. In the older population, spondylolisthesis occurs often due to the degenerative changes in the posterior facet joints and the disc. This eventually allows a "forward slippage" of the vertebra. Unlike the case above, in degenerative spondylolisthesis, slippage occurs most commonly at the level of L4 on L5. Physical examination may show localized pain in the low back especially with extension. Also, "step-down depression" of the spinous process at the effected level may exist. Neurological deficits are rare. The treatment options for spondylolisthesis often vary depending on the treating physician. However, most patients can be treated conservatively. Often patients are recommended a home exercise program or physical therapy that can strengthen the abdominal muscles, decrease lumbar extension and increase flexibility. Although OMT does not directly treat the defect, gentle OMT focusing on the hypertonic muscles as well as other somatic dysfunction can be helpful in relieving the patient's pain.

Answer A: Often patients are recommended a home exercise program or physical therapy that can strengthen the abdominal muscles, decrease lumbar extension and increase flexibility.


Answer C: Plain film is the best imaging choice for spondylolisthesis. This should include lateral and oblique view. Lateral view will show the pars defects as well as the anterior displacement of the vertebral body. Oblique view will show the classic "collar of the Scottie dog." Although sensitive for spondylolysis, CT maybe be too costly with high radiation exposure for the patient. MRI may be useful to visualize local soft tissue damage and edema, but unnecessary in this case


Answers D & E: Surgical intervention is indicated when patient shows neurologic deficit. Also surgery should be considered for patients with intractable low back pain often accompanied by radiculopathy into buttocks and posterior thigh. High-grade spondylolisthesis, with slip greater than 50%, commonly requires surgery. The patient in above case has grade II spondylolisthesis and does not have any neurologic deficits.




Bottom Line: For spondylolisthesis grade I and II (<50% slippage) without neurological deficits, treatment is usually conservative including an exercise program and osteopathic manipulative treatment.

COMBANK Insight : Spondylolisthesis can be graded based on the amount of anterior "slippage." Grade I: <25% Grade II: 25-50% Grade III: 50-75% Grade IV: 75-100%

For more information, see:
"Lumbosacral Spondylolisthesis" on Medscape


Last Updated: 9/21/2016

Link: "Anthrax" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 56-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaints of flu-like symptoms, difficulty breathing, and mild chest discomfort for the past 6 hours. History reveals he is a local goat farmer with a past medical history significant for hypertension, arthritis and basal cell carcinoma of the face. He denies sick contacts and is not a smoker. Physical examination reveals an oral temperature of 39ºC (102.2ºF) and clear lungs bilaterally on auscultation. An electrocardiogram is obtained and reveals sinus tachycardia at a rate of 106/min. A chest radiograph performed in the emergency department shows mediastinal widening without lobar or interstitial infiltrates. The most likely cause of this patient's symptoms is

1. acute myocardial infarction
2. aortic dissection
3. community acquired pneumonia
4. congestive heart failure
5. inhalational anthrax

The correct answer is: E
This patient is presenting with typical symptoms of inhalational anthrax caused by breathing in spores from the bacteria Bacillus anthracis. This gram-positive, spore-forming bacillus is known to infect sheep, goats, cattle and horses and can be transmitted to humans who handle sick animals or contaminated animal hides. The most common form of infection from Bacillus anthracis (95%) is cutaneous, with the inhalational form only comprising 5% of cases. Symptoms usually begin days after exposure and include flu-like symptoms, fever, chest discomfort and dyspnea. When spores are inhaled, they are ingested by pulmonary macrophages and are carried to the hilar and mediastinal lymph nodes. These lymph nodes become overwhelmed with infection and enlarge, seen as mediastinal widening on chest radiograph. Hemorrhagic mediastinitis, pulmonary edema and hemorrhagic pulmonary effusions can occur with inhalational anthrax. The mortality rate without treatment is 95%.

Answer A: Acute myocardial infarction (MI) may present with atypical symptoms including dyspnea, fatigue, low back pain and diaphoresis. It is not typical for patients with acute myocardial infarction to present with concomitant fever or without electrocardiographic changes consistent with myocardial infarction, therefore acute MI is not a likely answer.


Answer B: Aortic dissection occurs when a false lumen is created within the wall of the aorta. Typically patients present with significant chest and back pain. Although a negative chest radiograph may be present, mediastinal widening is a component of thoracic aortic dissection. This patient's fever is not explained by aortic dissection making inhalational anthrax a more likely answer.


Answer C: Community acquired pneumonia may present with fever, chills, dyspnea and chest discomfort, however, in the absence of lobar or interstitial infiltrates on chest radiograph the diagnosis of pneumonia is less likely.


Answer D: Left-sided congestive heart failure typically presents with dyspnea, dry cough and lower extremity edema along with pulmonary rales and bilateral interstitial infiltrates on chest radiograph. This patient's symptoms along with the absence of pulmonary infiltrates makes congestive heart failure an unlikely answer choice.




Bottom Line: Inhalational anthrax is an uncommon, usually fatal infection caused by inhalation of spores from Bacillus anthracis bacteria. Symptoms include a flu-like prodrome, chest discomfort and fever followed by rapid progression to respiratory failure or sepsis.

For more information, see:
"Anthrax" on Medscape.


Last Updated: 9/21/2016

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 38-year-old male presents to the office for evaluation of lower back pain. History reveals that the pain has been present for several years but has worsened over the last two months' duration. The patient also reports morning stiffness and occasional pain in his knees. Examination reveals pain with back flexion and a decreased range of motion in the spine with pain over the sacroiliac joints bilaterally. FABER test and Schober test are found to be positive. Radiographic images are not readily available. The most likely diagnosis is

1. Ankylosing spondylitis
2. osteoarthritis
3. polymyalgia rheumatic
4. rheumatoid arthritis
5. systemic lupus erythematosus

The correct answer is: A
This disease frequently presents in the 3rd to 4th decade of life and involves chronic inflammation of the spine and sacroiliac joints. Examination findings include back pain, decreased range of motion in the spine, morning stiffness and occasionally, pain/inflammation in other joints (e.g. hip, knees, ankles). Physical exam findings include pain with back flexion, diminished range of motion, positive Schober test, positive FABER test. Diagnostic evaluation includes blood work (HLA-B27), x-ray and MRI. Treatment options include drug-modifying antirheumatologic drugs, TNF-blockers, NSAIDs, steroids and physical therapy.

Answer B: This condition is associated with damage to the articular cartilage within joints. Morning stiffness can be present in many cases but it is usually monoarticular rather than polyarticular. Exam findings can include Heberden's nodules and Bouchard's nodules, as well as a decreased range of motion at the affected joint.


Answer C: This is a rheumatic condition associated with giant cell arteritis (temporal arteritis). Patient's frequently present with chronic aching and morning stiffness in the shoulders, hip girdles, neck and torso. Most patients are older than 50 years.


Answer D: This condition is the most common chronic inflammatory arthritis that tends to afflict multiple joints (frequently symmetric). Patients will present with atraumatic swollen joints, finger deformities or weakness early in the disease process. Chronic cases can have more prominent clinical findings including ulnar deviation of the phalanges, ocular issues (keratoconjunctivitis), foot deformities and spine involvement.


Answer E: This is a chronic inflammatory immunologic disease, more frequently encountered in women, that can affect multiple organ systems including the dermatologic, pulmonary, cardiac, renal, immune, musculoskeletal and nervous systems.




Bottom Line: Ankylosing spondylitis is a chronic, inflammatory disease-causing arthritis of the spine and sacroiliac joints.

For more information, see:
"Ankylosing Spondylitis" on Medscape.

Link: "Peritonisillar Abscess in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 26-year-old female presents to the emergency department with dysphagia, odynophagia, and unilateral neck pain. The throat discomfort has progressively worsened throughout the day. She does recall feeling “sick” for the past week. She is otherwise healthy with no significant medical history. Physical examination reveals an agitated woman in moderate to severe distress, leaning forward in her chair drooling. On inspection of the oral cavity you notice trismus, unilateral swelling, erythema of the posterior oral pharynx, and deviation of the uvula. The most likely diagnosis is

1. dental infection
2. Herpes Simplex virus pharyngitis
3. mononucleosis
4. peritonsillar abscess
5. retropharyngeal abscess

The correct answer is: D
The patient has historical and physical exam findings that make the most likely diagnosis a peritonsillar abscess. This patient has unilateral throat pain with painful swallowing (odynophagia) to the point that she is drooling. Patient also had trismus from inflammation resulting in spasm of the pterygoid musculature. Another key physical exam finding is the deviation of the uvula, which is pointing away from the infected tonsil. Patients may also present with unilateral referred ear pain.

Answer A: A dental infection presents more localized to a specific tooth. It is seen more commonly with Ludwig’s angina.


Answer B: Herpes simplex pharyngitis also presents with odynophagia. Physical examination reveals aphthous ulcers on the bilateral tonsils/oral pharynx.


Answer C: Mononucleosis presents as an exudative pharyngitis with lymphadenopathy. Patients will have with throat discomfort, but bilateral in presentation as opposed to unilateral.


Answer E: A retropharyngeal abscess can present with neck pain, drooling and odynophagia but can also present with neck stiffness. There will not likely be unilateral oral pharyngeal swelling or uvular deviation.




Bottom Line: Peritonsillar abscess will present with difficulty/painful swallowing, unilateral neck pain and uvula deviation away from abscess.

For more information, see:
"Peritonisillar Abscess in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.

Link: "Insulin therapy in type 2 diabetes mellitus" on UptoDate.


Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 49-year-old obese female presents to your clinic complaining of fatigue and increased urination. She tells you that she eats a poor diet and gets little exercise. She has a history of hypertension and hypercholesterolemia. Her family history is positive for coronary artery disease, hypertension and diabetes. A HbA1c is drawn and comes back 15.6. You decide to treat her with the type of insulin that has the longest duration. Which medication did you choose?

1. Aspart insulin
2. Glargine insulin
3. Lispro insulin
4. NPH insulin
5. Regular insulin

The correct answer is: B
The most likely diagnosis in this patient is diabetes mellitus.  It may commonly be diagnosed with routine screening tests.  Risk factors for diabetes include obesity, family history of diabetes, sedentary lifestyle, older age, poor diet, hypertension, HLA-DR4, HLA-DR3, high cholesterol, polycystic ovarian syndrome and a history of gestational diabetes.  The diagnosis of DM can be made with a fasting plasma glucose greater than 126 when symptoms of hyperglycemia are present, with a random plasma glucose greater than 200, with a two-hour plasma glucose greater than 200 following a 75 gram oral glucose tolerance test or with a HbA1c greater than 6.5%.  In the absence of risk factors, patients older than 40 should be screened for type II diabetes.  This patient has symptoms of hyperglycemia.  Polyuria occurs secondary to increased glucose levels being processed by the kidneys which leads to an osmotic diuresis.  Polydipsia occurs secondary to the osmotic diuresis as an attempt to rehydrate.  The American diabetes association now suggests using a HbA1C of greater than 6.5% as a criteria for diagnosing diabetes.  The preprandial glucose goal for diabetics is 90-130 while the postprandial goal level is
The cornerstone of medical therapy for diabetes is metformin.  It is usually well-tolerated and the most common side effects are gastrointestinal in nature.  Severe renal dysfunction (GFR less than 30 mL/min) is a contraindication to the use of metformin.  Abnormal BUN and Cr can also be indicative of renal dysfunction that precludes the use of metformin.  

The American Diabetes Association recommends using insulin as a first line medication in patients with a HbA1c greater than 10.0%.  Lispro insulin has the fastest onset of action at 5-10 minutes.  Glargine insulin has an onset of 1-4 hours and a duration of 24-28.  Thiazolidinediones, such as pioglitazone and rosiglitazone, are contraindicated in patients with CHF.

Answer A: Aspart insulin has an onset of 10-20 minutes and a duration of 3-5 hours.


Answer C: Lispro insulin has the fastest onset of action at 5-10 minutes.


Answer D: NPH insulin will last between 18-24 hrs, peaking between 6-10.


Answer E: Regular insulin has an onset of 30-60 minutes and a duration of 5-8 hours.




Bottom Line: Glargine insulin has an onset of 1-4 hours and a duration of 24-28

COMBANK Insight : COMLEX level 2 exams are much more clinically oriented than level 1 exams. You may be given a situation that involves a very common diagnosis and asked a clinically relevant question about that diagnosis. MHC-HLA antigens have shown up as one line questions before.

For more information, see:
"Insulin therapy in type 2 diabetes mellitus" on UptoDate.

Malignant Carcinoid Syndrome on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 58-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaint of recurrent, intermittent nonbloody diarrhea. History reveals that he has also been having intermittent facial flushing, fatigue, palpitations, dyspnea on exertion, and respiratory wheezing. The patient was recently started on an albuterol inhaler. Physical examination reveals bilateral pitting pretibial edema and a diastolic, decrescendo heart murmur heard best at the left lower sternal border. The best initial diagnostic test for the likely diagnosis is

1. computed tomography
2. fine-needle biopsy
3. plain chest radiography
4. urinary 5-hydroxyindoleacetic acid test
5. urinary serotonin levels

The correct answer is: D
The best initial test for the diagnosis of carcinoid syndrome is the urinary 5-hydroxyindoleacetic acid (5 HIAA) test.  5 HIAA is a serotonin metabolite.  A urinary excretion of 25 mg/d of 5 HIAA is diagnostic of carcinoid.  If the range value is 9-25 mg/d, the differential diagnosis includes carcinoid syndrome, nontropical sprue, or acute intestinal obstruction.  Carcinoid syndrome is a constellation of symptoms that results from metastases of carcinoid tumors that secrete excessive amounts of serotonin.  These tumors arise from neuroendocrine cells.  Carcinoid tumors tend to follow a “rule of one-third” which states that one-third of the tumors are located in the small intestine, one-third of the tumors are multiple, one-third have a second malignancy and one-third metastasize.  Fewer than 10% of patients with a carcinoid tumor will go on to develop carcinoid syndrome.  This is because in order to develop the syndrome, the patient must possess metastatic liver lesions thereby escaping the first pass effect and hepatic degradation, allowing access to systemic circulation.  Carcinoid syndrome typically presents with intermittent non-bloody diarrhea associated with facial flushing, wheezing, and cardiac abnormalities localized to the right side of the heart.  Treatment typically consists of the use of the somatostatin analog octreotide.  This, of course, only treats the symptoms rather than the underlying tumor.  Interferon alpha may be used to control symptoms also and has the added benefit of helping to arrest tumor growth.  Radiotherapy and/or chemotherapy may also be used.

Answer A: Computed tomography scans may be helpful after the diagnosis of carcinoid syndrome is made to evaluate the tumor burden and for metastases. CT will typically shows a homogeneous, ill-defined mesenteric mass with calcifications and a stellate or curvilinear fibrosis radiating from the mass.


Answer B: After a lesion is found, a biopsy may be obtained to confirm the histopathology of carcinoid tumor. It should be noted, however, that a fine-needle biopsy of a liver metastasis might cause a carcinoid crisis.


Answer C: Plain chest radiography may have been warranted in this patient given his respiratory complaints; however, it is not indicated in the routine workup for carcinoid syndrome.


Answer E: Urinary serotonin levels may be checked and are often elevated in carcinoid syndrome, however it has less diagnostic value and it is the metabolite of serotonin (5 HIAA) that is the best initial test.




Bottom Line: Consider the diagnosis of carcinoid syndrome in a patient with intermittent diarrhea, flushing, wheezing, and right-sided cardiac abnormalities.  The best initial test is a 24-hour urinary 5 HIAA test.

For more information, see:

Malignant Carcinoid Syndrome on Medscape

Tuberculosis on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 35-year old male presents to the office with the complaint of difficulty breathing worsening for five days. History reveals weight loss, night sweats, and persistent cough sometimes productive of blood-tinged sputum. Social history reveals he is a warden of a state prison. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 38.3ºC (101ºF), a heart rate of 86/min, and a respiratory rate of 18/min. Physical examination reveals an ill-appearing male with scattered wheezes and crackles in both lung fields. 
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The medication used to treat the most likely diagnosis is commonly co-administered with

1. cobalamin
2. mesna
3. riboflavin
4. pyridoxine
5. pantothenic acid

The correct answer is: D
Isoniazid is antitubercular agent that works by inhibiting mycolic acid synthesis and disrupting the cell wall.  It is used in the treatment of both active and latent tuberculosis, as well as nontuberculous mycobacterium (Mycobacterium kansasii).  Isoniazid comes with a black box warning stating that severe and sometimes fatal hepatitis may occur usually within the first three weeks of treatment; however, it may also develop even after months of treatment. Isoniazid is also known to cause peripheral neuropathies.  This occurs because isoniazid depletes the body of vitamin B6 stores.  Pyridoxine, also known as vitamin B6, is given with isoniazid to prevent this from occurring.

Answer A: Cobalamin is vitamin B12, which is an essential cofactor in various metabolic processes. Humans are unable to synthesize B12 and must obtain it from their diets. Deficiency in B12 may result in megaloblastic anemia and/or peripheral neuropathy.


Answer B: Mesna as a chemoprotective agent, and is given with cyclophosphamide and ifosfamide to prevent hemorrhagic cystitis.


Answer C: Riboflavin is vitamin B2. It is commonly used for off-label migraine prophylaxis.


Answer E: Pantothenic acid is vitamin B5, which is required by the body to synthesize coenzyme A (CoA). CoA is important in many metabolic activities, particularly in the tricarboxylic acid cycle (TCA). It is also important in the synthesis of fatty acids, cholesterol, and acetylcholine.




Bottom Line: Patients taking isoniazid should also be given pyridoxine to prevent peripheral neuropathy.

For more information, see:

Tuberculosis on Medscape

A 35-year old male presents to the office with the complaint of difficulty breathing worsening for five days. History reveals weight loss, night sweats, and persistent cough sometimes productive of blood-tinged sputum. Social history reveals he is a warden of a state prison. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 38.3ºC (101ºF), a heart rate of 86/min, and a respiratory rate of 18/min. Physical examination reveals an ill-appearing male with scattered wheezes and crackles in both lung fields. 
Question 2 of 2 in this set
Which of the following drugs used to treat the most likely diagnosis has a side effect of red-green color blindness?

1. rifampin
2. pyridoxine
3. cyclophosphamide
4. ethambutol
5. isoniazid

The correct answer is: D
Ethambutol is an antimycobacterial drug used to treat tuberculosis. Ethambutol can cause optic toxicity and optic neuritis. The first signs of optic toxicity due to ethambutol are blurry vision and red-green color blindness. Ethambutol is often given in combination with other antimycobacterials such as isoniazid and rifampin. Other side effects are peripheral neuropathy and liver toxicity.

Link: "Acute pericarditis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 65-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of low-grade fever and pleuritic chest pain. History reveals the patient was discharged from the hospital 2 weeks ago for a myocardial infarction. Physical examination reveals lungs are clear to auscultation and a friction rub is present on cardiovascular exam. An ECG is obtained, which shows no evidence of further cardiac pathology. The most effective treatment for this patient is

1. anti-anxiety medication
2. IV heparin
3. NSAIDs
4. oral antibiotics
5. sublingual nitroglycerin

The correct answer is: C
The history and physical exam are consistent with post-cardiac injury syndrome (also known as Dressler syndrome, or post-myocardial infarction syndrome). This generally benign, self-limited syndrome typically presents with autoimmune pleuritis, pneumonitis, or pericarditis characterized by fever and pleuritic chest pain, with onset up to 6 weeks' post-cardiac injury. This syndrome is thought to occur secondary to the presence of blood in the pericardial cavity, which occurs following cardiac surgery, cardiac trauma, or MI. The most effective therapy for this condition is treatment with a nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory agent (NSAID). Glucocorticoids are also used but are considered a second-line therapy.

Answer A: Anxiety may be present but should not be assumed before other etiologies are ruled out. The presence of fever would suggest an inflammatory event, not anxiety.


Answer B: Pulmonary embolus would require anticoagulation with heparin but is inconsistent with the above presentation.


Answer D: Infection such as bacterial pneumonia, which would require antibiotic therapy, would likely present with dyspnea, cough with sputum production, and rales on auscultation of the chest.


Answer E: Angina and recurrence of a myocardial infarction is always a concern in a post-MI patient, but the nature of the pain and the unchanged ECG are fairly reassuring and mitigate against administering sublingual nitroglycerin.




Bottom Line: Dressler syndrome, or postmyocardial infarction syndrome, should be initially treated with NSAIDs.

COMBANK Insight : Know what to expect on ECG in the days/weeks following an MI. Remember that Q waves are a marker of electrical silence, implying full thickness death of myocardium when pathologic. If a Q wave occurs in lead I then check for Q waves in AVL and the chest leads. If a Q wave occurs in lead II or AVF then check for Q waves in the other inferior leads.

For more information, see:
"Acute pericarditis" on Medscape.

Link: "Hypothermia" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Hypothermia
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 62-year-old male presents to the Emergency Department unresponsive. History reveals the patient is homeless and was found unconscious on a park bench. Past medical history reveals alcoholism. The patient is unresponsive to verbal command but withdraws his extremities to painful stimuli. An ECG is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most likely diagnosis is

1. hyperkalemia
2. hypocalcemia
3. hypothermia
4. myocardial infarction
5. pulmonary embolism

The correct answer is: C
Based on the ECG, it is most likely the patient is suffering from hypothermia. The classic ECG finding of hypothermia is the Osborn wave (or prominent J wave), which is an extra positive deflection between the terminal portion of the QRS complex and the beginning of the ST segment. The Osborn wave is usually positive in the left precordial leads, and has an amplitude that is inversely proportional to body temperature. Other ECG changes caused by hypothermia include prolongation of the PR, QRS, and QT intervals, T wave inversion, and bradyarrhythmias consisting of sinus bradycardia, junctional rhythms, or atrial fibrillation with a slow ventricular response. Profound hypothermia (core temperatures < 32ºC) causes peripheral vasoconstriction, impaired enzymatic activity, decreased cardiac output, and reduced respirations. Complications of hypothermia include aspiration pneumonia, adult respiratory distress syndrome, pulmonary edema, rhabdomyolysis, acute tubular necrosis, gastric dilatation, upper GI bleed, hyperviscosity syndrome, and DIC.

Answer A: Hyperkalemia will be characterized on ECG with reduced P waves and the development of peaked T waves. As the severity of hyperkalemia increases, the QRS will become widened and the ECG complex may evolve into a sinusoidal shape.


Answer B: Hypocalcemia will manifest with intermittent QT prolongation on ECG. This type of instability puts the patient at high risk for torsades de pointes.


Answer D: ST elevation and T-wave changes are classic findings for an acute myocardial infarction.


Answer E: The “S1Q3T3” pattern is characterized by a prominent S wave in lead I, Q and inverted T wave in lead III. This pattern is the classic finding seen in cases of pulmonary embolism. Ultimately, the right ventricle can enlarge causing a left deviation of the interventricular septum. This leads to the stretch of the right bundle branch resulting in a RBBB. If asked about the most common ECG finding with pulmonary embolism, however, (often a board favorite) the correct answer would be sinus tachycardia.




Bottom Line: Osborn waves represent the classic ECG finding in patients suffering from hypothermia.

COMBANK Insight : Be familiar with the classic ECG findings of the above conditions. ECG exhibits are a favorite on the exam.

For more information, see:
"Hypothermia" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 29-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of weakness and fatigue over the past 4 months. History reveals the weakness is worse at night, and the patient also experiences double vision. Physical examination reveals ptosis bilaterally and symmetrical weakness of the upper and lower extremities and trouble sustaining contraction. Edrophonium challenge leads to a brief improvement of the symptoms. The most appropriate step in evaluation of this patient is

1. chest CT
2. lumbar puncture
3. MRI of brain and spinal cord
4. muscle biopsy
5. upper endoscopy

The correct answer is: A
The patient is suffering from myasthenia gravis, an autoimmune disease caused by antibodies against acetylcholine receptors. It is characterized by weakness that worsens with use. Patients will often complain of diplopia, fatigue and periodic weakness. The symptoms improve when given edrophonium, a drug that blocks the action of acetyl cholinesterase. Myasthenia gravis is often associated with thymomas and thus after a diagnosis is made, a CT of the chest is mandatory. Myasthenia may resolve with thymectomy.

Answer B: A lumbar puncture can be used to diagnose meningitis and is helpful in the diagnosis of multiple sclerosis if oligoclonal bands are present.


Answer C: An MRI of the brain and spinal cord is useful in the diagnosis of multiple sclerosis. Multiple plaques in the white matter will be present.


Answer D: Muscle biopsy can be used in the diagnosis of certain myopathies; it is not needed to diagnose myasthenia gravis. The best test to diagnosis myasthenia is the anti-acetylcholine receptor antibody.


Answer E: Periodic monitoring by upper endoscopy is indicated for patients with scleroderma as they are at a higher risk of developing esophageal cancers due to severe gastric reflux. It is not needed in the initial work up of myasthenia gravis.




Bottom Line: A thymoma needs to be ruled out in patients with myasthenia gravis.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 17-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of difficulty concentrating and headaches. History reveals the patient sustained a blow to the top of her head by a softball during gym class. Physical examination reveals the patient has a decreased CRI at 7 cycles/min and her sphenobasilar synchondrosis (SBS) favors a flexion pattern. Which of the following is true regarding SBS flexion?

1. AP diameter of the cranium increases
2. Midline bones move into extension
3. Paired bones internally rotate
4. The dura is pulled cephalad
5. The sacrum nutates

The correct answer is: D
Flexion at the SBS causes the dura to be pulled in the cephalad direction. Recall the coordination of cranial motion. In craniosacral flexion, the midline bones flex, sacrum counternutates, paired bones externally rotate and the AP diameter decreases. The head becomes wider and flatter. In extension, the midline bones extend, sacrum nutates, paired bones internally rotate, and the AP diameter increases. The head becomes longer and deeper.

Answer A: During craniosacral flexion, the AP diameter of the cranium decreases. The transverse diameter of the head increases.


Answer B: During craniosacral flexion, the midline bones move into flexion.


Answer C: During craniosacral flexion, the paired bones externally rotate.


Answer E: During craniosacral flexion, the sacrum counternutates.




Bottom Line: During craniosacral flexion, the dura is pulled cephalad.

Link: "Iron Deficiency Anemia" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 70-yr-old Caucasian male presents to the emergency department with the complaints of fatigue and shortness of breath. History reveals some chest pain when he is walking around the house for the past three days. He has noticed his stool is a darker color, which he has not experienced before. His past medical history includes hypertension, hyperlipidemia, diabetes, and atrial fibrillation. His medications include lisinopril, hydrochlorothiazide, glipizide, metoprolol, full strength aspirin, and Coumadin.  He denies tobacco, alcohol and drug use. Physical examination reveals tachycardia without murmur, gallop or rub. Abdomen reveals mild tenderness diffusely in all quadrants without guarding or rebound tenderness. Rectal examination is guaiac positive. One month ago labs were performed and were normal. Laboratory analysis today reveals:

 White blood cells  12,000 /mm3
 Hemoglobin   7.5 g/dL
 MCV   78 um3
 RDW   14.5%
 Creatinine   1.1 mg/dL
 BUN   20  
 INR  7
 Lactate Dehydrogenase  150 U/L 
 Haptoglobin   120
 (Normal 30-200 mg/dL)
Serum Fe   30 ng/mL
Ferritin   10 ng/mL
 TIBC   450 mcg/dL 
(Normal 240-450 mcg/dL)
 Fe Saturation  7.5%
 Vitamin B12   370 pg/mL 
(Normal 200 - 900 pg/mL)
 Folate   20 ng/mL
 (Normal 2.7 - 17.0 ng/mL)
 Hgb electrophoresis  normal

The most likely diagnosis is

1. anemia of chronic disease
2. hemolysis
3. iron deficiency anemia
4. thalassemia
5. vitamin B12 deficiency

The correct answer is: C
The most likely diagnosis is iron deficiency anemia.  The constellation of symptoms such as fatigue, dyspnea, dizziness, melena with hemodynamic instability (tachycardia and hypotension) all suggest symptomatic gastrointestinal blood loss.  Melena suggests upper GI source whereas hematochezia often suggests lower GI source.  CBC for iron deficiency anemia most often reveals a microcytic anemia (lower than 80 MCV). Peripheral smear shows microcytic (small) and hypochromic (large central pallor) red blood cells.  Iron studies demonstrate low serum Fe, elevated TIBC, low Fe saturation, low ferritin, and low percent iron saturation.  In fact, ferritin of less than 10 is specific for iron deficiency anemia.  Hemoglobin electrophoresis is normal in iron deficiency anemia.

Answer A: This clinical presentation depicts an acute drop in hemoglobin with evidence of GI blood loss, i.e. melena in a patient on both anti-platelet (aspirin) and anti-coagulation (Coumadin) in the setting of supra-therapeutic INR. Anemia of chronic disease does not usually present as an abrupt drop in hemoglobin and usually does not present acutely with hemodynamic instability. In addition, iron studies usually result in low serum iron, elevated ferritin, low TIBC, and low iron saturation.


Answer B: Hemolytic Anemia may result from congenital hemoglobin abnormalities, erythrocyte membrane abnormalities (i.e. Hereditary Spherocytosis), or abnormalities of the enzymes (i.e. G6PD) required for erythrocyte metabolism. It is characterized by increased destruction of erythrocytes resulting in increased unconjugated bilirubin, LDH, uric acid, decreased level of haptoglobin (which is due to the binding of free hemoglobin in the blood causing a depletion of haptoglobin level).


Answer D: Thalassemia results from defects in the synthesis of either the alpha or beta chains, leading to ineffective erythropoiesis. It's usually prevalent in Africa, Mediterranean, Middle East, and Asia. Iron studies including serum iron, ferritin, TIBC, percent iron saturation are normal, which is not in this case. Hemoglobin electrophoresis is normal for one and two gene deletions, and will be abnormal for four gene deletions. The iron studies suggest Iron deficiency anemia and the hemoglobin electrophoresis is normal which goes against Thalassemia.


Answer E: Folate and as well as Vitamin B12 deficiency usually result in macrocytic anemia not microcytic anemia which is present in this question stem. Furthermore, the vitamin B12 level and folate level are within the normal range. Vitamin B12 deficiency usually presents with neurological deficits including dementia and peripheral neuropathy.




Bottom Line: GI blood loss can result in Iron Deficiency Anemia which manifests as microcytic, hypochromic anemia, with low serum iron, elevated TIBC, low Fe saturation, and low ferritin.

For more information, see:
"Iron Deficiency Anemia" on Medscape

Link: "Diabetic autonomic neuropathy of the gastrointestinal tract" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 64-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of heartburn and early satiety. History reveals intermittent vomiting that tends to occur several hours following a meal, and an 8 lb. weight loss over the last month. Current medications include lisinopril, metformin, and atorvastatin. Physical examination reveals a soft but mildly distended abdomen with tenderness to palpation in the epigastrum.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. celiac disease
2. diverticular disease
3. gastroesophageal reflux disease
4. gastroparesis
5. post-obstructive ileus

The correct answer is: D
Gastroparesis is one of the many complications of diabetes mellitus. Autonomic dysfunction can potentially involve any part of the sympathetic or parasympathetic chains and manifest in a myriad of ways. Symptoms of diabetic gastroparesis include heartburn, abdominal pain, nausea, vomiting of undigested food (mostly occurring several hours after a meal), bloating, early satiety, and weight loss due to poor nutrient absorption combined with low-calorie intake. The above patient has several of these findings and clinical suspicion should be based on the patient’s history of taking metformin, implying a history of diabetes.

Answer A: Celiac disease is a genetic malabsorptive disorder characterized by gluten intolerance. Symptoms of celiac disease in adults include diarrhea, steatorrhea, weight loss, and feelings of bloating.


Answer B: Diverticular disease results from outpouchings of colonic mucosa and submucosa through the muscular layer of the intestine. Risk factors include a high fat, low fiber diet and being over 60 years of age. Patients are usually asymptomatic but may experience constipation and lower abdominal pain.


Answer C: Although heartburn is significant with GERD, the additional symptoms presented in the case are inconsistent with this diagnosis. In patients with GERD, expect to get a history of dyspepsia 30-90 minutes following meals, regurgitation, and pain worse in the supine position but improved with standing.


Answer E: Post-obstructive ileus also results in slow transit times and delayed gastric emptying but is most common in the days following surgery.




Bottom Line: Gastroparesis is an autonomic sequella of diabetes that most commonly presents with dyspepsia, vomiting of undigested food early feelings of fullness, and weight loss due to poor nutrient absorption combined with low-calorie intake.

COMBANK Insight : Gastroparesis should be considered in any patient with known diabetes mellitus who presents with abdominal symptoms. In particular, look for feelings of early fullness, vomiting after meals, and weight loss. Complications of diabetes are highly testable and tend to appear, in some form, on most COMLEX exams.

For more information, see:
"Diabetic autonomic neuropathy of the gastrointestinal tract" on UpToDate.

Link: "Diabetic autonomic neuropathy of the gastrointestinal tract" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 64-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of heartburn and early satiety. History reveals intermittent vomiting that tends to occur several hours following a meal, and an 8 lb. weight loss over the last month. Current medications include lisinopril, metformin, and atorvastatin. Physical examination reveals a soft but mildly distended abdomen with tenderness to palpation in the epigastrum.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most appropriate initial step in the management is

1. cimetidine
2. metoclopramide
3. omeprazole
4. ondansetron
5. streptomycin

The correct answer is: B
Metoclopramide (Reglan), is a prokinetic agent that is 1st line in the treatment of diabetic gastroparesis. The drug works by accelerating gastric emptying time, which promotes motility of the GI tract. Metoclopramide is also an effective treatment for GERD, because of its ability to increase lower esophageal sphincter pressure to help reduce reflux of gastric contents.

Answer A: Cimetidine (Tagamet) inhibits histamine at H2 receptors of gastric parietal cells, leading to reduced gastric acid secretion, gastric volume, and concentrations of hydrogen ions. H2 blockers have not been shown to be beneficial for patients suffering from gastroparesis.


Answer C: Placing the patient on a proton pump inhibitor such as omeprazole (Prilosec) would be inappropriate. These agents inhibit gastric acid secretion through H+/K+ ATPase enzymatic inhibition in the gastric parietal cells and are useful in cases of severe esophagitis and in patients who fail to respond to H2 receptor antagonist therapy.


Answer D: Ondansetron (Zofran) is a serotonin 5-HT3 receptor antagonist that is a powerful antiemetic but does not promote bowel motility or increase gastric emptying, two of the most important effects needed to appropriately treat the above patient. Ondansetron works by reducing vagal activity and blocks serotonin receptors in the chemoreceptor trigger zone.


Answer E: A second drug that has also shown to be beneficial in patients suffering from gastroparesis is erythromycin, not streptomycin as suggested by this answer.




Bottom Line: All patients suffering from diabetic gastroparesis should be placed on either metoclopramide or erythromycin to promote gastrointestinal motility and increase gastric emptying time.

COMBANK Insight : Although ondansetron is used as a distractor in the above case, it is one of the most highly tested drugs on COMLEX. You will typically be presented with a case where a patient is receiving chemotherapy and suffers from refractory nausea and vomiting. Ondansetron will be the best antiemetic in this scenario.

For more information, see:
"Diabetic autonomic neuropathy of the gastrointestinal tract" on UpToDate.

Link: "Clinical features and diagnosis of cervical radiculopathy" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 36-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of neck and shoulder pain and a burning sensation over her outer arm. History reveals the symptoms began two months ago and has come and gone for several weeks, but now it is constant. On physical examination, there is weakness with left wrist extension. The most appropriate test to aid in the diagnosis is

1. Lachman’s test
2. McMurray’s test
3. Neer’s test
4. Spurling’s test
5. Thompson test

The correct answer is: D
This patient is suffering from cervical radiculopathy. The most common level involved is C5-C6. Cervical disc herniation is a common cause of impingement on the cervical nerve roots, causing radicular symptoms. When the C5-C6 disc herniates, it impinges the C6 nerve root causing pain in the neck, shoulder, lateral arm and radial forearm. It can cause weakness in the wrist extensors, brachioradialis and a decreased or absent biceps reflex. One test that can be performed as part of the physical exam is the Spurling test. This is done with the patient seated, then passively extending the neck and rotating it towards the side of the suspected radiculopathy and applying compression. If this maneuver recreates the patient’s symptoms, it suggests the cervical nerve roots are the source. Imaging studies and nerve conduction studies study should be performed next.

Answer A: Lachman’s test evaluates the knee for a torn anterior cruciate ligament (ACL). The knee is placed into 30 degrees of flexion. The tibia is then translated anteriorly to asses for laxity and presence of end-point of the ACL.


Answer B: McMurray’s test evaluates the knee for a torn meniscus. The supine patient’s knee is flexed to 90 degrees with a valgus stress applied. Then, the knee is externally rotated and extended. A positive test is indicated by pain or a click.


Answer C: The Neer test is performed by having the patient fully pronate the affected arm, then forcefully flexing it. This causes impingement of the rotator cuff tendons within the subacromial space, causing pain.


Answer E: Thompson’s test evaluates for a ruptured achilles tendon. The patient lies prone on the exam table, with their feet hanging over the end of the table while the physician squeezes the patient’s calf. Squeezing the calf shortens the muscle unit of the gastrocnemius and soleus, placing tension on the Achilles and plantar flexing the foot. A positive test is indicated by the lack of plantarflexion since the tendon is ruptured.




Bottom Line: Cervical radiculopathy most commonly occurs at C5/C6 disc level and affects the C6 nerve root. Patients complain of neck and shoulder pain and may have weakness of the wrist extensors. The Spurling test recreates the symptoms.

For more information, see:
"Clinical features and diagnosis of cervical radiculopathy" on UpToDate.
Miller MD, Thompson SR, Hart JA, et al. Review of Orthopaedics. Philadelphia, PA: Elseiver-Saunders; 2012: 524.

Link: "Molluscum Contagiosum" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics

A worried mother brings her 10-year-old daughter to the outpatient clinic with chief complaint of rash. She returned home from gymnastics camp two days prior. Physical examination reveals multiple flesh-colored, dome-shaped papules around her eyes and inner forearms, as shown in the exhibit. The papules are not pruritic or painful. You remove a small lesion with a curette for examination.


Question 1 of 2 in this set
Histological analysis of this papule will most likely reveal

1. atypical lymphocytes
2. Henderson-Paterson (inclusion) bodies
3. owl's eye inclusion bodies
4. pseudohyphae and budding yeast
5. sarcoptes scabiei mite

The correct answer is: B
Henderson-Paterson bodies are intracytoplasmic inclusion bodies observed with a molluscum contagiosum infection. Molluscum contagiosum is caused by a poxvirus that infects epithelial cells and disrupts the bonds that normally hold them together. The skin lesions are slightly umbilicated, dome-shaped, flesh-colored papules. Patients are usually asymptomatic. It can be transmitted by direct skin contact, e.g., children sharing a bath, daycare centers, or gymnasiums. In adults, it is considered a sexually transmitted disease and papules usually occur in the genital region. Scratching or touching the papules can cause autoinoculation to another body part. Diagnosis is usually by clinical exam only, but if lesions are atypical, removal of a small lesion with a curette and histological examination can confirm. Infected cells will appear dark and round and disperse easily with pressure, unlike normal epithelial cells, which remain stuck together. Lesions are self-limiting, but can last up to 9 months.

Answer A: Atypical lymphocytes are characteristic of infectious mononucleosis caused by Ebstein Barr virus. EBV infection usually begins with pharyngitis, fever, malaise, and fatigue. If treated mistakenly with ampicillin for streptococcal pharyngitis, a prolonged pruritic drug-related maculopapular rash can erupt. The papules described in this clinical vignette are not pruritic and the rash is not maculopapular, making this an unlikely cause.


Answer C: Owl’s eye inclusion bodies are highly specific for cytomegalovirus (CMV) infection. CMV is a herpesvirus that is transmitted via sexual contact, breast milk, or respiratory droplets. Primary infection is usually asymptomatic and reactivation occurs in immunocompromised patients, e.g., transplant and HIV patients. There are a variety of clinical syndromes, including CMV retinitis, colitis, pneumonitis, and encephalitis. It can also cause a seronegative mono-like illness. Treat with ganciclovir.


Answer D: Pseudohyphae and budding yeast are observed on KOH preparation and are indicative of candidiasis. Candida infections usually occur in moist areas such as skin folds, below the breasts, the vagina, and the axillae. Skin infections usually present as pink, circular, erythematous macules with smaller satellite lesions, unlike what is presented in this vignette.


Answer E: Sarcoptes scabiei is a tiny mite that mates on the skin’s surface, then burrows into the stratum corneum and lays eggs. The burrowing leads to the pruritus that is common to Scabies. Erythematous, excoriated papules are observed on the hands, axillae, and genitals. Treat with permethrin.




Bottom Line: Molluscum contagiosum is a poxvirus infection that causes self-limiting flesh-colored, slightly umbilicated, dome-shaped papules. Patients are usually asymptomatic. This virus is transmitted via direct contact with the lesions or fomites. In children, it can be transmitted at daycare or gymnasiums. In adults, it is considered a sexually transmitted disease and occurs in the genital region. If genital lesions are observed in children, child abuse must be suspected. It can be more disfiguring and common in immunocompromised populations. Diagnosis is usually clinical, however, characteristic Henderson-Paterson inclusion bodies can be observed upon histological examination.

For more information, see:
"Molluscum Contagiosum" on Medscape.
Habif, TP. Clinical Dermatology: A color guide to diagnosis and therapy. 5th ed. Mosby Elsevier. 2010, pp 465-466.

Link: "Molluscum Contagiosum" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics

A worried mother brings her 10-year-old daughter to the outpatient clinic with chief complaint of rash. She returned home from gymnastics camp two days prior. Physical examination reveals multiple flesh-colored, dome-shaped papules around her eyes and inner forearms, as shown in the exhibit. The papules are not pruritic or painful. You remove a small lesion with a curette for examination.


Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most appropriate treatment is

1. cryosurgery
2. ganciclovir
3. observation
4. oral fluconazole
5. permethrin

The correct answer is: C
There are varieties of treatments for molluscum contagiosum; however, in children, the most conservative methods must be used. Since this is a self-limiting disease, observation is the best choice of “treatment”. Another treatment for children is curettage if the lesions persist or if there are not too many.

Answer A: This is the treatment of choice in adult patients with a high pain tolerance and is not usually recommended as a treatment for children.


Answer B: Ganciclovir is used to treat CMV infections, not molluscum contagiosum.


Answer D: . Oral fluconazole is used to treat candidiasis and not molluscum contagiosum.


Answer E: Permethrin cream is used to treat scabies, not molluscum contagiosum.




Bottom Line: Treatment for molluscum contagiosum is commonly benign neglect, as the disease is self-limiting. There are varieties of other treatment options. Cryosurgery can be used, but is limited to adults because it is so painful. In addition, curettage and cantharidin are also treatment options and can be used on children.

COMBANK Insight : Pay attention to the age of the patient, it can change treatment course!

Link: "Scarlet Fever" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 12-year-old male presents to the office complaining of a sore throat and swollen tonsils that started suddenly two days ago. Vitals reveal a temperature of 39 ºC (102.2 ºF) blood pressure of 120/70 mmHg, a heart rate of 100/min, and a respiratory rate of 16/min. Physical examination reveals tonsillar exudates and submandibular lymphadenopathy. There is a fine, erythematous patch on the chest, back, and upper extremities as shown in the exhibit. The most likely diagnosis is

1. erysipelas
2. erythema multiforme
3. infectious mononucleosis
4. roseola
5. scarlet fever

The correct answer is: E
This patient has an acute exudative tonsillitis, fever, and submandibular lymphadenopathy, with no mention of cough. This is most consistent with streptococcal pharyngitis. The presence of a rash suggests systemic involvement of the infection and is classic for scarlet fever. The rash is described as sandpapery, or as a fine erythematous punctate eruption that usually is present on the trunk and extremities. Scarlet fever is caused by group A beta-hemolytic streptococci, and the release of erythemogenic/erythrogenic toxin causes the hallmark rash. It is treated with penicillin.

Answer A: Erysipelas is a common bacterial skin infection most commonly due to streptococcus pyogenes. It involves the upper dermis and is described as a raised, sharply demarcated area of erythema with slowly advancing margins that is tender to palpation. It often involves the lymphatics, which causes “streaking”. It is commonly seen on the face and extremities and follows a bacterial pharyngitis or trauma to the area. This patient has a much different skin exam than a patient with erysipelas, although the etiology of the two is the same.


Answer B: Erythema multiforme (EM) is an acute, self-limiting skin condition that is considered to be a type IV hypersensitivity reaction associated with certain infections and/or medications. The classic skin findings are target lesions, that are small, red, purpuric macules or plaques that most commonly occur on the extensor surfaces of extremities and the trunk. EM thought to be included in the spectrum of skin disorders including Stevens-Johnson syndrome and toxic epidermal necrolysis.


Answer C: Infectious mononucleosis (mono) presents as fever, pharyngitis and adenopathy much like the patient in this scenario. However, mono is not typically associated with a fine erythematous rash. Mono patients misdiagnosed with group A streptococcal pharyngitis and treated with penicillin may develop a maculopapular rash, although this is not the case in this scenario. Mono is typically caused by the Epstein-Barr virus (EBV) and treatment is supportive.


Answer D: Roseola is a viral exanthem most commonly seen in children less than 2-years-old. It is caused by human herpesvirus 6 (HHV-6) and classically presents as a maculopapular rash that develops after the fever breaks. The acute illness of roseola includes several days of fever that may be accompanied by upper respiratory or gastrointestinal tract symptoms.




Bottom Line: Acute streptococcal pharyngitis accompanied by a fine, sandpapery, erythematous rash on the trunk or extremities is scarlet fever. It is caused by group A beta-hemolytic streptococcus.

COMBANK Insight : Familiarize yourself with all viral exanthems in childhood. They are tested on all levels of the COMLEX exam.

For more information, see:
"Scarlet Fever" on Medscape.

Link: "Type 2 Diabetes Mellitus" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 55-year-old obese female presents to the office with a known history of diabetes mellitus. History reveals she is finding it more difficult to control her blood sugar despite taking her oral hypoglycemic medicines and watching her diet. She states when she takes her blood sugar at home her lowest measurement is 200 mg/dL and her highest is 300 mg/dL.  Laboratory analysis reveals her HbA1c levels have been steadily increasing over the past three visits from 7.0 to 10.2. You obtain a serum C-peptide and find it to be lower than the normal range. This information indicates

1. the patient has type I diabetes mellitus and needs to begin insulin therapy.
2. the patient is in the “honeymoon” period of type I diabetes mellitus.
3. the patient is a type II diabetic who needs to begin insulin therapy.
4. the patient is a type II diabetic who needs to decrease her oral hypoglycemic therapy.
5. the patient is a type II diabetic who needs to increase her oral hypoglycemic therapy.

The correct answer is: C
The C-peptide in this question is indicative of the amount of insulin being produced by the pancreas. In this case the patient has a low C-peptide which indicates low insulin production by the pancreas. Type II diabetic patients have developed an insulin resistance in the peripheral tissues; thus the pancreas will produce increased amounts of insulin causing the C-peptide to initially be high. Patients with type II diabetes, however, may also develop inadequate insulin production by the pancreas as their disease progresses as the pancreas becomes "burned out" from overproducing insulin in an attempt to compensate for the insulin resistance. Once pancreatic insulin production decreases, patients will present with uncontrolled glucose and HbA1c. The next step in treatment of these patients is to begin insulin therapy. Knowing the pathophysiology of the disease is essential to answering this question correctly.

Answer A: It is very unlikely that this patient has type I diabetes based on her age, body habitus, and drug regimen.


Answer B: Again, it is unlikely that this patient has type I diabetes. The "honeymoon" is the time period in which type I diabetic patient experiencing a “remission” phase that occurs a few days to a week after beginning treatment with insulin. Type I diabetic patients may have a normal C-peptide during their “honeymoon” period but will have a low C-peptide level throughout the rest of their life since deficient insulin production is the pathophysiology of this disease. During this time period, usually lasting a few months, the patient needs less insulin than will eventually be required.


Answer D: In order to better manage their diabetes, a patient with rising HbA1c needs to increase their diabetes medications whether it is the oral hypoglycemics or insulin therapy.


Answer E: Oral hypoglycemic agents work primarily by decreasing the body’s resistance to insulin and/or increasing endogenous release of insulin from the pancreas. Therefore increasing the dose of oral hypoglycemics in the hopes of increasing insulin production will likely be ineffective as the low C-peptide in this case indicate pancreatic "burn-out."




Bottom Line: Difficulty with blood glucose control using oral hypoglycemics in type II diabetics is an indication for the initiation of insulin therapy.

COMBANK Insight : COMLEX Level 2 CE Insight: It is important to have a strong understanding of diabetes mellitus including pathophysiology, genetics, treatments, and screening (eg: diabetic eye exams, urine microalbumin, and monofilament testing). Diabetes mellitus is important to board examiners because of its high prevalence as well as it being a strong risk factor for cardiovascular disease (recall that diabetic patients can have "silent" ischemia or myocardial infarctions!). Know how to differentiate types I and II from each other and the difference of pathophysiology and genetics for each type. Also be aware of different terminology that may show up on the COMLEX. You may see insulin-dependent or non-insulin dependent diabetes instead of types I and II. Keep in mind that non-insulin dependent diabetic patients are "type II", but insulin-dependent diabetic patients may be either "type I" or "type II". If you don't understand the former statement review the answer again for this question. Be ready to answer a battery of questions about diabetes mellitus on the COMLEX.

For more information, see:
"Type 2 Diabetes Mellitus" on Medscape.
"Management of persistent hyperglycemia in
type 2 diabetes mellitus" on UpToDate.
