Link: ML in HIV on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 50-year-old male presents to the office with mental status changes, weakness, and blurry vision. Past medical history reveals a diagnosis of AIDS ten years ago. Lumbar puncture is obtained and CSF analysis is diagnostic for an infection causing progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy. Which of the following is true concerning his infection?

1. he will die of another infection before this one becomes fatal
2. it is caused by an RNA virus
3. the infectious agent is widespread in the population
4. the main cells infected are the microglia
5. the main cells infected are neurons

The correct answer is: C
Progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy (PML) is caused by JC virus. This virus is widespread in the population, with antibodies found in 75% of healthy human sera. Disease, however, is found only in the immunocompromised, such as AIDS patients. The virus infects astrocytes and oligodendrocytes, the myelin-forming cells of the CNS white matter. PML affects only the white matter (thus the word leukoencephalopathy). The virus is very prevalent in the population, but it can be reactivated in patients taking specific immunomodulating drugs. PML is aggressive, and death usually occurs within six months.

Answer A: PML is an aggressive disease that can cause rapid decline and death.


Answer B: The JC virus is a DNA virus. Measles virus is an RNA virus that can lead to long-term neurological symptoms (subacute sclerosing panencephalitis).


Answer D: The JC virus affects oligodendrocytes. Microglia are the resident macrophages of the CNS, which search for infection, damaged neurons, and other aggregates.


Answer E: PML does not affect neurons themselves, but the oligodendrocytes that myelinate CNS neurons.




Bottom Line: The JC virus is widespread, but only causes fatal disease (PML) in immunocompromised patients, most commonly patients infected with AIDS.

For more information, see:
ML in HIV on Medscape.

Link: "Imaging of Benign Breast Calcifications" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Surgery
A 40-year-old female presents to the office with the concern of a breast mass she found on self breast examination. History reveals ten years ago she had a bilateral reduction mammaplasty for macromastia. She has no significant past medical history and no further surgical history. She takes no medication and smokes 1 pack of cigarettes weekly. There is no family history of cancer. Breast examination reveals a slightly retracted nipple and a fixed mass in the upper outer quadrant of the right breast. Mammogram shows a 3 x 3.5 cm spiculated lesion with coarse calcifications. Ultrasound shows a hypoechoic mass. Multiple core biopsy samples demonstrate foamy macrophages and fat globules. What is the most appropriate next step in the management of this patient?

1. lumpectomy with sentinel node dissection
2. MRI
3. radiation therapy of the right breast
4. routine follow-up without intervention
5. simple mastectomy

The correct answer is: D
The above findings are consistent with fat necrosis of the breast, an uncommon condition associated with breast trauma or surgery. Fat necrosis can mimic breast cancer in its clinical and radiographic examination because it often presents as a fixed, spiculated mass with nipple retraction and has evidence of calcification on mammogram and appears solid on ultrasound. The calcifications seen in this patient were described as “coarse,” which is more characteristic of benign lesions. Calcifications associated with breast malignancy tend to be “microcalcifications.” Fine-needle, core, or excisional biopsy is diagnostic and shows foamy macrophages and fat globules. Fat necrosis is not a premalignant lesion and most commonly resolves with time. The next best step is routine follow-up without intervention.

Answers A & C & E: Surgical procedures would be indicated if the biopsy revealed breast carcinoma. There is no indication for surgery in treatment of a benign condition such as fat necrosis.


Answer B: An MRI would not be useful in workup of fat necrosis. Remember that calcium does not magnetize; therefore, MRI is never appropriate in evaluation of calcifications found on mammogram.




Bottom Line: Breast fat necrosis of the breast can mimic malignancy. Biopsy is the best diagnostic tool and will reveal foamy macrophages and fat.

For more information, see:
"Imaging of Benign Breast Calcifications" on Medscape.
"Overview of benign breast disease" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 63-year-old male presents to the office worried about a tremor in his right hand. His medical history is significant for hypertension, high cholesterol and hypothyroidism. On review of systems he admits to having intermittent nausea and feeling depressed. He says that the tremor started about one year ago. Initially it would occur infrequently and when he was at rest, but has worsened over the last few months to now where he it occurs almost constantly when he is sitting down. It has made it difficult to read the newspaper. On exam he does have a slow tremor in his right hand. He also seems to have less emotion in his face and decreased arm swing in his right arm when walking. Which of the following medications should be avoided in this patient?

1. amitriptyline
2. metoclopramide
3. ondansetron
4. promethazine
5. sertraline

The correct answer is: B
The patient has parkinsonism; there is not enough information from this question stem to formally diagnose him with idiopathic Parkinson’s Disease (PD), but he has some hallmarks that can lead us down that path. He has risk factors for PD- age, gender and his symptoms are consistent with a typical initial presentation of PD- unilateral, slowly progressive tremor, decreased arm swing, flat facies. The hallmark pathologic finding in PD is depletion of dopamine from the substantia nigra. Any medications that are dopamine antagonists should be avoided. Metoclopramide is a commonly used anti-emetic medication. Its mechanism of action is the dopamine D2 receptor. Any medications that block dopamine should be avoided in PD.

Answer A: Amitriptyline is a tricyclic antidepressant. It has a wide range of actions, the most prominent is inhibiting re-uptake of serotonin and norepinephrine. It also has action at serotonin, histamine and adrenergic receptors. It has a wide range of uses including anti-depressant, chronic pain, migraine prevention and eating disorders to name a few. It does not have any dopaminergic effects and could be used in PD.


Answer C: Ondansetron is a serotonin 5-HT3 blocking medication. It is used for nausea and vomiting. It does not have any effect on dopamine and can be safely used in PD.


Answer D: Promethazine is a medication used for nausea and vomiting. It is an anti-histamine H1 blocking medication. It also has some anti-cholinergic effects. It does have some very weak affinity for D2 receptors and a side effect is dystonia. It should probably not be a first line anti-emetic in this patient, and should be used with caution.


Answer E: Sertraline is a selective serotonin re-uptake inhibitor (SSRI). It is used to treat depression. Depression is exceedingly common in patients with PD and should be asked about during every visit. SSRIs are first line therapy and should be offered to the appropriate patients. There is a theoretical interaction with monoamine oxidase inhibitors (MAOIs) as both increase the amount of serotonin and can lead to serotonin syndrome. Selegiline and rasagiline are both MAOIs with affinity to the B receptor, and are frequently used in treatment of PD. This interaction is theoretical and the combination of an SSRI with an MAOI-B (especially at low doses) is common.




Bottom Line: Hallmark symptoms of Parkinson's disease include; unilateral, slowly progressive tremor, decreased arm swing, flat facies. Any medications that block dopamine should be avoided in PD.

For more information, see:
Le. T, Bhushan V, Hofmann J, et al. First Aide for the USMLE Step 2 2012: A Student-to-Student Guide. New York, NY: McGraw-Hill; 2012

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 25-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of lower back pain. History reveals she has been a gymnast since the age of four. Physical examination reveals tight hamstrings bilaterally, bilateral lumbar paraspinal hypertonicity, and a straight leg raise test that reproduces her lower back pain bilaterally. Plain radiographs of the lumbar spine in the oblique view demonstrate a fracture of the pars interarticularis of L4 with the lumbar spine maintaining appropriate alignment. The most likely diagnosis is

1. psoas syndrome
2. spinal stenosis
3. spondylolisthesis
4. spondylolysis
5. spondylosis

The correct answer is: D
Spondylolysis occurs when there is a defect in the pars interarticularis without displacement of the affected vertebra on top of the adjacent inferior vertebra.  The pars interarticularis is the part of the vertebra that is located between the superior and inferior articular processes of the zygapophyseal joint.  In order to obtain an adequate view of the pars interarticularis, plain radiographs must be taken in the oblique view.  In the oblique view, a defect in the pars interarticularis may appear as a collar on the "Scottie dog."  This is because the posterior elements form a silhouette of a Scottie dog when viewed obliquely.  The transverse process represents the nose, the pedicle represents the eye, the superior articular facet represents the ear, the inferior articular facet represents the front leg, and the pars interarticularis represents the dog's neck.  This defect is relatively common, appearing in up to 5% of the general population.  Its prevalence is higher in patients who do repetitive back extension, such as gymnasts, as this increases the likelihood of fatigue fractures in the pars interarticularis.  The most common vertebrae involved L4 or L5.  Patients often present with the complaint of lower back, buttock, and/or posterior thigh pain that is described as an ache.  The pain is typically increased with extension-based activities, and there are usually no neurologic deficits.  Physical examination will likely reveal tight hamstrings bilaterally, and a stiff-legged, short stride, waddling gait.  Treatment typically consists of activity modification, physical therapy, and analgesics as necessary.

Answer A: Psoas syndrome is a clinical syndrome caused by a flexion contracture of the iliopsoas muscle complex. It often results from prolonged positions that shorten the iliopsoas complex, typically in those patients who have desk jobs. There are other possible organic causes, however, that may result in spasm of the iliopsoas complex through viscero-somatic or somato-somatic reflexes. These organic causes include salpingitis, ureteral calculi, ureter dysfunction, metastatic prostate carcinoma, appendicitis, or sigmoid colon dysfunction. These organic causes must be ruled out before beginning therapy for mechanical causes. Psoas syndrome typically presents as an ache or muscle spasm in the lower back that may sometimes radiate to the groin. The pain is typically increased when standing or walking. Physical examination will demonstrate a tender point medial to the ASIS, a positive Thomas test, a positive pelvic shift test to the contralateral side, non-neutral dysfunction of L1 or L2, sacral dysfunction on an oblique axis, and contralateral piriformis spasm. Acute spasm is typically treated with ice and indirect osteopathic manipulation techniques. Because stretching may cause further spasm in the acute phase, stretching should be limited to more chronic psoas spasms.


Answer B: Spinal stenosis is the result of degenerative changes causing narrowing of the spinal canal or intervertebral foramina causing pressure on nerve roots, or the spinal cord itself. These degenerative changes may include loss of intervertebral disc height, hypertrophy of the zygapophyseal joints, or calcium deposits within the ligamentum flavum and the posterior longitudinal ligament. Patients typically complain of lower back pain that may radiate to the legs. The pain is typically described as an aching lower back with shooting pain or paresthesias going down one or both legs. The symptoms are usually worsened with extension, when standing, walking, or lying supine. Treatment initially consists of activity modification, physical therapy, analgesics as necessary, and may progress to requiring epidural steroid injections, or surgical laminectomy with decompression.


Answer C: Spondylolisthesis presents very similarly to spondylolysis, however plain radiography in the lateral view will demonstrate anterior displacement of one vertebra in relation to the adjacent inferior vertebra. Signs, symptoms, physical examination, and therapies are very similar to those of spondylolysis. An additional physical examination finding that may be seen in spondylolisthesis, but is not seen and spondylolysis, is a positive vertebral step off sign, which is noted when palpating the spinous processes, and there is an obvious forward displacement of one vertebra in relation to the inferior vertebra.


Answer E: Spondylosis is simply a radiologic term for ankylosing of adjacent vertebral bodies and degenerative changes within the injured vertebral disc.




Bottom Line: Spondylolysis is the term used to describe a defect in the pars interarticularis without anterior displacement of the affected vertebra in relation to the adjacent inferior vertebra.

COMBANK Insight : Spondylosis, spondylolysis, and spondylolisthesis are very similar terms that are easily tested on the COMLEX exam.  Be sure to know the difference between these definitions.

Last Updated: 9/25/2016
135 of 200

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Services Rendered
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Surgery
A 54-year-old male presents to the pre-operative holding area for removal of a pheochromocytoma. Past medical history reveals hyperlipidemia, hypertension and a deep vein thrombosis two months ago, for which the patient takes lisinopril, lovastatin and enoxaparin. The patient has not taken any medications in the last 24 hours. The patient should receive which of the following before surgery to prevent hypertensive crisis?

1. fresh frozen plasma
2. heparin
3. lisinopril
4. metoprolol
5. phenoxybenzamine

The correct answer is: E
The patient in the above scenario needs to be given an alpha-blocker, such as phenoxybenzamine, before his surgery is started. The stress of the operation and manipulation of the tumor can cause a massive release of catecholamines and lead to a hypertensive crisis if not treated beforehand. Alpha-blockers prevent norepinephrine from binding to vascular smooth muscle, causing vasodilation.

Answer A: The patient in the above scenario does not need fresh frozen plasma. Low molecular weight heparins, such as enoxaparin, have a short half-life and unlike warfarin, only need to be stopped a day prior to surgery.


Answer B: This patient does not need presurgical anticoagulation. His enoxaparin should be restarted after surgery.


Answer C: Lisinopril will not help in preventing a hypertensive crisis from a pheochromocytoma, as it does not block alpha-receptors.


Answer D: Metoprolol is a beta-blocker that can be used in addition to an alpha-blocker during surgery for a pheochromocytoma. However, due to beta-2 mediated vasodilation it should not be given before an alpha-blocker because it can worsen a hypertensive crisis




Bottom Line: Patients with pheochromocytomas need to receive alpha-blockers before surgery to remove the tumor.

Link: "Ischemic Stroke in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 49-year-old male presents to the emergency department by ambulance. Paramedics report that approximately thirty minutes prior he developed slurred speech, right facial droop, and weakness to his right side. He cannot articulate his complaints, but can appropriately answer “yes/no” questions with a head nod. His past medical history reveals obesity, uncontrolled hypertension, hyperlipidemia, and type-2 diabetes. Vitals reveal a blood pressure of 180/100 mmHg, a heart rate of 75/min, a respiratory rate of 16/min, an oxygen saturation of 99% on room air, and a temperature of 37 °C (98 °F). Physical examination reveals right extremity weakness and an asymmetric face when asked to smile. The most important initial step in management is

1. give a trial of steroids and acyclovir
2. immediately give tissue plasminogen activator (t-PA)
3. immediately lower blood pressure
4. obtain a non-contrast enhanced brain CT
5. obtain CT angiography of the brain

The correct answer is: D
This patient has signs and symptoms of an acute stroke given the acute onset of symptoms and focal neurologic findings on examination. In addition, he is high risk due to his history of obesity, diabetes, hypertension, and hyperlipidemia. He is presenting within 3 hours of symptom onset, and therefore should be evaluated emergently so that he may receive thrombolytics if he qualifies and has no contraindications. This patient's presentation is consistent with a cerebrovascular accident (CVA) that involves the left middle cerebral artery (MCA) territory. This type of CVA will commonly cause an expressive aphasia due to ischemia to Broca’s area. In addition, right-sided weakness is indicative of a left MCA territory infarct. The most important initial study to obtain is a non-contrast enhanced brain CT. The importance of CT in acute stroke is not to detect the presence of visible infarcted brain tissue, but to rule out a subarachnoid hemorrhage or hemorrhagic infarct, so that thrombolytics can be given with least amount of risk of bleeding complications. In fact, the overall sensitivity of CT for ischemic stroke in the emergency department is around 16%, making it a poor test to detect ischemic stroke.

Answer A: Bell’s palsy is a peripheral nerve palsy due to inflammation on or around cranial nerve VII (facial nerve). This nerve exits the stylomastoid foramen as it exits the skull and supplies motor function to the facial muscles and taste to the anterior two-thirds of the tongue. Importantly, a patient with Bell’s palsy will have complete hemiparesis of their face (upper and lower portions), whereas in acute MCA territory infarct, the paralysis will only be to the lower portion of the contralateral face. This question stem does not specifically indicate which portion of the face is paralyzed, however Bell’s palsy will never cause speech disturbances or other extremity weaknesses because it is limited to one lower motor neuron. Bell’s palsy is thought to be due to herpes simplex 1 (HSV-1), and is usually treated with steroids and acyclovir, but is not the most likely diagnosis in this case.


Answer B: Tissue plasminogen activator (t-PA) is indicated in acute ischemic infarct within 3 hours of symptom onset in patient's 18-years of age or older. Contraindications to t-PA are CT evidence of intracranial hemorrhage or hypodensity in over 1/3 of the MCA territory, systolic BP > 185 or diastolic > 110 Hg despite aggressive management, headache or stiff neck suggestive of subarachnoid hemorrhage, intracranial tumor, arteriovenous malformation or aneurysm, or any evidence of active GI bleeding. This patient has not yet received a brain CT to evaluate for intracranial bleeding or other pathology as listed above. Therefore, administration of t-PA as an initial management strategy is incorrect and could cause disastrous results if there is intracranial bleeding, aneurysm, tumor or arteriovenous malformation.


Answer C: There is a lack of evidence to whether lowering blood pressure in acute CVA is beneficial. In fact, some data may suggest that lowering blood pressure may be harmful because of the decrease in cerebral perfusion pressure (defined as mean arterial pressure minus intracranial pressure). At this point, the blood pressure may be due to stress/anxiety/pain and should be addressed at some point. It should not interfere with obtaining a STAT brain CT.


Answer E: CT angiography (CTA) is commonly used in the workup of CVA after an initial non-contrast brain CT is obtained. At most centers, the order of operation for suspected ischemic stroke amenable to tissue plasminogen activator (t-PA) is as follows: 1) non-contrast brain CT, 2) CTA, 3) t-PA, 4) magnetic resonance imaging (MRI). If there is any sign of bleeding on the initial brain CT, there is an absolute contraindication for t-PA administration. CTA is used following the non-contrast CT to evaluate for focal vessel obstruction that may be amenable to coiling or direct arterial thrombolysis by interventional radiology/neurosurgery. This patient should undergo non-contrast enhanced brain CT as a first step in management.




Bottom Line: Signs and symptoms of acute stroke include the acute onset of any focal neurologic findings. In general, 80% of strokes will be ischemic and 20% hemorrhagic. Maintain a high level of suspicion and get a non-contrast enhanced brain CT immediately to evaluate for hemorrhagic infarct or subarachnoid hemorrhage before giving t-PA.

For more information, see:
"Ischemic Stroke in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.

Link: "Neural Tube Defects" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 25-year-old G2P1 female at 18 weeks' gestation presents to the office for review of recent laboratory analysis. The results revealed she has an elevated maternal serum alpha-fetoprotein. An ultrasound is obtained and reveals the appropriate aging of the fetus. She admits to taking her prenatal vitamin for a couple of days then stopping due to nausea. Which of the following is the fetus at the greatest risk for?

1. defect in the L4 pars interarticularis
2. sacrococcygeal teratoma
3. defect in the L4 lamina
4. trisomy 21
5. trisomy 18

The correct answer is: C
Maternal serum alpha-fetoprotein is measured in the second trimester as part of the triple or quad screening. An elevated alpha-fetoprotein level without other laboratory abnormalities is commonly due to inaccurate dating. In the above scenario an ultrasound has been obtained to rule this out. Elevated alpha-fetoprotein may also be elevated due to neural tube defects. Spina bifida is a common neural tube defect characterized by failure of the lamina of a vertebral segment to close. These usually occur in the lumbar spine. This can be benign (occulta) or cause serious neurological damage if the spinal cord herniates.

Answer A: A defect in the pars interarticularis of the lumbar spine is associated with spondylolysis.


Answer B: A sacrococcygeal teratoma is a germ cell tumor common in neonates but does not fit with the findings in this case.


Answer D: Trisomy 21 leads to Down’s syndrome which may present with a decreased alpha fetoprotein during pregnancy.


Answer E: Alpha fetoprotein is decreased in trisomy 18.




Bottom Line: Lack of folic acid during pregnancy and elevated alpha fetoprotein are associated with neural tube defects. An example is spina bifida characterized by failure of the vertebral lamina to close.

COMBANK Insight : The current recommendations require folic acid supplementation of 400mcg/day = 0.4mg/day. Women who have had a history of pregnancies complicated by neural tube defects should be supplemented with 4mg/day 1 month prior to conception and through the first trimester.

For more information, see:
"Neural Tube Defects" on Medscape

Patient Presentation: Bleeding
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 30-year-old female presents to the emergency department with a one week history of abdominal pain, vomiting, and bloody diarrhea. History reveals the abdominal pain has been gradually worsening over the past week, and is currently a 10/10 in intensity. She has also noted bright red blood in her stool for 5 days' duration. Her nausea and vomiting began late last night. Past medical history is significant for ulcerative colitis and medications include sulfasalazine and an over-the-counter multivitamin. She smokes a pack of cigarettes a day and drinks alcohol socially. She recently returned from a trip to Indonesia. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 98/54 mmHg, a heart rate of 110/min, a temperature of 39.1°C (102.4°F), and a respiratory rate of 18/min. Physical examination reveals her abdomen is exquisitely tender in all 4-quadrants and there is guarding and rebound tenderness. An abdominal radiograph is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most likely diagnosis is

1. acute ulcerative colitis flare
2. enteroinvasive Escherichia coli infection
3. small bowel obstruction
4. toxic megacolon
5. traveler's diarrhea

The correct answer is: D
This patient's clinical presentation, history of ulcerative colitis, and radiograph with dilated large bowel are consistent with toxic megacolon. Abdominal pain suggests a possible complication of this patient's ulcerative colitis, such as perforation or toxic megacolon. Patients usually present with a 1-week history of bloody diarrhea symptoms that are not responsive to medication. On exam, patients are often tachycardic, febrile, and hypotensive. Abdominal exam will usually reveal abdominal tenderness and distention and peritoneal signs are often present. Bowel sounds will be decreased or absent. On plain film, such as shown above, the transverse colon is very dilated, usually greater than 6 centimeters. Toxic megacolon is a very serious complication and mortality rate is around 40%. Approximately 50% of patients may improve with medical management, such as bowel rest, intravenous steroids, antibiotics, and fluids. However, if clinical status continues to deteriorate, immediate surgery with total colectomy is often indicated.

Answer A: Acute flares of ulcerative colitis usually consist of bloody diarrhea with rectal urgency, discomfort, and cramps. Fever can be present, but is infrequent. Abdomen may be tender in the lower quadrants, but severe abdominal pain with peritoneal signs and hemodynamic instability are suggestive of an acute complication, such as toxic megacolon--- which is confirmed by radiograph in the above patient.


Answer B: Invasive Escherichia coli can also cause bloody diarrhea, but fever, hypotension, peritoneal signs, and dilated colon on radiograph would be highly unusual.


Answer C: Small bowel obstruction presents with constipation, abdominal distention, and abdominal pain. It does not occur in the setting of diarrhea. Air-fluid levels and dilated loops of small bowel will usually be seen on radiography.


Answer E: While this patient's history of recent travel would be concerning for traveler's diarrhea, her clinical presentation and exam does not support this. Traveler’s diarrhea is caused by enterotoxic E. coli, which causes a watery, non-bloody diarrhea.




Bottom Line: Toxic megacolon is a known complication of ulcerative colitis and Clostridium difficile infection that presents with bloody diarrhea, severe abdominal pain, and acute sepsis. On plain radiograph, a very dilated (>6 cm) transverse colon is often seen.

For more information, see:
"Toxic Megacolon" on Medscape.

Link: "Azotemia" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 30-year-old female patient presents to the emergency department unconscious after a car accident. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 75/50 mmHg, a heart rate of 120/min, respiratory rate of 12/min, and a temperature of 37ºC. Physical examination shows delayed capillary refill, generalized skin pallor, and a distended abdomen. Laboratory tests and abdominal radiographs are pending. Fluid administration with 2 large-bore intravenous lines is initiated and a type and crossmatch for possible blood transfusions is obtained. The most likely laboratory findings in this patient are

1. FeNa >4%, BUN:Cr>20:1, urine granular casts, serum creatinine increased
2. FeNa >1%, BUN:Cr<20:1, urine granular casts, serum creatinine increased
3. FeNa <1%, BUN:Cr>20:1, no urine granular casts, serum creatinine normal
4. FeNa >4%, BUN:Cr<20:1, granular casts, serum creatinine normal
5. FeNa >4%, BUN:Cr<20:1, no granular casts, serum creatinine increased

The correct answer is: C
This patient is most likely severely intravascularly depleted and as a result, blood to the kidneys will be significantly decreased.  In prerenal azotemia, the hallmark findings are BUN:Cr>20:1, normal to slightly elevated creatinine levels, no urine casts, and a fractional excretion of sodium (FeNa) <1%.  If the patient continues to be intravascularly depleted, she can progress to intrinsic renal failure where BUN:Cr1%. Management consists of fluids and blood products.

Answers A & D: These findings are not specific for prerenal azotemia.


Answer B: These findings point towards intrinsic renal failure such as acute interstitial nephritis, glomerulonephritis, or acute tubular necrosis.


Answer E: These findings are consistent with postobstructive renal failure which can be caused by benign prostatic hypertrophy, pelvic malignancies, or trauma.




Bottom Line: When BUN:Cr>20:1 and FeNa<1%, always consider prerenal azotemia as a cause of elevated creatinine, potassium, and phosphorus.  Etiologies include intravascular depletion from blood loss, blunt trauma, sepsis, or excess diuretic or NSAID use.

COMBANK Insight : This is a high yield topic because prerenal azotemia is a very common condition that can lead to the deadly possibility of intrinsic renal failure.

For more information, see:
The Massachusetts General Hospital Handbook of Internal Medicine. 4th Ed. Lippincott Williams & Wilkins: Philadelphia, PA 2011, pp. 4-12 - 4-13.
"Azotemia" on Medscape.

Link: "Hyperprolactinemia" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Discharge
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Psychiatry
Discipline: Psychiatry
A 45-year-old male presents with a milky, white discharge from his nipples. He states that his discharge has been present ever since being placed on a new medication by his psychiatrist. The patient is unable to recall the name of the pill he is taking. Based on this information, you suspect the patient was most likely started on:

1. Fluoxetine
2. Haloperidol
3. Lithium
4. Nortriptyline
5. Phenelzine

The correct answer is: B
Haloperidol is characterized as a typical antipsychotic and is commonly used in the treatment of schizophrenia. One of its notable side effects is hyperprolactinemia, which occurs due to its dopamine-inhibiting effects allowing for the oversecretion of prolactin. Increased prolactin levels may cause abnormal breast swelling and lactation. Normally, dopamine blocks the release of prolactin from the anterior pituitary. When dopamine release is blocked by drugs such as haloperidol, the blockade on prolactin release is lost, leading to increased prolactin levels.

Answer A: Fluoxetine is a selective serotonin re-uptake inhibitor. It blocks the re-uptake of serotonin in the presynaptic cleft. It is used as the first-line treatment for depression and requires approximately 4-8 weeks of administration before seeing its' effect. These drugs have been shown to be helpful for bulimia and anxiety. Adverse reactions include sexual dysfunction (secondary to serotonin levels) and increased risk of suicidal ideation in adolescents. They can cause serotonin syndrome, which can be treated with cyproheptadine.


Answer C: Lithium is a mood stabilizer commonly used to treat bipolar disorder may adversely lead to excessive thirst, polyuria, hypothyroidism, weight gain, and tremor. Lithium use in pregnancy has been associated with Ebstein's anomaly in the fetus.


Answer D: Nortriptyline is a tricyclic antidepressant that blocks the re-uptake of norepinephrine and serotonin. It is used for second-line treatment for depression. It is fatal at only 5 times the therapeutic dose due to cardiac QT prolongation. Its main side effects are weight gain, sexual dysfunction, and anticholinergic symptoms.


Answer E: Phenelzine is a monoamine oxidase inhibitor. It inhibits the deamination of serotonin, norepinephrine, and dopamine. It is used as a second line treatment for depression. Adverse effects include dizziness and hypertensive crisis (secondary to consumption of foods containing tyramine).




Bottom Line: Hyperprolactinemia is a known side effect of haloperidol, which occurs due to its dopamine-inhibiting effects thus causing a oversecretion of prolactin.

COMBANK Insight : Basic physiology is not commonly tested unless it is associated with pathophysiology or a side effect of a medication. The HPA axis however is still commonly tested.

For more information, see:
"Hyperprolactinemia" on Medscape.

Link: "Small-Bowel Obstruction" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 55-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaints of abdominal pain and vomiting. Further history reveals that she began having diffuse abdominal pain two days ago and has not been able to eat or drink anything since that time. She reports bilious, non-bloody emesis up to five times per day. She has a past medical history of alcoholism and endometriosis. Past surgical history is pertinent for a femoral hernia repair and laparoscopic endometrial ablation, both several years ago. Vitals reveal a blood pressure of 160/100 mmHg, a heart rate of 90/min, a respiratory rate of 24/min, and a temperature of 37ºC (98.6ºF). Physical examination reveals an uncomfortable but non-toxic appearing woman. Her abdomen is distended, resonant to percussion, and diffusely tender to palpation. An abdominal radiograph is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most appropriate intervention is

1. attempt manual disimpaction of colon
2. consult gastroenterology
3. give stimulant laxative to facilitate peristalsis
4. obtain stool sample and send for clostridium difficile toxin
5. place nasogastric tube

The correct answer is: E
This patient has a history of two prior abdominal surgeries which places her at increased risk of small bowel obstruction (SBO). This patient’s physical exam is notable for a tender, tympanitic abdomen and she has an abdominal radiograph showing air-fluid levels that is consistent with SBO. SBOs can be partial or complete depending on the degree of obstruction. They are also described as simple or strangulated depending on evidence of inadequate bowel perfusion. Strangulated obstructions are surgical emergencies due to potential vascular compromise that can lead to bowel ischemia/necrosis. The most common causes of SBO is prior abdominal surgery (adhesions), hernias, inflammatory bowel disease, and malignancy. If the abdominal radiograph fails to adequately show a small bowel obstruction, a CT of the abdomen and pelvis is indicated to further elucidate the cause of and location of the obstruction. In addition to providing intravenous fluids and anti-emetics, the most appropriate initial treatment is decompression of the gastrointestinal tract with a nasogastric tube (NGT). Often a conservative, nonoperative approach such as NGT placement is tried for several days assuming the patient is hemodynamically normal and improving with this therapy.

Answer A: Severe constipation is a common cause of abdominal pain and can be treated by manual disimpaction. However, this patient does not report a history of constipation nor has an abdominal radiograph showing colonic distention consistent with constipation. This radiograph shows air-fluid levels in the small bowel consistent with SBO. Manual disimpaction would not provide this patient any benefit.


Answer B: SBO is generally managed by internal medicine and general surgery. Rarely does gastroenterology become involved at all in these cases because gastroenterology can do little to affect the patient’s outcome in the acute setting. The patient will either improve with NGT therapy (nonoperative) or will need surgery to correct the problem. General surgery should be consulted early if there are signs of bowel strangulation and tissue ischemia such as tachycardia, fever, or signs of peritonitis.


Answer C: Stimulant laxatives are contraindicated in SBO or in any setting where mechanical obstruction is in the differential. Giving a patient a stimulant laxative will cause increased peristalsis of the bowel and cause increased pressure on the point of obstruction. This can cause worsening of the SBO, propagate strangulation/tissue ischemia and can even cause intestinal perforation.


Answer D: It is unlikely that this patient is suffering from clostridium difficile colitis by history and physical exam. Patient’s with this condition have voluminous, foul-smelling diarrhea and have abdominal radiographs showing distention of the colon. Often they have recently been prescribed antibiotics. A potentially life threatening complication of clostridium difficile colitis is toxic megacolon. Toxic megacolon is described as a syndrome of non-obstructive colonic dilatation larger than 6 cm and signs of systemic toxicity. The radiograph in this scenario shows dilated loops of small-bowel and air-fluid levels that is consistent with SBO, not colitis.




Bottom Line: In small-bowel obstruction, initiate care by decompressing the bowel with the placement of a nasogastric tube. Recognize SBO on abdominal radiography by the presence of air-fluid levels.

For more information, see:
"Small-Bowel Obstruction" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Services Rendered
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Surgery
A 34-year-old female is undergoing a minor surgical procedure and is under general anesthesia. The patient is inadvertently given 40,000 units of heparin before realizing the single-dose vials of the medication which are 1000 units each were replaced with multi-dose vials that are 10,000 units each. Which of the following should be administered immediately?

1. aminocaproic acid
2. fresh frozen plasma
3. protamine sulfate
4. sodium bicarbonate
5. vitamin K

The correct answer is: C
Protamine sulfate is used to treat heparin toxicity. It is a highly basic peptide that combines in an ion pair with heparin (which is acidic), and neutralizes the anticoagulation effects of heparin. Heparin toxicity is followed by measuring aPTT levels.

Answer A: Aminocaproic acid is used to treat toxicity from tissue plasminogen activator (tPA) and streptokinase. TPA toxicity is followed by measuring aPTT and PT levels.


Answer B: Fresh frozen plasma is used to treat warfarin toxicity with active bleeding.


Answer D: Sodium bicarbonate is used to treat aspirin toxicity. Aspirin toxicity is followed by measuring serum salicylate levels.


Answer E: Vitamin K is used to treat warfarin toxicity with no active bleeding (e.g. melena). Warfarin toxicity is followed by measuring PT/INR levels.




Bottom Line: Protamine sulfate is used for heparin overdose.

COMBANK Insight : Management of anti-coagulant toxicity and overdose is often seen on the COMLEX. It is worth noting that the management of warfarin toxicity depends on the presence or absence of bleeding.

For more information, see:
"Therapeutic use of heparin and low molecular weight heparin" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Consciousness Alterations
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 64-year-old female presents to the emergency department after becoming comatose at her home. History reveals that she had been taking medicine for headaches, and she had complained of dizziness and tinnitus prior to losing consciousness.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The patient's symptoms are most likely due to toxicity of

1. acetaminophen
2. amitriptyline
3. ibuprofen
4. naproxen
5. salicylates

The correct answer is: E
This constellation of symptoms is most consistent with salicylate toxicity, which typically begins with dizziness and tinnitus and ultimately leads to loss of consciousness and death. It can be treated with dialysis and/or sodium bicarbonate.

Answer A: Phase 1 of acetaminophen toxicity would reveal anorexia, nausea and vomiting, malaise, and a subclinical rise in serum transaminases that levels begins 12 hours following ingestion.


Answer B: Amitriptyline is classified as a tricyclic antidepressant. Amitriptyline toxicity typically presents with anticholinergic effects, dry mouth, loss of appetite, drowsiness, muscle stiffness, nausea, constipation, nervousness, dizziness, blurred vision and insomnia. Although tinnitus can also be present with TCAs, it is a rare finding.


Answers C & D: The effects of excessive NSAIDs would include nausea, dyspepsia, gastrointestinal ulceration/bleeding, elevated liver enzymes, diarrhea, epistaxis, headache and dizziness. These effects would also be seen with naproxen since it is also classified as an NSAID.




Bottom Line: Salicylate toxicity initially manifests with dizziness and tinnitus, but ultimately leads to loss of consciousness and death.

COMBANK Insight : Diagnosis and management of salicylate and acetaminophen toxicity are routinely tested.  It has appeared on many recent examinations. They are both commonly consumed in excessive amounts because of their accessibility.

For more information, see:
"Salicylate (aspirin) poisoning in adults" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Consciousness Alterations
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 64-year-old female presents to the emergency department after becoming comatose at her home. History reveals that she had been taking medicine for headaches, and she had complained of dizziness and tinnitus prior to losing consciousness.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The best test to support this diagnosis is a/an

1. arterial blood gas
2. complete metabolic panel
3. CT of the brain with contrast
4. CT of the brain without contrast
5. electroencephalogram

The correct answer is: A
In the above scenario, the presence of a mixed respiratory alkalosis and metabolic acidosis would support the diagnosis of salicylate (aspirin) toxicity. Patients who present to the hospital with loss of consciousness should always receive an arterial blood gas analysis to assess oxygenation and ventilation.

Answer B: A complete metabolic panel is also warranted but would provide little support for the diagnosis of salicylate toxicity.


Answer C: There would be no reason to order a CT of the brain with contrast in this patient.


Answer D: A non-contrast CT of the brain would be used to rule out intracranial hemorrhage and should be ordered only after a blood gas is performed.


Answer E: An electroencephalogram would be of little use in the initial work-up and should be considered if the patient remains comatose or if brain function needs to be assessed.




Bottom Line: Arterial blood gas analysis should be performed in all patients suspected of having salicylate toxicity. A mixed respiratory alkalosis and metabolic acidosis would support the diagnosis.

Link: "Amaurosis fugax (transient monocular or binocular visual loss)" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Family Medicine
An 83-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of temporary loss of vision in her left eye. History reveals the patient had 5 minutes of visual loss and describes it as if a “shade” was pulled over her left eye. Past medical history reveals hypertension and diabetes. Neurologic and ophthalmologic examination are normal.
Question 1 of 3 in this set
Stenosis of which of the following arteries is likely to be responsible for these findings?

1. Anterior cerebral
2. Carotid
3. Lenticulostriate
4. Middle cerebral
5. Vertebrobasilar

The correct answer is: B
The patient in the above case is experiencing transient monocular blindness, also known as Amaurosis fugax. This condition is a short-lived episode of blindness in one eye. Individuals often describe the event as if “a shade or curtain was pulled over my eye.” The event itself is short-lived in most cases but may be representative of a more severe cerebrovascular event. Blindness is often caused by an embolus originating in the carotid artery, which ultimately occludes the ophthalmic artery, causing monocular blindness.

Answer A: If the anterior cerebral artery is involved, patients may experience gait abnormality or urinary incontinence due to frontal lobe involvement.


Answer C: Lenticulostriate arteries are small vessels that branch off the middle cerebrals and feed the basal ganglia; occlusion of these arteries would not cause these symptoms.


Answer D: Strokes involving the middle cerebral artery often present with hemiplegia and can cause homonymous hemianopsia.


Answer E: Symptoms secondary to vertebrobasilar emboli cause vertigo and CN palsies.




Bottom Line: Amaurosis fugax refers to transient monocular blindness involving carotid emboli with occlusion of the ophthalmic artery.
